OURS

A Novel by Roger Grubb

ACT ONE: THE IDEA
CHAPTER 1

The Hands

My hands don't belong on a keyboard.

I know this because I'm looking at them right now, and they're ridiculous. Thick fingers, scarred knuckles, a thumbnail that grew back wrong after I dropped a joist on it in 1994. These are hands that have framed two hundred houses, hung ten thousand doors, sanded enough oak to build a forest backward. There's a callus on my right palm that's been there so long it has its own callus. My ex-wife used to say my hands looked like they'd been in a fight with a toolbox and lost.

She wasn't wrong about much, Linda. Just about me.

I'm sixty-two years old, I'm sitting in the Peet's on Solano Avenue in Albany, California, and I'm building something on a refurbished ThinkPad T480 I bought off eBay for $340, and it would make most people in Silicon Valley laugh until their kombucha came out their nose. A social media platform. Built by a carpenter. Who learned to code from YouTube videos at fifty-five.

Go ahead. I'd laugh too.

But here's the thing about hands like mine — they know when something fits. You run your fingers along a joint and you feel it, right down in the grain. Tight means right. Gaps mean trouble. It doesn't matter if it looks good from across the room. What matters is what happens when you press.

And I've been pressing on this idea for two years, and it's tight. No gaps. It fits.

Let me tell you how a man who couldn't spell "algorithm" until he was fifty-seven ended up trying to take on the biggest companies in human history. It starts, like most things that matter, with somebody I love.

Mia is twelve.

She's my granddaughter — my son Kevin's kid — and she's the reason I stopped sleeping in November. Not because she's in trouble. Because I finally saw what she was doing.

Kevin and his wife Amy brought Mia over for Sunday dinner, which in my apartment means spaghetti and whatever salad I can make look intentional. Kevin's a good man. Works at the State Department of Transportation, has a steady, careful life I couldn't give him when he was small. He forgave me for the drinking years, or says he did, which might be the same thing. Amy's a speech therapist. They're good people. Solid. I'm proud of them in a way that makes your chest hurt if you think about it too long.

So Mia's at my kitchen table, and she's got her phone out — iPhone 15, lavender case, cracked corner she hasn't told Kevin about. She's scrolling TikTok. Making faces at videos. Occasionally she films herself doing something — a dance, a reaction, a lip sync to some Olivia Rodrigo track I can identify from the first three notes. I've done that since I was a kid. This game where I try to name everything before it names itself. License plates from the back of my father's Chevy at six years old. Songs from the first bar. Cars from the grille. Wood species from the grain before the saw touches it. My brain catalogs. It files. She posts her video. She checks the likes. She scrolls more.

Every grandparent in America has seen this. We shake our heads and say "kids these days" and go back to whatever we were doing, which for most of us is the same thing on a bigger screen.

But that night — November 12th, my mother's birthday — I didn't look away. I watched.

I watched Mia scroll for forty-five minutes. In that time, she consumed maybe two hundred pieces of content. She filmed three videos and posted them. She liked sixty or seventy things. She commented on a dozen more. She shared a video with a friend. She followed two new accounts. She tapped on an ad for Nike Air Max 97s, lingered for eight seconds, then scrolled past.

And something in my brain woke up and said: she's working.

Not playing. Not wasting time. Working.

Every scroll generated data. Every like was a signal. Every second she spent on a video told the platform exactly what she wanted and what she didn't. Her three posted videos? Free content that would draw other people to the platform, who would generate more data, who would scroll past more ads. Her face, her voice, her creativity — contributed for free to a machine that prints money.

TikTok made twenty-three billion dollars last year.

Mia made zero.

I sat there at my kitchen table watching my granddaughter generate value for a corporation that couldn't find Albany on a map, and I got angry.

Not the hot kind. I used to do the hot kind. A fifth of Jim Beam and telling your wife she doesn't understand you and waking up on the couch with your shoes still on and your kids' eyes a little more careful than they were yesterday. Fifteen years of that, and all it built was wreckage.

This was the cold kind. The kind that doesn't yell. It measures, and plans, and waits, and builds.

I looked at Mia, and I looked at my hands, and I thought: these hands have built two hundred houses for other people. Maybe it's time to build something for her.

People want this part to be a movie montage — the old guy squinting at a screen, light bulb moments, triumphal music. It wasn't like that. It was two years of feeling stupid. Two years of YouTube tutorials where twenty-three-year-olds talked too fast and assumed I knew what a "dependency" was. Two years of typing things wrong and getting error messages that might as well have been written in Mandarin.

I started in 2019. I was fifty-five. I'd been sober for fifteen years, which means I'd been alive — really alive, not just showing up — for fifteen years. Sobriety gives you time. Enormous, terrifying amounts of time. Time you used to fill with bottles and blackouts is now just... there. Waiting. Asking you what you're going to do with it.

The carpentry years ended when I was thirty. Three herniated discs and a doctor who said "you can keep lifting or you can keep walking, but you can't keep doing both." So I traded the nail gun for a desk — construction technology, building the project management software that coordinates how buildings get built. Ten years of learning how information flows through an organization, how the logic of code and the logic of carpentry are the same logic wearing different clothes. I vested my stock options at year ten. Walked away because I'd learned what I needed to learn.

Real estate came next. Fifteen years — as a realtor first, then building a staging company, then an investment company. I watched money make generous people more generous and frightened people dangerous. I watched first-time buyers sign papers with shaking hands, making the biggest financial decision of their lives across a table from a stranger. I learned to read faces for what's underneath, for the stress that doesn't show until you push.

And through all of it, I built furniture. Live-edge walnut tables, dovetail bookshelves, a rocking chair for my mother that she says is the only place her back doesn't hurt. The hands needed something real to hold.

Then Kevin got me a used MacBook Air for Christmas. Silver aluminum, 13-inch screen, a sticky "e" key he swore would fix itself. "For email, Dad." He had no idea.

I don't remember why I clicked on the first coding tutorial. Brad Traversy, walking through HTML basics in his home office. Maybe it was an ad. Maybe curiosity. But I remember the feeling when I typed ten lines of JavaScript into Notepad++ and a blue box appeared on my screen — 200 pixels by 200 pixels, the digital equivalent of a birdhouse — a box I'd made, a box that hadn't existed thirty seconds ago.

I can build in here.

Not with wood. With logic. A dovetail joint is beautiful, but it serves twelve people at Thanksgiving. A piece of code can serve twelve million.

I'm not a genius at this. My code is ugly by Silicon Valley standards. I name my variables things like "moneyThatGoesToUser" because life's too short for single-letter abbreviations. I comment everything because I know that in three months I won't remember what I was thinking. I still google "how to center a div" at least once a week.

But I can think about how things connect. Which piece holds which piece. Where the weight goes. Where it'll crack if you push too hard. That's carpentry. That's also architecture — the software kind.

And for two years, I built. Quietly. In this Peet's, at this table, with these ridiculous hands.

Cold anger doesn't burn out. Hot anger is a match — it flares and it's gone and you're left in the dark with nothing but ash and apologies. Cold anger is a furnace. You feed it carefully. You control the temperature. You use it to shape things.

I've been feeding mine since November.

Every time I read that Zeta made a hundred and sixty-four billion dollars. Every time I see a retiree scrolling Facebook for two hours a day, generating data, seeing ads, making money for Zeta's CEO while living on a fixed income. Every time some tech CEO talks about "connecting the world" while his company's entire business model depends on selling the world's attention to the highest bidder. Furnace.

Two hundred and fifty billion dollars. That's what the social media industry made last year. A quarter of a trillion dollars. All of it from one thing: human attention. Your attention. My attention. Mia's attention.

They built empires on it. Hired lobbyists. Bought senators. Built campuses with free sushi bars and meditation rooms and rock climbing walls, all paid for by the minutes you and I and twelve-year-old Mia spent looking at our phones.

What if you just... paid people?

What if the money that flows from your eyeballs to their shareholders took a detour through your bank account first? What if every scroll, every like, every minute of your attention was worth something — and you could watch it add up in real time?

The money's already there. The advertisers are already paying. The only question is who gets it.

Right now, they get it. All of it.

I'm building something that changes that.

It's called Ours. And by the time you're done reading this story, you'll understand why some very powerful people tried to stop me, why a woman who heals animals for a living made me believe I could heal something bigger, and why a sixty-two-year-old carpenter from Albany, California is the last person they expected — and the only person who could.

I've got a dog to pick up first. Sixty-pound pit bull with a cone on his head and a bill I can't afford.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 2

The Carpenter Who Codes

Here's something they don't tell you about learning to code at fifty-five: everything you know is both useful and useless at the same time.

Useful, because building is building. Whether you're framing a wall or writing a function, you're taking raw materials and assembling them into something that stands up. How does this piece connect to that piece, and will it hold?

Useless, because the raw materials are invisible. You can't pick up a variable and feel its weight. You can't run your hand along a loop and know if it's going to bind. When wood splits, you hear it. When code breaks, you stare at a screen full of red text that tells you you're wrong on line 147 but won't tell you why.

`TypeError: Cannot read properties of undefined (reading 'map')`

I spent three hours on that error once. In three hours I could've hung a door, installed trim on a bedroom, and still had time for a sandwich. Instead, I sat in this Peet's and quietly lost my mind because I'd put a period where a comma should've gone.

Carpentry is honest. You cut a board too short, you can see it. The board is either the right length or it isn't, and the universe doesn't require a Stack Overflow account to tell you which.

But when code works, it works. In a way that wood never can.

I built a table last year. White oak, hand-planed, wedged tenon joints. Gorgeous. Six weeks of work and it seats eight people. Eight. That's the ceiling. I could build six tables in a year. Forty-eight people, served by a year of my life.

The first useful thing I coded — a material cost calculator for carpentry projects — took me two weeks. The interface looked like a tax form had a baby with a spreadsheet. But I put it on the internet and in the first month, eleven hundred people used it. While I slept.

That's when the dimensions of my life changed.

Kevin gave me the laptop on Christmas Day, 2019. A MacBook Air, used, with a sticky "e" key that he swore would fix itself. It didn't. For six months, every "e" required a deliberate, forceful press, which gave my early code a strange intensity.

"For email, Dad," Kevin said.

An article tried to destroy me later — you'll get to that — and it called me "a carpenter." Like that was all I was. Like the decade in tech and the fifteen years in real estate and the staging company and the investment company didn't exist.

Here's the real story.

I swung a hammer from twenty to thirty. Two hundred houses, give or take, until my back decided it was done. Three herniated discs. That was the first time I lost a career, and it taught me something I'd need later: the thing you think defines you isn't the only thing you are.

A contractor I'd worked for had a brother-in-law at a construction management firm. They needed someone who understood buildings to work on the systems side — scheduling, logistics, eventually the software. I didn't have a degree. I had hands that knew how a building worked from the foundation up, and that was worth more than a diploma when the software was supposed to model what the hands already knew.

Ten years in construction tech. Enterprise systems. Project management platforms. The unsexy kind nobody writes magazine articles about. I was good at it. I understood the users because I'd been the user. After ten years, I vested, and I got out. You build something valuable inside someone else's house, and on the day you're allowed to own a piece of it, you realize the house was never yours.

Then real estate. Fifteen years. And before you make the face — let me tell you what that job actually is. It's sitting across a table from a couple married for thirty years and understanding in five minutes that she wants the house with the garden and he wants the house with the garage and neither of them will say it out loud because the real negotiation is the marriage, not the mortgage. It's watching a first-time buyer's hands shake when they sign papers they don't fully understand.

I watched marriages dissolve over kitchen countertops and friendships shatter over shared investments. I watched people lie about termites and tell the truth about cancer, and I learned that you can predict which one they'll do based on how much money is on the table.

I spun the realtor business into a staging company, then an investment company. Three careers. Carpentry taught me how things fit together. Tech taught me how systems scale. Real estate taught me people.

So when the article said "a carpenter," they weren't wrong. They were just incomplete.

I don't remember choosing the first YouTube tutorial. I remember the video. A kid, couldn't have been older than twenty-two, sitting in what looked like a dorm room. "Everything you see on the internet," he said, "is just text files telling your browser what to show. That's it. And you can write them right now."

I opened a text editor. I typed what he told me to type. I saved the file and opened it in my browser:

Hello, World.

Blue text on a white background, in a font I'd chosen, in a size I'd specified, centered on the page because I'd told it to center. I'd built that.

My hands were shaking. Not from withdrawal — I was fifteen years past that. From the feeling you get when a door you thought was locked has been open the whole time.

I built my first real webpage that week. A portfolio of my furniture, with descriptions in plain English that would make a marketing person cry. "This is a walnut dining table. It seats eight. The legs are tapered. I made it." Nobody visited except me and my mother, who called to say she was proud, which she would have said if I'd posted a photo of a sandwich.

But I'd caught something. A fever.

I'm not a prodigy. I'm not a natural. I saw the code the way a man with reading glasses sees a restaurant menu — squinting, tilting, asking the waiter what the hell "jus" means, and eventually ordering the steak.

I learned HTML first. Then CSS. Then JavaScript, which is where the real pain begins.

JavaScript is a language designed by people who are much smarter than me but apparently hate clarity. There's a function called `this` that refers to different things depending on where you're standing when you call it. I once spent an entire Saturday understanding why `0.1 + 0.2` doesn't equal `0.3` in JavaScript. It equals `0.30000000000000004`. I called Marcus and told him I thought my computer was broken. He laughed so hard he pulled a muscle.

But I kept going. You want to know why? Because I'm an alcoholic.

That sounds like a non sequitur. It's not. Eighteen years of AA teaches you something that Stanford computer science apparently doesn't: how to sit with discomfort. How to be bad at something and not quit. How to show up every single day to a process that is humiliating and slow and occasionally makes you want to throw your laptop out a window, and do it anyway, because the alternative is going back to the person you were before, and that person is dead.

Step One: admit you are powerless. I am powerless over JavaScript's type coercion. I accept this.

Step Ten: continue to take personal inventory. My code has bugs. My code will always have bugs. I will find them and fix them and not pretend they don't exist.

Step Twelve: carry the message. Build something that helps someone. That's the whole game.

By 2021, I could build things. Real things. Not beautiful things — my code looked like a construction site, all scaffolding and exposed wiring. But they worked.

I built a tool that calculated lumber quantities for deck projects. I built a sobriety milestone tracker — you entered your sober date and it told you how many days, hours, and minutes you'd been free, updating in real time. I showed it at a meeting and a woman named Dolores, sixty-seven years sober, watched the seconds tick up on her phone and said, "Every one of those seconds was a war," and I had to leave the room.

I built a website for my friend Terry's landscaping business that was so ugly Terry's wife asked if it was "still loading." I said yes and quietly redesigned the entire thing at two in the morning.

None of this was going to change the world. But each thing taught me how to think in systems.

A house is a system. The foundation holds the frame, the frame holds the roof, the roof sheds the water, the water goes to the ground, the ground supports the foundation. Every piece connects to every other piece. A good carpenter holds the entire system in his head at once. Software is the same thing, except the pieces are invisible and the connections number in the thousands.

Somewhere between the deck calculator and my mother's recipe app, I realized: I might not write code like a Silicon Valley engineer. But I think about systems like a man who's built two hundred of them with his bare hands. And that might matter more.

Without Marcus, there is no Ours. There's just a retired carpenter with a laptop and a grudge.

Marcus Webb is the smartest person I've ever met, and smart is what got him into trouble in the first place.

We met at a meeting in 2012. He'd been sober for three months and looked like he'd been sober for three minutes — shaking, sweating, eyes like two cigarette burns in a bedsheet. He was forty-one, and he'd just lost everything: Goldman Sachs, the apartment in Tribeca, the wife who loved him, the daughter who didn't understand why Daddy wasn't coming home.

Marcus didn't leave Wall Street. Wall Street left him. He was a quant — one of the best — during the era when quants were gods. Derivatives, risk portfolios, arbitrage windows measured in milliseconds. Numbers obeyed Marcus. Willingly, naturally, as if they'd been waiting for someone who understood them.

Then the game went algorithmic. Machine learning, high-frequency trading, systems that processed data at speeds no human brain could match. Marcus was forty years old watching twenty-six-year-olds write programs that did in three seconds what used to take him a day of brilliant calculation. His edge became a museum piece.

He couldn't adapt. For a man whose entire identity was built on being the smartest person in every room, being the slowest was a kind of death.

The cocaine wasn't the cause. It was the response. He snorted his way through the last eighteen months at Goldman desperately, knowing it wasn't working, unable to stop. They didn't fire him for performance. They didn't have to. He was quietly restructured into a position that didn't exist. Wall Street's version of being set out on the ice.

Marcus is Black, six-foot-four, and has a voice that makes whatever he says sound like it matters. He could read you a grocery list and you'd think it was a manifesto.

But people assume a former Goldman quant must be brilliant at everything. He's not. Marcus can model the financial future of a nation-state but cannot remember where he parked his car. He once called me from the Target on Eastshore Highway asking which detergent to buy because there were too many options and the decision tree was "not yielding a clear optimal outcome." Those words. In a Target. To a carpenter.

His ex-wife had handled everything — bills, social calendar, parent-teacher conferences, the basic logistics of being a human being. When she left, Marcus discovered that life is full of systems he'd never bothered to learn. He couldn't cook anything that didn't come with microwave instructions. He showed up to his first day of community college teaching with his shirt inside out. He once paid a $400 electric bill because he didn't know you could dispute it.

Absolute genius in his lane. Absolutely lost in every other lane.

We didn't become friends right away. Recovery doesn't work like that. You circle each other. You listen. You recognize something — not similarity, but a kind of damage that rhymes. His damage was brilliance in a system that outgrew him. Mine was anesthesia against a life I didn't think I deserved.

The friendship happened slowly, then all at once. Coffee at Mel's on San Pablo after a meeting, talking until two in the morning. Then again. Then weekly. Then: "You want to come over for dinner? My apartment's small but the pasta's good." He made the pasta. Terrible. Noodles boiled for twenty minutes, ketchup for sauce. I ate every bite.

By 2019, Marcus was teaching math at Berkeley City College, making a fraction of what he'd earned at Goldman, and happier than he'd ever been. He'd reconnected with his daughter Nia, sixteen by then and terrifyingly smart in the exact same way he was. He still couldn't cook. He still showed up places with mismatched socks. But he was sober, and for the first time in his life, being the smartest person in the room wasn't the point.

He was the first person I showed my code to. He scrolled through the JavaScript and said: "Roger, this works. It's ugly as homemade sin, but it works."

"Thanks."

"Your variable names are a war crime."

"I like descriptive names."

"You named a variable `howMuchWoodDoWeActuallyNeedAfterAccountingForWaste`."

"And you know exactly what it does."

He laughed. Then he said: "Show me more."

And I did. And something started. Not the idea — that came later. But the partnership. Two men who'd failed at the important things finding each other on the other side and discovering that together, they might be one whole person.

Marcus could model financial systems intuitively, precisely, seeing the whole structure at once. I could build interfaces and think about users. He sees equations. I see the woman in apartment 4B who needs her closet door to stop sticking.

Together, we were dangerous.

We just didn't know it yet.

I'm sitting in this Peet's right now, at the same table where all of it started. The "e" key works now — I replaced it with a key from a broken MacBook I found at the Goodwill on San Pablo. Three hours and a magnifying glass. Harder than any piece of code I've ever written.

The barista knows me. Dani, twenty-four, thinks I'm writing a novel. She's right and I'm only just now realizing it. She brings me coffee without asking — light roast, black. She has no idea what's on this screen.

If any of them knew what two broke, middle-aged, recovering addicts were building in this room, they'd move to the Philz down the block.

My hands are on the keyboard. They still don't belong here.

But they're not leaving.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 3

The Napkin

The napkin is framed now. It hangs on the wall of my apartment between a photo of my kids at Coney Island and a watercolor my mother painted of the house I grew up in. Visitors think it's a joke. It's not. It's the most important thing I've ever written, and I wrote it with a pen that was running out of ink on a napkin I stole from a dispenser at Peet's.

It says:

$250B/year. Their money? No. OURS.

User gets 65%. Platform gets 20%. Creators get 10%. Reserve 5%.

Real-time counter. They watch it grow. They tell everyone.

The feed is sacred. Ads in the Market. Separate. Opt-in.

Name: OURS.

Seven lines. Written in forty-five seconds. Two years of thinking that happened in less time than it takes to order a coffee.

It was a Tuesday in January. I was at Peet's because I'm always at Peet's, and Dani had just put my coffee down without being asked. I had my laptop open and I was doing what I'd been doing for three months since the night with Mia: research. The economics of attention. How the money moves. Who pays, who gets paid, who gets nothing. I'd read the SEC filings, the ad tech white papers, the Congressional testimonies where Zeta's CEO sat in a suit that didn't fit and answered questions from senators who didn't understand what they were asking.

I kept getting stuck on the same number: $250 billion.

That's what the global social media advertising market generated last year. More than the GDP of Finland. More than the entire U.S. airline industry. Let me make it mean something.

Divide that $250 billion among the roughly 4.5 billion social media users on Earth. Every person gets about $55 a year. Not life-changing money for someone in Manhattan. But for a student in Manila, a retiree in Alabama, a mother in Lagos — $55 is groceries. $55 is school supplies. $55 is real.

And that's the equal split. A heavy Instagram user in the United States might be worth $80 to $120 per year in ad revenue. A TikTok power creator? Hundreds, maybe thousands.

All of them getting paid exactly zero.

I'd known this for months. That Tuesday morning, the fact found its urgency.

Two women at the next table. Late twenties. One showing the other something on her phone.

"Look — I posted that video Saturday and it already has forty thousand views."

"Forty thousand? That's insane. Are you making money from it?"

"No, I don't have enough followers for the creator fund. You need like ten thousand."

"So TikTok gets forty thousand views of your video and you get..."

"Nothing. But the exposure is good."

I looked at my laptop. TikTok's parent company, ByteDance. Revenue: $120 billion. And this woman had just given them forty thousand views — each one a tiny slice of human attention converted into ad revenue — and she was grateful for the exposure.

My hand reached for the napkin.

The math came first.

$250 billion in global social media ad revenue. That's the pie. Nobody disputes the size. It's right there in the quarterly earnings reports.

Current distribution: platform shareholders get virtually all of it. Users get zero. YouTube's partner program pays creators roughly 55% of ad revenue on monetized content, but exactly 0% on everything else. And YouTube is the generous one.

My distribution: flip it.

Users get 65%. Not creators, not influencers. Users. The woman who scrolls for twenty minutes on her lunch break. The grandfather who watches woodworking videos. The teenager who opens the app because she's bored in study hall. All of them generating data, consuming ads, creating the attention that the entire system runs on. All of them getting paid.

Platform gets 20%. That's us. At scale, $50 billion a year. Zeta runs on a 28% profit margin. Twenty percent is generous to us and revolutionary to users.

Creators get 10%. An additional revenue stream on top of what they earn as users.

Reserve: 5%. For emergencies, expansion, legal defense. Because they will come for us.

I wrote these numbers and stared at them. They worked. Not in a "this sounds nice" way — in a structural way. Every number supports every other number. Change one and the others adjust.

Then the second idea hit.

Here's the problem with paying users: if you're paying people to see ads, won't the platform become a wall of commercials with a social network duct-taped to the side?

So the feed had to be sacred.

I wrote it on the napkin and underlined it twice.

No ads in the feed. Ever. Your social feed — the place where you see your friends, your family, the accounts you follow — is yours. Chronological. No algorithm. No sponsored posts. Just the people you chose to follow, in the order they posted.

The ads go somewhere else entirely. A separate tab. The Market.

The Market is a destination, not an interruption. You go there on purpose. Inside: Discovery Cards — swipeable, visual, every page pays you. Story Experiences — short brand videos, thirty seconds to a minute, paying more because they ask for more. Try-It Tools — interactive things, like placing furniture in your room through your camera, paying the most because they require the most.

And everywhere, always, the counter. Ticking up. $0.003... $0.007... $0.012...

You're in control. You chose to be there. The ads have to be good, because they're competing for your willing attention. Advertisers who make boring ads earn boring engagement. Advertisers who make something worth watching earn real attention from real humans who are there because they want to be.

Users aren't spammed. The social experience is clean. Advertisers get better engagement because willing audiences convert at five to ten times the rate of interrupted ones. And Ours can promise something no other platform can: your feed will never have an ad in it.

I felt the thing I feel when a joint slides together perfectly. The click. The fit. No gaps.

The name was last.

I'd been thinking about names for weeks. The placeholder was "PayFeed," which Marcus would later describe as "the kind of name that makes venture capitalists physically recoil."

A name has to do three things. Easy to say. Mean something true. Sound like it's always existed.

I was staring at the napkin, at the phrase "their money," and my pen was moving before I'd finished the thought. I crossed out "their" and wrote a single word:

OURS.

Not theirs. Not Zeta's. Not some billionaire's side project.

The data is Ours. The money is Ours. The platform is Ours.

Say it: "It's Ours."

That's not a tagline. That's a revolution in five letters.

I called Marcus from the parking lot. Hands shaking.

"I need you to come to the Peet's on Solano."

"Roger, it's eleven in the morning on a Tuesday. I'm teaching."

"What are you teaching?"

"Linear algebra."

"Is anyone listening?"

Pause. "That's hurtful and accurate. Give me twenty minutes."

He showed up in eighteen. I handed him the napkin. He read it standing up, coat still on. He read it again. He sat down. He read it a third time.

Then he took out his phone and started calculating. I watched his eyes go somewhere inside, where the numbers live and talk to each other in a language I'll never speak. His thumbs moved fast. He muttered. "User acquisition cost... retention multiplier... assume 40% margin on ad unit..."

Five minutes. Ten. I didn't interrupt.

He put the phone down. His eyes were wet.

"Roger."

"Yeah."

"This works."

"I know."

"No. You don't. Listen to me." He leaned forward. "This doesn't just work. The unit economics are better than the existing model. You know why?"

I didn't.

"Willing attention is worth more than stolen attention. An advertiser paying to reach someone who chose to be in the Market, who's actively engaged, who has a verified identity because they need one to cash out — that impression is worth three to five times a drive-by Facebook impression. Your CPMs would be higher than theirs, not lower. You're not giving away revenue. You're creating a premium product."

"You're telling me that paying users doesn't cost more — it earns more?"

"I'm telling you that the economic model of the internet — where users are the product — is not just morally wrong. It's economically inefficient. You've stumbled onto something that's both more ethical and more profitable, and the only reason it doesn't exist yet is because the people who could build it are the people who benefit from the current system."

He looked at the napkin again.

"What are you calling it?"

"It's on the napkin."

"Ours." He said it quietly, tasting it. Then again: "Ours." He nodded. "Yeah. That's the name."

"You in?"

He didn't hesitate.

"I've been waiting for this my whole life. I just didn't know I was waiting for a carpenter to bring it to me on a napkin."

We sat in that Peet's until they closed. Dani brought refills without asking.

We talked about everything. The technical architecture. The legal structure. The advertising model. The security requirements. The inevitability that very large companies would try to destroy us.

Marcus ran the numbers a dozen different ways. Conservative. Aggressive. Worst case. Best case. Nuclear apocalypse case, which Marcus defines as "we launch and nobody comes" and which he calculated would cost us roughly $8,000 in wasted cloud computing.

In every scenario except nuclear apocalypse, it worked.

"The question isn't whether the model works," Marcus said, around 4 PM, surrounded by napkins covered in calculations. "The question is whether the world is ready for it."

I looked out the window at Solano Avenue. A woman pushing a stroller while checking her phone — scrolling Instagram, generating data, making money for someone who wasn't her.

"The world has been ready for this since the first time someone asked 'why am I not getting paid for this?' It just needed someone dumb enough to try."

He grinned. "And you're exactly that dumb."

"That's why you love me."

"I tolerate you. There's a difference."

But he was smiling. I took the original napkin and folded it carefully. Put it in my shirt pocket.

"We're going to need a better napkin," Marcus said.

"We're going to need a lot of things."

"Yeah. But Roger?" He looked at me across the table full of numbers. "We've got the one thing that matters."

"What's that?"

"A reason."

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 4

The Dog

His name is Biscuit and he's the dumbest dog alive.

I say this with love. I say this as a man who has watched this sixty-pound pit bull mix eat a sock, throw up the sock, look at the sock, and eat the sock again. Biscuit has the survival instincts of a lemming and the attention span of a goldfish and he is, without question, the best thing that happened to me in the years between getting sober and meeting Sofia.

I got him from the Berkeley Humane Society in 2017. He was two then — a brindle pit with one ear that stands up and one that flops over, as if his head couldn't commit to a look. The shelter said his previous owner surrendered him because they were "moving." What they meant was the dog had eaten a couch. An entire couch. Not a cushion — the structural members. The frame. He ate a couch the way termites eat a house: systematically, from the inside out, over a single afternoon.

I took him home because he looked at me through the kennel gate with the same expression I saw in the mirror the morning I got sober: confused, ashamed, and vaguely hopeful that someone was going to give him another chance.

Biscuit doesn't appear in the business plan. He's not a metaphor. He's a dog who eats things he shouldn't and loves me more than I deserve and who, on a Thursday evening in February, swallowed a chicken bone that got lodged in his intestine and almost died.

Which is how I met the woman who saved him.

There's a particular kind of fear you feel when something you love is suffering and you can't fix it.

I know this fear from the drinking years. I know it from the nights I heard my kids crying through the bedroom door and I was too far gone to get off the floor. I know it from the morning Linda left and Kevin looked at me — he was eleven — and said, "It's okay, Dad," and I understood that my son was taking care of me when it was supposed to be the other way around.

Biscuit was lying on the kitchen floor making sounds I'd never heard a dog make. Not whimpering. Something deeper, from inside his body. His belly was rigid. His eyes were wild. He looked at me not asking me to make it stop, just asking me not to leave.

I Googled "emergency vet Albany" and found the Pet Emergency & Specialty Center of Marin. Open 24 hours. Twenty-two minutes on I-580.

I carried Biscuit to the car. Sixty pounds of shaking dog in my arms. He pressed his face into my neck the whole ride, and I drove with one hand and held him with the other and I talked to him the entire time. "You're okay, buddy. You're going to be okay." Lying to a dog with the same conviction I used to lie to myself.

The clinic was bright — hospital bright, fluorescent. A woman in the waiting room holding a cat, crying quietly. A man with a bandaged German Shepherd. The receptionist — a kid with a nose ring and tired eyes — took one look at Biscuit and said, "Bring him back."

They took him from me. I stood in the hallway with his leash in my hand and nothing to do. Nothing to build. Nothing to fix. Just waiting.

I hate waiting. It's the helplessness. In a workshop, I'm in control. At my laptop, I'm in control. But waiting in a vet's hallway at 9 PM, listening to sounds I can't identify coming from behind a door I can't open — that's surrender. And surrender and I have a complicated relationship.

Forty minutes.

The door opened and she came out. I did not fall in love at first sight. I didn't feel a thunderbolt. What I felt was relief, because she was walking toward me and she wasn't wearing the expression that means your dog is dead.

She was tall — five-nine, maybe five-ten — with dark hair pulled back tight, no makeup, surgical scrubs with what I hoped was not Biscuit's blood on the sleeve. She had a face that's beautiful in a way that has nothing to do with prettiness. Strong jaw. Dark eyes that were both warm and assessing. A woman who'd been awake for a very long time and had decided to keep going anyway.

"Mr. Grubb?"

"Roger."

"I'm Dr. Reyes. Your dog is stable."

Three words. I exhaled for what felt like the first time in an hour.

"He swallowed a chicken bone. It perforated the intestinal wall — there's some peritonitis, infection in the abdominal cavity. We've removed the bone and repaired the perforation. He'll need to stay overnight, possibly two nights. He's on IV antibiotics and pain management."

Clear, precise, no wasted words. I liked her immediately.

"Is he going to be okay?"

"He should recover fully. Pit bulls are resilient." She almost smiled. "And from what I saw in there, Biscuit is... enthusiastic about life."

"He ate a couch once."

"I believe it."

I laughed. She didn't quite laugh, but the tension went out of her eyes for half a second.

"You can see him in the morning. Go home, get some sleep."

"I won't sleep."

"I know." She said it like she knew. Not because she was a vet who dealt with worried owners every night. Because she recognized the feeling.

She turned to go. Stopped.

"Mr. Grubb — Roger. One thing."

"Yeah."

"No more chicken bones."

"I didn't give it to him. He stole it from the trash."

"Then you need a better trash can. Your dog doesn't have judgment. That's your job."

And she was gone.

I drove home with Biscuit's leash on the passenger seat and her voice in my head. Your dog doesn't have judgment. That's your job. Direct. Honest. No performance.

I came back at 7 AM. Biscuit was in a kennel, groggy, with a cone that made him look like a satellite dish with legs. He saw me and his tail moved — not a wag, exactly. A signal. I'm here. You came back.

"Hey, buddy." I put my hand through the kennel gate and he pressed his nose into my palm. Warm. Alive.

"He had a good night."

I turned. Sofia was there. Clean scrubs, hair down, and she looked like a different person. Not softer, exactly. More visible. The surgeon mask was off and underneath it was someone tired in a way that went deeper than one shift.

"Healing well. You can take him home tomorrow."

"Thank you. For saving him."

She looked at me then. Really looked. Not the clinical assessment from last night.

"You build things," she said.

"What?"

"Your hands." She nodded at them. "Those are builder's hands. Carpenter, maybe?"

"Carpenter. Started at twenty. Did a lot of other things since, but the hands remember the first job."

"Pretty hands don't build anything." She said this like it was a law of physics. "My father has hands like that. He built houses in El Salvador, then here."

Something passed between us. Not attraction — not yet. Recognition. She looked at my hands and saw not the scars and calluses but the work. The decades of making things stand up.

I'd spent fifteen years in real estate shaking hands with people who sized you up by your watch and your car. Buyers who'd look at my hands across a closing table and you could see them recalibrate. Downward. Sofia didn't look at my hands and see what was wrong with them. She looked at them and saw what they'd done.

"I should let you rest," she said. To Biscuit, not to me. She scratched behind his floppy ear and his tail moved again. Stronger.

"Come back tomorrow for discharge. We'll go over care instructions." She paused. "And Roger — buy a trash can with a locking lid."

"Yes ma'am."

"Don't 'ma'am' me. I'm younger than you."

"Everyone's younger than me."

Almost. She almost smiled.

I went home and bought a trash can with a locking lid. Forty-seven dollars at Target. The most important purchase of my life, it turns out.

Because the next week, when I came back for Biscuit's follow-up, she was there. And the week after that.

Tuesdays. Our day.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 5

The Ask

There's a moment in every building project when you stop drawing and start cutting. The plans are done. The math is checked. And you stand there with the saw in your hand and the board in front of you, and you know that the first cut turns theory into commitment. You can erase a pencil line. You can't un-cut a board.

Marcus and I had the napkin. We had the math. We had two days of scribbling on every flat surface in my apartment, including the back of an electric bill and, at one desperate point, my forearm. What we didn't have was the ability to build it.

I could build a frontend. I could make things look right, feel right, respond when you touched them. My code was ugly but functional — solid as bedrock, never making the cover of Architectural Digest.

Marcus could model anything with money in it. Give him a revenue stream and he'd tell you where it came from, where it was going, and what it would look like in seven years under twelve different scenarios.

But the backend — the engine that would make Ours work at scale — was neither of us. The database architecture. The real-time payment processing. The ad-serving infrastructure. The security layers. The things that happen behind the wall, invisible to the user, holding everything up.

We needed someone who could build the hidden parts. And we needed them to believe in what we were doing, because we couldn't pay them.

I'd been active in coding communities online — Stack Overflow, Hacker News, the JavaScript subreddit — not as a contributor, but as a lurker. I spent hours on GitHub reading other people's code the way I used to read Fine Woodworking. Studying technique. Playing my identification game. I could look at a repository's structure and guess the developer's background within thirty seconds. Computer science degree: everything in neat folders, naming conventions followed to the letter. Self-taught: creative structure, unconventional naming, comments that sounded like someone talking to themselves. Bootcamp graduate: clean on the surface, messy underneath.

I'd been following a developer named Jake Morrison for about six months. His GitHub profile said he was twenty-five, and his commit history suggested someone who coded compulsively, with occasional bursts of brilliance that made me stop scrolling and stare.

Jake had built a real-time analytics dashboard for a nonprofit — an open-source project that tracked donation flows and showed them updating live on screen. The counter ticked up with every donation. $1... $5... $3.50... refreshing in real time, smooth as water.

I stared at that counter for twenty minutes the first time I saw it. It was what I'd been imagining for Ours — the real-time earnings display. This kid had already built the thing I needed.

I sent him a message on GitHub. Brief. Direct.

"I'm building a social platform that pays users for their attention. Your real-time donation tracker is the closest thing to what I need that I've found anywhere. Would you be interested in talking?"

Fourteen minutes later:

"What do you mean, pays users?"

I sent him the napkin.

Three minutes:

"When can we talk?"

Jake Morrison showed up to our first video call from what appeared to be a closet. Not metaphorically — an actual closet. Hangers behind his head. A winter coat draped over a chair that was definitely not designed to be in a closet. He was thin, sharp-featured, with dark circles that said he'd been staring at screens since before screens were flat.

"Sorry about the background. My roommate's doing a podcast in the living room."

"I once worked on a job site next to a guy who played accordion during lunch. This is fine."

He laughed. Marcus was on the call too, wearing his professor face — thoughtful, measured, slightly intimidating. When Marcus wants to evaluate someone, he goes quiet and lets them talk.

Jake filled the silence immediately. Which told us he was young. But what he said told us he was brilliant.

"I've been thinking about this since you sent me the napkin." He pulled up handwritten notes, diagrams, technical specifications. He'd done this in fourteen hours. "The real-time counter is the easy part. I've built those. The hard part is the payment infrastructure. You need to process micropayments — fractions of a cent — at scale, in real time, and settle them without the transaction fees eating your margin. Traditional payment processors charge twenty to thirty cents per transaction. If you're paying someone three-tenths of a cent per ad interaction, the processing fee is a hundred times the payment."

Marcus leaned forward. Jake had just identified the problem Marcus had spent two days modeling.

"So how do you solve it?"

"Batch processing. You don't settle each micropayment individually. You aggregate them — every hour, or when the balance hits a threshold. The user sees the counter tick up in real time, but the actual settlement happens in batches. The counter is a display layer. The payments are a separate system underneath."

"How do you prevent fraud?"

"Phone verification for account creation. Behavioral analysis on engagement patterns — bots don't scroll like humans. And the most elegant anti-fraud mechanism is already built into the system." Jake grinned. "You need a verified bank account to cash out. Bots can't cash out. The payment verification IS the identity verification."

Marcus looked at me. I looked at Marcus. This kid sees it.

"How old are you?" I asked.

"Twenty-six."

"Why'd you leave Stanford?"

He paused. "How'd you know I went to Stanford?"

"Your GitHub commits reference Stanford network libraries. And your code is structured, documented, consistent. You didn't learn that on YouTube."

"I left because I didn't want to build ad tech for Google."

"What do you want to build?"

"Something that matters."

Marcus gave the smallest possible nod.

"Jake. We can't pay you. We have no funding. We have no office. I'm a retired carpenter and Marcus is a math professor and we're building this from my kitchen table. Everything about this is insane."

"I know."

"The big platforms will try to destroy us."

"I know."

"You'd be walking away from a career at any company in Silicon Valley to work for free with two guys who are twice your age."

"You're not half as qualified as you think you aren't. Everyone I went to school with could build this technically. None of them would think to build it, because they've been trained to think inside the existing system. You're outside it." He leaned forward. "Mr. Grubb, I've read your code."

"God help you."

"Your variable names are insane."

"I like them."

"You named one `theExactAmountOfMoneyThatShouldGoToThePersonNotTheCorporation`."

"And you knew exactly what it did."

Full smile. "Yeah. I did. That's why I'm here."

Jake drove up from New York the following weekend. He walked into my apartment, looked at the whiteboards, looked at the sketches, looked at the napkin framed on the wall, and said: "This is the most dangerous thing I've ever seen."

"Dangerous how?"

"Dangerous because it works. Everyone in tech thinks paying users is a nice idea that can't scale. They're wrong. The only reason no one's done it is because the people with the resources to build it are the people who'd lose the most if it existed."

I learned things about Jake over that first weekend. Not because he told me — Jake didn't tell you things about himself. He showed you.

He flinched when I raised my voice. Not dramatically — a micro-adjustment, a tightening of the shoulders, a fractional turning away. The kind that's been rehearsed a thousand times until it's automatic. I noticed it on Saturday when I got excited about a feature idea and my voice climbed, and Jake's whole body went half an inch sideways. Away.

He also asked permission for everything. Not the big things — the small things. "Is it okay if I use this mug?" "Can I open this window?" Little questions that sound polite but aren't. They're the vocabulary of a person who grew up in a house where every action required clearance from someone whose approval was essential and impossible to predict.

He mentioned his mother once. "My mother wanted me to finish Stanford" — in a tone so flat, so carefully emptied of emotion, that the absence told me everything about the feeling underneath. I'd learn more later. The yelling kind of household, not the hitting kind. A mother who controlled every variable of her son's life with the volume and intensity of her disapproval. She loved him. But she loved him the way a sculptor loves marble: by removing everything that didn't match her vision. What was left was a young man with extraordinary technical gifts and no internal compass. He could build anything you asked him to. He just couldn't tell you whether he should.

That gap — between ability and judgment — would nearly destroy everything we built.

He set up at my kitchen table and started building. Not planning. Building. Within four hours, he had a prototype of the payment aggregation system running. A counter that ticked up, a balance that accumulated, a batch settlement function that bundled micropayments into cashable amounts.

Marcus watched over his shoulder, checking the financial logic. I watched the two of them work together — Marcus's careful, methodical thinking and Jake's fast, intuitive building. Framing and finishing.

We worked until 3 AM. The prototype had a working counter, a working wallet, and a simulated ad interaction that paid the user $0.01 per engagement.

I opened it on my phone. Tapped a simulated ad. Watched the counter tick from $0.00 to $0.01.

One cent. The first cent. The most important cent in the history of this project.

"It's a penny," Jake said.

"It's proof," Marcus said.

I looked at the counter. $0.01. One cent that nobody in the history of social media had ever given to a user.

"It's a start," I said.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 6

The Build Begins

I need to tell you what it looks like when three men who have no business building a technology platform decide to build a technology platform.

It looks like my apartment.

Marcus bought four more whiteboards. Jake brought in two monitors, a tangle of cables, and a power strip with six outlets and not enough prongs for all of them — eleven devices, six outlets, three splitters, one fire hazard. I moved the kitchen table six inches to the left so we could fit a folding table for what Jake called "the dev station."

My apartment is 740 square feet. Before Ours, it was a perfectly reasonable space for one man and one dog. After Ours, it was a startup headquarters with a couch.

Biscuit positioned himself under Jake's table, on the theory that proximity to the youngest person maximized his chances of unauthorized snacks. He was correct.

We looked ridiculous. Two men in their late fifties and early sixties, one in his mid-twenties, crammed into a space designed for one, arguing about database architecture over leftover spaghetti at midnight. If you'd walked in and I'd told you we were building something to compete with Zeta, Google, and TikTok, you would've smiled the way you smile at a child who tells you they're going to be an astronaut.

The first week was architecture. Jake drew diagrams on the whiteboards — boxes connected to boxes connected to circles connected to databases, arrows indicating data flow.

"This is the user service. It handles authentication, profiles, preference management. It talks to the ad service here, which talks to the payment aggregator here, which batches transactions and sends them to the settlement service here."

"And this?" I pointed at a circle floating between everything.

"The real-time event stream. Every user interaction fires an event. The stream routes it to the appropriate service."

"What's this?" Marcus pointed at a small square labeled "COUNTER."

"The most important piece of UI in the entire system. It subscribes to the event stream and updates the user's visible balance in real time. Someone engages with an ad in the Market, the event fires, the payment aggregator calculates the payout, the counter increments. $0.003... $0.006... $0.009..."

"And the actual money?"

"Doesn't move yet. The counter is a promise. The money settles in batches — every six hours, or when the balance hits a threshold. The user never sees the batching. They just see the number going up."

Marcus studied the diagram. "The counter is a dopamine mechanism. But instead of likes, it's money."

"Exactly."

"Which means it's more addictive than likes. Not everyone wants social validation, but everyone wants money."

"That's what makes this dangerous. In a good way. The engagement loop isn't manufactured — it's real. Users aren't getting a fake reward. They're getting actual value. The dopamine is backed by dollars."

I built the screens.

While Jake and Marcus argued about event-driven architecture and whether to use PostgreSQL or MongoDB — a debate that lasted three days and got heated enough that Biscuit left the room — I did what I know how to do.

A door handle is a user interface. A staircase is a user flow. Every screen needs to answer: what did the user just do, what are they doing now, and what should they do next?

Every screen was a sketch first. Pencil on paper. Here's the feed. Clean. Chronological. No ads. Just the people you chose to follow, in the order they posted.

Here's the Market. A separate tab. You tap into it on purpose. Inside: Discovery Cards — swipeable, visual. Story Experiences — short brand videos that pay more. Try-It Tools — interactive things that pay the most.

And everywhere, the counter. Top right corner. Small but visible. Ticking up. A quiet, constant reminder that your time here has value.

Jake took my static pages and made them alive. Where I'd drawn a card, he made it swipeable. Where I'd drawn a counter, he made it tick. Where I'd placed a button that said "Cash Out," he connected it to a simulated payment system.

The first time I swiped a Discovery Card on my phone and watched the counter tick from $0.00 to $0.01, I felt it again. The click. The fit.

We fell into a rhythm. Mornings, Jake coded. He started at 6 AM with a focus so intense that speaking to him before noon was like interrupting a surgeon. He typed with a speed that made my carpentry-damaged fingers ache just watching.

Marcus worked afternoons and evenings. He was refining the financial model, running scenario after scenario. What if we launched with a thousand users? A hundred thousand? What if advertisers paid $5 CPMs? $15? Every assumption tested, every variable stressed. He was trying to break our model, and it kept not breaking.

I worked whenever they didn't need the kitchen table — early mornings and late nights. Screen by screen. Slow. Deliberate. My code inched forward while Jake's sprinted. But mine looked like something a human would want to use.

Three weeks in, we had something. Not a product — a prototype. You could create an account. See a feed. Tap into the Market, browse Discovery Cards, watch a simulated Story Experience. Watch the counter tick up. See your balance grow. Tap "Cash Out" and watch the money move to a simulated bank account.

It was held together with digital duct tape and prayer. Jake estimated a hundred simultaneous users before collapse. The ad content was all placeholder. The payment system was simulated.

But the experience was real enough.

Marcus downloaded it on his phone. He sat on my couch, opened the Market, and started swiping Discovery Cards. For twenty minutes. In silence. His counter ticked: $0.01... $0.04... $0.08... $0.11...

When he looked up, his expression had changed.

"Roger. This is the most seductive thing I've ever held in my hand. And I'm a recovering addict. I know seductive."

"Is that a compliment?"

"It's a warning. Every person who sees this counter will check it. They'll check it in line at the grocery store and in bed before they fall asleep and first thing in the morning. Because it's real money, and it's growing, and it's theirs."

"Good. That's the point."

"No, listen." He set the phone down. "The point is beautiful. The mechanism is powerful. You need to understand the difference. Those platforms use dopamine. You're using money. Money beats dopamine. Every time."

Jake looked up from his code. "He's right. This is weapons-grade engagement. The question is whether you trust users with it."

"I trust users. I don't trust platforms. That's the whole point. We built this so users are in control — they choose to enter the Market, they choose how long to stay, they choose when to leave. Nobody tricks them. Nobody hijacks their feed. Every other platform uses powerful mechanisms to override choice. We use a powerful mechanism to reward it."

Marcus picked up the phone again. $0.11.

"Then let's put this in people's hands."

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 7

The Vet's Hands

I brought pierogi.

This requires explanation. My mother is German-Russian — her family were Volga Germans, ethnic Germans who'd lived in Russia for two hundred years before her parents came over in the 1930s. My grandmother spoke German at home and Russian when she was angry, which was often enough that I picked up the swear words in both languages before I learned them in English. Ma taught me three things: how to keep a clean house, how to say what you mean, and how to make pierogi. The first two didn't stick. The third saved my life, in the sense that a man who can cook is a man who can feed himself, and a man who can feed himself after his wife leaves and his kids grow up is a man who might actually survive.

Pierogi are labor. You make the dough from scratch — flour, egg, sour cream, a pinch of salt. Roll it thin. Cut circles with a glass. Then the filling: potatoes mashed with sharp cheddar and onions fried until they're gold and sweet. You spoon the filling onto each circle, fold, press the edges with a fork. Every crimp matters. Skip one and the whole thing opens up in the water, dumps its guts, falls apart. It's architecture.

My grandmother made them with sauerkraut and pork, the old-country way. Ma softened it for American kids — potato and cheese. I make them both ways now. The old-country version when I'm feeling stubborn. The American version when I'm cooking for someone I'm trying to impress.

That Tuesday, I made the American version.

I made them because I couldn't stop thinking about Sofia Reyes. About her hands. Not in a romantic way. Or not only. I'd watched her examine Biscuit — the precision of her touch, her fingers reading tissue the way mine read wood grain. She pressed and listened. She felt for what was underneath. She moved with the economy of someone who has performed the same actions ten thousand times and burned away everything unnecessary.

So I made pierogi because it was something my hands could do while my mind tried to figure out why a woman I'd spoken to for eleven minutes was living in my head rent-free.

Biscuit's follow-up was at 2 PM. I got there at 1:30 because I'm always early — a carpentry habit. The pierogi were in a Tupperware container in a paper bag, because I'm sixty-two and I bring food in paper bags and I'm not going to pretend that's not who I am.

The clinic was different during the day. Calmer. Natural light. A fish tank in the waiting room with three angelfish and a pleco sucking algae off driftwood.

Sofia walked in and Biscuit's tail went nuclear. Full helicopter. She hadn't even looked at him yet and he was already in love. Dogs know.

"Mr. Grubb."

"Roger. We covered this."

"Roger." She washed her hands, pulled on gloves, and turned to Biscuit. Before she touched him, she paused. Lowered her face to his level. Looked into his eyes. Didn't say anything. Just read him.

Then she examined him. Abdomen, incision site, gums, eyes, lymph nodes. Her hands moved fluid and deliberate, each touch landing exactly where it needed to be and staying exactly as long as it needed to stay.

"He's healing perfectly. Stitches can come out in a week."

"I'll bring him back."

She looked at me. The longer look.

"You don't have to drive across town for stitch removal."

"I like the drive."

"It's twelve minutes."

"I like short drives."

Almost smiled. "What's in the bag?"

I don't know if you've ever fed a veterinary surgeon handmade pierogi in an exam room at 2:30 on a Tuesday afternoon, but I'm going to recommend it as a life strategy.

First, she says she can't eat during clinic hours. Then she looks at the pierogi. Then she says maybe just one. Then she eats one standing up, leaning against the counter, and her eyes close for a moment — half a second — and when they open, something has shifted. The armor has a crack in it. Not because the food is magic. Because someone made it for her, and no one has done that in a while.

"Did you make these?"

"My mother's recipe. Her mother's, actually. My grandmother came over from Russia and she brought exactly three things: a Bible, a cast-iron pan, and the recipe for pierogi."

"My parents are from El Salvador. Chalatenango." She took another pierogi. The "just one" rule was dead. "My mother would kill for this recipe."

"My mother would kill if I gave it away. But I might be persuadable."

"What would it take?"

"Tell me what your work feels like."

She stopped chewing. Nobody asks her that.

"What do you mean?"

"I'm a carpenter. I know what my work feels like. The weight of the wood, the sound of the saw, the way the sawdust smells different depending on the species. I'm curious what your version is."

She was quiet for a long time. Long enough that Biscuit put his chin on my knee.

Then Sofia Reyes told me what her work feels like, and I fell in love. Not with her body, not with her face. With her mind.

"It feels like listening," she said. "My patients can't talk. They can't tell me where it hurts. So I have to listen with my hands. The tissue tells me things — swelling says inflammation, rigidity says pain, heat says infection. I put my hands on an animal and I'm reading a story written in a language I've spent fifteen years learning."

She paused. She wasn't used to talking about this.

"The hardest part isn't the surgery. The surgery is mechanics — I know where to cut, what to move, how to repair. The hard part is the reading. Because animals are honest. They don't perform wellness. They don't lie about their pain. If a dog is hurting, the dog shows you. They don't have ego. They just have bodies, and the bodies tell the truth."

Something crossed her face. A memory.

"I had a relationship. Before vet school. He was charming the way people who've practiced charm their entire lives are charming — smooth, seamless, nothing out of place. Every word could be a fabrication, and you wouldn't know. Not for months. Not until the architecture of lies got so complicated that a piece fell away, and then another, and then you were standing in the rubble of something you thought was real."

"After that, I needed to be somewhere honest. I needed to put my hands on something that couldn't lie to me. Animals don't know how to lie. A dog doesn't pretend to love you when it doesn't. What you see is what's real. I needed real more than I needed anything else in the world."

"People lie about their pain," I said.

"Constantly. I decided I'd rather spend my life with patients who couldn't speak than with people who spoke and said nothing true."

We looked at each other across the exam room. Biscuit between us, his tail sweeping the table.

"I know what you mean. Wood doesn't lie either. If the grain is weak, it shows you. If the joint is bad, it tells you. I spent years lying to everyone in my life — my wife, my kids, myself. The wood was the only thing that told me the truth."

"What truth?"

"That I was falling apart. Every board I cut clean was evidence that my hands still worked, even when the rest of me didn't."

I don't know why I told her that. I don't talk about the drinking years to women I'm trying to impress. But she'd been honest with me. So I told her about the wood. About the years when the only straight lines in my life were the ones I cut.

She didn't flinch. She didn't do the thing people do when you mention alcoholism — the small recoil, the recalibration. She just listened. With her full attention, without judgment.

"You're sober now."

"Twenty-one years."

"That's a long time to hold steady."

"Some days are longer than others."

She nodded. She understood that — not because she was an addict, but because she'd stood in the wreckage of a beautiful lie and rebuilt her ability to trust from the foundation up.

"I should get back." She'd eaten four pierogi. The Tupperware was half empty.

"Take the rest."

"I can't take your food."

"You just did. Bring the container back next Tuesday."

"There's no appointment next Tuesday."

"Then I'll make one up. Biscuit probably needs... something. A checkup. An emotional support consultation."

She laughed. An actual laugh. Not the almost-smile. A real laugh from the belly that surprised the person making it. She covered her mouth with her hand — her surgeon's hand, still in the blue glove.

"You're asking me out."

"I'm asking you to return my Tupperware. There's a difference."

"Is there?"

"Not really."

She pulled off the gloves. Dropped them in the trash.

"I don't date patients' owners. It's a rule."

"Okay."

"But Biscuit's follow-up care is almost done. After the stitch removal, he's no longer a patient."

"So next Tuesday..."

"Next Tuesday he's a former patient. And you're a man with good pierogi and interesting hands." She picked up the Tupperware. "But I want the recipe too."

"That's a second-date request."

"Presumptuous."

"Accurate."

She was at the door. Biscuit strained toward her. She scratched his floppy ear and he leaned into her hand with his whole body.

"Tuesday," she said.

"Tuesday."

She left. I sat in the exam room for a full minute, holding the leash of a pit bull who was trying to follow her, feeling something I hadn't felt in so long I'd forgotten what it was called.

I drove home. Called Marcus.

"How's the dog?"

"Healing."

"Good. Listen, I've been running the numbers on—"

"Marcus."

"What?"

"I met someone."

Long pause. Then, very quietly: "It's about damn time."

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 8

First Blood

The first fifty users were family.

Not literally — though Kevin and Amy signed up, and Marcus's daughter Nia, and my mother, who needed forty-five minutes of phone guidance and called three times to ask if this was "the Facebook." I mean the first fifty were people who knew us, trusted us, and were willing to put an unfinished app on their phones because someone they loved asked.

Marcus recruited his community college students. Jake brought in eight friends from his coding circles. I asked people from my AA meetings, my carpentry network, Kevin's friends, Amy's coworkers.

Fifty people. A rounding error at Instagram.

But fifty was enough.

Day One: seventeen users opened the app. The feed had almost nothing in it — fifty people don't generate much content. Kevin posted a photo of Mia at her school science fair. Amy posted a sunset over the Richmond Bridge. The Market had twenty Discovery Cards Jake and I had built using stock photos and fake brands. "Wilderness Outfitters — 20% Off Hiking Boots." None of it was real, but the experience was: you swiped a card, you watched the counter tick.

Day Two: twenty-nine users. People were texting each other: "Did you see the counter thing?"

Day Three: all fifty.

People were checking the app eight times a day. For context, the average Instagram user opens the app about seven times, and Instagram has spent fifteen years and billions of dollars engineering that behavior. We had fifty people, stock photos, fake brands, and a counter. We beat Instagram's engagement rate in seventy-two hours.

Marcus ran the numbers at midnight, laptop balanced on a stack of carpentry magazines.

"Roger. Come look at this."

The chart showed daily active usage by hour. Three spikes: 7 AM, 12 PM, 10 PM. Morning, lunch, bedtime. But between the spikes, instead of the gradual decline you'd see on a normal social app, a sustained plateau. People weren't just checking at peak times. They were checking throughout the day.

"They're watching the counter," Marcus said.

"Of course they are. It's money."

"No, listen. They're not spending more time in the Market. The average session is still about four minutes. They're opening the app, checking their balance, and closing it. Multiple times a day. The counter itself is the engagement mechanism."

Jake looked over. "Same behavior as stock trading apps. People check their portfolio ten times a day even when they're not trading. They just want to see the number."

Average daily earnings per user in the first week: $0.34. Two dollars and thirty-eight cents for the week.

"That's nothing," Jake said. "That wouldn't buy a coffee."

"It's not nothing," I said. "Two dollars and thirty-eight cents isn't nothing to Delores. The one in Marcus's class. She's a home health aide going back to school at forty-six. Two dollars is bus fare. We're not building this for people who think two dollars isn't worth mentioning. We're building it for people who count."

What fifty people taught us:

The counter was everything. More important than the feed. More important than the content. Every tick was proof — visible, real-time proof — that their attention had value.

The Market needed to be better. Fake Discovery Cards got stale after three days. We needed real brands. No advertisers would commit to a platform with fifty users. But the Market had to feel curated, worth visiting.

The feed was lonely. Fifty people don't make a community. But the people who did engage used the platform differently. Without an algorithm amplifying outrage, without ads interrupting every fourth post, people were just... people. Talking. Sharing. Being human.

Mia signed up on Day Four. Kevin was nervous — she was twelve — but I showed him the security features and the fact that all fifty users were people we knew.

She posted a video of herself dancing. Eleven likes, which in the economy of fifty users is like going viral. She checked her counter: $0.02. She showed Kevin.

"Dad, I made two cents."

"That's... great, sweetie."

"On TikTok I never made anything. This pays me."

Eight words. The entire thesis of Ours. I wrote it on the back of the electric bill still on the kitchen counter and tacked it to the wall next to the napkin.

The cease-and-desist letter arrived on a Thursday.

Physical mail. A digital company threatened through the United States Postal Service. The letter was from Harwick, Strand & Associates, LLP. Three pages, single-spaced, on paper so thick you could feel the money in it.

The translation: Our client, who we refuse to name, holds patents related to user engagement compensation systems. Your platform appears to infringe on multiple patents. Cease all development immediately.

No client name. No specific patents. No evidence. Just a threat, wrapped in expensive paper, delivered to my mailbox in Albany.

I read it three times. Once for comprehension. Once for fury. Once to confirm they hadn't named who was afraid of me. Because that's what this was. Fear. Dressed in a suit.

Fifty users. Fake brands. Stock photos. And someone with Harwick, Strand & Associates on retainer was spending money to tell me to stop.

I called Marcus.

Silence. Then: "Fifty users."

"Fifty users."

"Someone is tracking us."

"Do we have a lawyer?"

"Marcus, we can barely afford spaghetti."

Then the Marcus Webb laugh — low, rumbling.

"Roger. They just told us we're real. A cease-and-desist for a fifty-user platform isn't legal strategy. It's a panic response. Someone inside one of these companies saw what we're building, did the math, and panicked. You don't send lawyers after an idea that doesn't work. You ignore it. You let it die. The fact that they sent this means they know it works."

I walked to Ace Hardware on Solano. Bought a black document frame — 8½-by-11, the cheap MCS brand with fake walnut grain, $7.99. A real carpenter buying a fake wood frame. Trimmed the letter with an X-Acto #11 blade, hung it with a finish nail I tapped in with a 16-ounce Estwing that cost more than the frame. Right next to the napkin and the electric bill.

Jake came over that evening. "Is that what I think it is?"

"Our first review," I said.

Something had changed. Until that letter, we were three guys with an idea. After that letter, we were three guys with enemies. And enemies are the clearest proof that what you're doing matters.

Let them send their letters. Let them hire their lawyers.

I've been building things my whole life. I don't stop because someone tells me to.

I stop when it's done.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 9

The Money Problem

Forty-seven thousand dollars.

That's what I had. Liquid. Touchable. Forty years of working, a divorce that took what it took, two kids raised, fifteen years of drinking and the wreckage that comes with it. The tech options had cashed out twenty years ago. The real estate investments were tied up in properties I couldn't sell fast. On paper I wasn't poor. For the purpose of building a technology platform, I was broke.

Marcus had less. Community college professors don't make Goldman money.

Jake had negative money. A hundred and eighty thousand in student debt from three years at Stanford, accruing interest at a rate that Marcus once calculated on a napkin and then turned face-down because looking at it made him uncomfortable. Jake lived in a converted walk-in closet in an Oakland apartment shared with four other people.

Three men. Roughly negative $130,000 in combined net worth. Building something that needed at least $500,000 to reach minimum viable product.

The obvious answer was investors.

The obvious answer was a trap.

My first pitch meeting was on Sand Hill Road. First Light Ventures. Angel group, $250K to $500K, 10-15% of the company. I drove down in my 2011 Ford F-150 with 187,000 miles and a passenger seat I'd vacuumed three times that still looked like a lumberyard. Rush hour traffic from the East Bay stretched the drive to ninety minutes. Time enough to rehearse the pitch and wonder if I was wasting everyone's afternoon.

The co-working space had glass walls, exposed brick, and furniture that cost more than my truck. I was wearing khakis and a button-down Linda had bought me in 2008.

I delivered the pitch. Twelve slides. Fifteen minutes. Direct, no jargon. Here's the problem, here's the solution, here's the math, here's the team.

They asked good questions. Sharp questions. Then came the one I'd been dreading.

"What's your experience in tech?"

I told the truth. Carpenter, ten years. Construction tech, ten years. Real estate, fifteen years. Self-taught coder at fifty-five. Technical co-founder: a twenty-six-year-old Stanford dropout.

The room temperature changed. The questions got different. Less about the business and more about me.

"Have you considered bringing on an experienced CEO?"

"Someone to run my company for me?"

"Provide operational expertise."

"I've run construction sites with twenty subcontractors and million-dollar budgets. I've managed a pipeline of forty residential properties at a time. I've built a staging company from a pickup truck into a half-million-dollar operation. A startup is a construction project with code instead of lumber."

They looked at each other. The look that means they've already decided and the rest of the meeting is theater.

They passed. "The idea is compelling but the team needs seasoning." Investor-speak for: we don't trust you to build it, because you don't look like the people we usually trust.

The second meeting was worse.

Catherine Chen, partner at Redwood Capital, was direct, aggressive, uninterested in politeness. She'd built her career funding social platforms — early money in two companies I'd heard of and three I hadn't.

She took the meeting because Marcus, through a chain of connections stretching from Berkeley to Sand Hill Road, had gotten the deck in front of her. She'd called within two hours. "I want to meet. Tomorrow."

We video-called. Glass office in San Francisco. Wall of photos — her shaking hands with people from magazine covers.

"I read your deck," she said. No pleasantries. "The economics are sound. The market thesis is correct. Paying users isn't just ethical, it's more profitable. I've been saying this for five years and no one has had the balls to build it."

"We're building it."

"You're three people in an apartment. You're sketching it."

"Sketches become blueprints. Blueprints become buildings."

She smiled. Not warmly. Appraisingly.

"I'll give you two million. For thirty-five percent of the company."

Two million dollars. Enough to build the MVP, hire engineers, launch properly, survive the first year.

I looked at Marcus, off-camera but visible to me. He was doing math on his phone. If Catherine took 35% at the seed round, standard dilution through Series A and B would leave me owning roughly 8% of my own company by the time it mattered.
