Eight percent of Ours would no longer be ours.

"That's generous," I said.

"It's fair. You have no revenue, no user base, no proven team. Ideas at your stage are worth about five to six million pre-money. I'm offering two at five-point-seven. It's textbook."

"I need to think about it."

"Ideas don't stay unique. Someone else will build this. Someone younger, with a better resume. The window is open now. It won't stay open."

She wasn't wrong. That was the worst part.

"I'll call you Friday."

"I'll be waiting. But not long."

Marcus turned his phone to me:

Seed: 35% to Redwood* *Series A: 20% dilution* *Series B: 18% dilution* *Roger's stake after Series B: 7.8%

"So what do I do? We need money."

"We need money that doesn't cost us the company."

I walked. Down Solano Avenue, past Ace Hardware, past Peet's, past Tony's. January fog had rolled in off the Bay and everything was gray and close, the kind of afternoon where the whole East Bay feels like the inside of a pocket. But I walked, because everything in my apartment was asking the same question: what are you willing to give up?

Catherine's offer was real. Two million dollars. Enough to build, hire, fight.

And all it would cost was ownership.

I called Marcus from the sidewalk. Fog in my hair. Collar up against the damp.

"I'm not taking it."

"Okay."

"We'll find another way."

"Okay."

"You think I'm crazy."

"Roger, I left Goldman Sachs to teach algebra to eighteen-year-olds. I'm not in a position to call anyone crazy. But you're choosing hard over easy. Slow over fast. Control over resources."

"I'm choosing to own what I build."

Silence on the line. Fog thickening on the street.

"Then we bootstrap. We scrape. We do it the way people built things before venture capital existed — with sweat and stubbornness and the willingness to eat rice and beans for a year."

"Jake's already eating rice and beans."

"Then he's got a head start."

Catherine called on Friday. I told her no. Three seconds of silence. Then: "You'll be back. They always come back."

I hung up and framed those words in my head, right next to the cease-and-desist.

My wall was getting crowded.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 10

Tuesday

Tuesdays became the architecture of my heart. If you'd asked me at sixty-one what day of the week mattered least, I'd have said Tuesday. The forgotten day. Monday has the weight of beginning. Friday is Friday. Tuesday is a placeholder.

Sofia Reyes turned Tuesday into the day I got dressed for.

The stitches came out on a Tuesday. Biscuit sat on the exam table with the patience of a dog who has been promised something. Sofia worked with the small scissors, and we talked.

Not about anything important. She asked about the apartment. I told her about the whiteboards. She asked about the whiteboards. I told her about Ours — the napkin, the math, the counter, the fifty users, the cease-and-desist.

"You're building this to pay people," she said.

"Yes."

"Because you believe their attention has value."

"Because it does have value. Measurable, calculable, specific value. And they're being stolen from."

"That word — stolen. You use it like it's personal."

"My granddaughter is twelve. She spends two hours a day making content for platforms that pay her nothing. Yeah. It's personal."

Sofia pulled the last stitch. Set down the scissors.

"My parents came to this country with nothing. They worked in a restaurant — my father in the kitchen, my mother cleaning. Sixty hours a week. Saved everything for fifteen years so I could go to veterinary school." She paused. "The idea that someone is extracting value from people who create it and giving nothing back — I don't need a business model to understand that. I grew up watching it."

"The stitches are out," she said. "Biscuit is officially a former patient."

"And I'm officially not a patient's owner."

"No. You're not."

"So."

"So."

"Tuesday. Dinner. You pick the place because I've been cooking for one for seven years and every restaurant I know has a bar, and I don't go to bars."

"There's a Salvadoran place on Central Ave. My mother says it's not as good as hers but it's acceptable, which from my mother is a Michelin star."

"Tuesday."

"Tuesday."

Rinconcito was the size of a large bathroom. Six tables. A kitchen you could see from every seat. Walls covered in photographs of a country I'd never been to but could feel in the room. The smell hit you at the door: pupusas on the griddle, curtido fermenting, black beans simmering with an authority that said they'd been on the stove since before we arrived and would be there after we left.

Sofia was already there. Dark green blouse, jeans broken in properly, small silver hoops. Boots — ankle-height, leather, low heel, side zip. Her hair was down, which shouldn't have mattered but did. Hair up is the surgeon. Hair down is the person. I was meeting the person for the first time.

She'd ordered curtido and chips, which told me she was someone who takes charge.

Here's what dating at sixty-two is: an inventory. Here's what I have. Here's what's broken. Here's what still works. Take it or leave it, because the energy required to pretend is energy I no longer have. Two failed marriages teach you things about yourself you'd rather not know. Carol left because I was a drunk and a ghost. She was right to leave. Linda and I — sober by then, present — couldn't survive her own battle with pills. Leaving a woman who's drowning because you know you'll drown with her is the right decision and a decision that leaves a scar you carry forever.

So here I was, across from a woman in a green blouse, with two divorces and no interest in pretending.

"You look different without the scrubs," I said.

"You look the same."

"I only have three outfits."

"That's either honest or tragic."

"Both. Aren't they usually?"

She laughed. The real one. The belly one.

Her parents, Miguel and Rosa, left Chalatenango during the civil war. 1983. Sofia was born in Los Angeles in 1984. UC Davis for undergrad, Cornell for vet school, and she'd landed here because a small-animal surgical residency opened up and nobody else wanted to live in the East Bay in January.

She'd opened the clinic at thirty-two. Solo practice. Built from nothing — one exam room, one surgical suite, and a loan from a bank that almost didn't give it to her.

"How'd you convince them?"

"I brought my business plan. Twelve pages. Financial projections, market analysis, competitive landscape." She took a bite of pupusa. "And then I brought my mother."

"Your mother?"

"Rosa Reyes walked into that bank in her church dress and told the loan officer — in Spanish, which he didn't speak, while I translated — that her daughter had graduated top of her class and had never failed at anything, and that if the bank didn't give her the loan, my mother would find another bank and tell every woman in her church to move their accounts there."

"Did that work?"

"My mother's church has eleven hundred members. The loan officer did the math."

I laughed so hard the curtido almost came back up.

"Your mother sounds like someone I need to meet."

"She'll feed you until you can't move and then ask why you're not eating."

"My mother does the same thing."

"Latin mothers are not strategy. They're force of nature."

We talked for three hours. The restaurant emptied around us. The owner — Lupe, who knew Sofia by name and called her "doctora" — kept bringing food we hadn't ordered.

Sofia told me about the surgeries she loved and the ones that haunted her. The cat with the tumor she couldn't remove. The parrot she'd operated on for forty-five minutes to extract a swallowed bead, and that had bitten her so hard during recovery she still had the scar. She showed me. Left index finger. A half-moon of white tissue. I held her hand to look at it and she let me, and the holding lasted longer than the looking.

I told her about the drinking. Not the highlights — the details. The morning I couldn't hold a circular saw steady. The night Kevin found me on the kitchen floor and drove me to my first meeting.

She didn't flinch.

"Twenty-one years," she said.

"Twenty-one years, four months, eleven days."

"You count the days?"

"Every day. Not because I'm afraid of drinking. Because counting is how I stay honest."

She looked at me across the table. The restaurant was empty except for us and Lupe, who was pretending to clean.

"Roger. I haven't been on a date in four years."

"That's a long time."

"The last person I made time for didn't understand that my work isn't a job. It's who I am. I can't be with someone who needs me to be less of who I am."

"I've never asked anyone to be less of who they are."

"You barely know me."

"I know your hands. I know how you work. I know you perform surgery on parrots and translate for your mother and you were there at 9 PM on a Wednesday when my dog needed you." I paused. "I know enough."

Silence. Lupe had stopped pretending to clean.

"Tuesday," Sofia said.

"What about it?"

"Tuesday is my short day. I close the clinic at five. If you want to do this — whatever this is — Tuesdays are what I have."

"Then Tuesdays are what I'll take."

Every Tuesday for the next seven months — through the building of Ours, through the fights and the failures and the funding crises and the betrayals — Tuesday was the day that held me together.

Rinconcito or my apartment or walking the Bay Trail on the nights when the fog pulled back and you could see the Golden Gate lit up across the water. I'd tell her about the platform. She'd tell me about the surgeries. We'd trade the day's damage and somewhere in the trading, the weight got lighter.

Not every love story starts with fireworks. Some start with stitches, and pierogi, and a dog who ate a chicken bone.

Those are the ones that last.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 11

Five Hundred

Five hundred users.

I want you to understand what five hundred means in the context of social media. Instagram has two billion. TikTok has a billion and a half. Facebook has three billion. Five hundred is nothing. Five hundred is a high school. Five hundred is the number of people who attend a mid-sized church in a mid-sized town. Five hundred is invisible.

But five hundred real people, not bots, not test accounts, not duplicates, five hundred real human beings who have downloaded your app, created an account, and come back the next day and the day after that and the day after that — five hundred is a heartbeat. It's proof of life.

We hit five hundred three months after our fifty-user beta, and we did it the only way available to people with no money: one person at a time.

Jake built a referral system. Simple, elegant, impossible to game. Every user got a unique referral code. When someone signed up with your code, you both got a bonus — fifty cents to the referrer, twenty-five cents to the new user. Not a lot of money. But enough that our existing users texted friends, posted codes on their Instagram stories, mentioned Ours at dinner parties and in break rooms and at bus stops.

Word spread the way fire spreads through dry grass, not explosively, but steadily, following the paths of human connection. A user in Marcus's math class told her sister in Syracuse. The sister told her hairdresser. The hairdresser told her clients. Five became ten became thirty. Clusters of users appeared in Albany, then Troy, then Syracuse, then scattered across the East Bay like seeds blown from a single flower.

The demographics surprised me. I'd expected young people, Gen Z, digital natives, the TikTok generation. And we had some. But the fastest-growing segment was women between thirty-five and fifty-five. Working mothers. Nurses. Teachers. Administrative assistants. Women who spent time on their phones during lunch breaks and after the kids went to bed, who scrolled and liked and shared, who had never once thought of their screen time as labor until someone showed them a counter that proved it was.

Delores — Marcus's student, the home health aide — became our most engaged user. She spent twelve minutes a day in the Market, swiping Discovery Cards and watching Story Experiences with the methodical attention of someone who has learned that small amounts add up. Her first-month earnings: $8.47. She texted Marcus a screenshot.

"I made $8 doing what I already do on my phone. Why isn't everything like this?"

Marcus forwarded me the text. I read it six times. Then I printed it and put it on the wall.

The wall was getting full.

The problem with five hundred users was the same problem we'd had with fifty: the Market was mostly fake. We'd expanded from stock photos to a handful of real local businesses — Peet's Coffee on Solano, Pegasus Books on Shattuck, a dog groomer called Mudpuppy's that Sofia had recommended, but the ad inventory was thin. Users would swipe through the Discovery Cards in three or four minutes and hit the bottom. Nothing left to engage with. Nothing left to earn from.

We needed advertisers. Real ones. National ones. Brands with budgets that could fill the Market with content worth seeing and worth paying users for.

But national brands don't advertise on platforms with five hundred users. They advertise on platforms with five hundred million users. The gap between where we were and where we needed to be was so vast it had its own weather system.

Jake proposed a solution at 2 AM on a Wednesday, which was when Jake proposed most of his solutions, because Jake's brain operated on a schedule that had no relationship to the sun.

"Programmatic ads," he said. He was eating cereal from a mixing bowl — my mixing bowl, which I'd stopped fighting about. "We connect to existing ad networks. Google AdSense, Zeta Audience Network. They serve ads into our Market automatically. We take the revenue they'd normally give to the platform, and we split it with the user instead."

"We'd be using Zeta's ads to fund a platform that competes with Zeta," Marcus said.

"Yes."

Marcus smiled. The wolf smile. "I love it."

"Won't they cut us off once they figure out what we're doing?" I asked.

"Eventually. But by then we'll have enough users and enough engagement data to attract direct advertisers. We just need a bridge. Programmatic ads are the bridge."

Jake built it in a week. Connected to three ad networks. The Market suddenly had real ads — actual brands, actual products, actual content — served automatically, endlessly refreshing. Users no longer hit the bottom. They could scroll the Market for as long as they wanted, and every engagement paid them.

The effect was immediate. Average daily earnings jumped from thirty-four cents to fifty-one cents. Daily active usage jumped from eight sessions to eleven. The counter ticked faster. Users stayed longer. The flywheel started to turn.

And the data — the data was extraordinary.

Here's what nobody in advertising expected: willing engagement outperforms forced engagement by a factor of four.

On Instagram, the average user spends 1.7 seconds looking at an ad before scrolling past it. Most of that time is the fraction of a second it takes their thumb to register that they're seeing an ad and decide to skip it. The "engagement" that advertisers pay for is, in most cases, a flinch.

On Ours, the average Market engagement was twenty-two seconds. Twenty-two seconds of a person's genuine, voluntary, undivided attention. They'd chosen to enter the Market. They'd chosen to look at this ad. They were being paid to look, yes, but payment created investment — you don't skim something you're being compensated to examine. You actually look. You actually consider. You actually remember.

Click-through rates on programmatic ads in the Market: 4.7%. Industry average on Instagram: 0.9%. On Facebook: 1.1%.

Our ads performed four times better than the industry standard. Not because our users were different. Because our users were choosing.

Marcus built a deck around this data. Clean, devastating, irrefutable. He called it "The Willing Attention Thesis." The core argument: attention that is purchased, transparent, and consensual is exponentially more valuable than attention that is stolen, hidden, and manipulated. Advertisers who participated in the Ours Market weren't just doing the right thing — they were getting better results.

I understood this intuitively, from a career Marcus didn't have.

Open houses. I'd done hundreds of them — standing in staged living rooms for three hours on a Sunday afternoon while strangers walked through someone else's life and decided whether they wanted it. And the thing I'd learned, the thing that every good realtor knows and most advertising executives don't, is that people who walk through the door on purpose behave completely differently from people who are tricked through it.

The drive-by visitor — the person who saw the sign on the lawn and wandered in because they were bored or curious or killing time: that person looks at the kitchen countertops, opens a closet, checks the water pressure, and leaves in eleven minutes. They're browsing. They're not serious. You can dress the house in all the staging you want and they'll appreciate it the way they appreciate a museum — aesthetically, abstractly, without any intention to buy.

The intentional visitor — the person who saw the listing online, liked the photos, read the description, drove twenty minutes to get there, and walked in with a pre-approval letter in their purse: that person touches the walls. They stand in the master bedroom and imagine waking up there. They look out the kitchen window and picture their kids in the backyard. They're not browsing. They're building a future in their mind, and every feature of the house is material for that construction.

Same house. Same staging. Same open house. Completely different quality of attention.

The Ours Market was a staged open house. The users who entered it had chosen to be there. They weren't being tricked, or ambushed, or force-fed content between the posts they actually wanted to see. They'd walked through the door on purpose, with intention, knowing exactly what was inside. And that intention: that willingness — transformed the quality of their attention the way pre-approval transforms the quality of a buyer.

"This is the knife," Marcus said, showing me the deck. "This is how we cut them. Not morally. Economically. We don't need to convince advertisers that paying users is ethical. We need to show them it's profitable."

The Willing Attention Thesis. Remember that name. It's going to matter.

The article appeared on a Tuesday. Of course it was a Tuesday. The universe, apparently, had opinions about my schedule.

Sofia and I were at Rinconcito. Pupusas, horchata, the usual. My phone was face-down on the table because I'd promised Sofia, not explicitly, but through the established grammar of Tuesdays, that the phone stayed down during dinner. This was one of her rules. Not stated, not demanded, just understood. Tuesdays were analog. Tuesdays were two people in a restaurant being present.

The phone buzzed. And buzzed. And kept buzzing, in the way that a phone buzzes when multiple people are trying to reach you simultaneously for the same reason.

Sofia looked at the phone. Looked at me.

"Something's happening," she said.

I turned it over. Fourteen text messages. Six missed calls. Two from Marcus, two from Jake, one from Kevin, one from a number I didn't recognize.

Marcus's text was first:

TechCrunch just published a piece about us.

I opened the link. Sofia read it over my shoulder, her chin almost touching my arm, her hair smelling like the antiseptic she used at the clinic, which sounds unromantic but which I associated so specifically with her that it made my chest tight.

The article was titled: "OURS: The Social Platform That Pays Users Is Either Brilliant or Insane."

Someone had talked. Someone in our five hundred users, or someone connected to one of them — had tipped off a tech journalist named Amara Okafor at TechCrunch. The article was short but devastating in its specificity. It knew our revenue-sharing model. It knew about the Market. It knew about the counter. It knew about the 65/20/10/5 split. It quoted "a source familiar with the platform's beta" saying the engagement metrics were "unlike anything I've seen in fifteen years of covering social media."

"Is this good?" Sofia asked.

"I don't know."

"Your phone is still buzzing."

"I know."

"Are you going to answer it?"

I looked at her. Tuesday. Pupusas. Horchata. The one night a week that belonged to us and not to the platform called Ours.

"After dinner," I said.

She didn't smile. She did something better — she put her hand on mine, on the table, next to the phone that wouldn't stop buzzing, and she held it there while we finished eating. Her surgeon's hand on my carpenter's hand. Two sets of working fingers intertwined.

We ate. The phone buzzed. We finished.

I called Marcus from the parking lot.

"How bad is it?"

"It's not bad. It's complicated." His voice was controlled — the Marcus voice that means he's processing multiple scenarios simultaneously. "The article is accurate, which means someone inside our user base leaked to a journalist. That's a security problem. But the article is also positive, which means the journalist saw the data and concluded we're real. That's a credibility signal."

"What happens now?"

"Two things. First, our sign-up page is going to get hit. Amara's articles average fifty thousand readers in the first twenty-four hours. Even a 1% conversion rate is five hundred new users. We might double our user base overnight."

"Can the system handle that?"

"Jake's already on it. He's been scaling the infrastructure for the last hour. He says we can handle five thousand concurrent users."

"What's the second thing?"

Marcus paused. The Marcus pause. The one that means the next words carry weight.

"The second thing is that every major platform just learned we exist. Before this, we were invisible. A cease-and-desist from an unnamed firm. Five hundred users in the East Bay. Background noise. After this article, we're a signal. And signals get tracked."

I leaned against my truck. The parking lot was dark. Sofia was inside, settling the check that Lupe wouldn't let her pay.

"Marcus. Who leaked?"

"I don't know. But the article quotes someone 'familiar with the platform's beta.' That's not a random user. That's someone who understands the technical details. The revenue-sharing split. The Market mechanics. The engagement metrics."

"How many people know those details?"

"You. Me. Jake. And anyone Jake gave the full pitch to when recruiting."

We were quiet. The implication sat between us like a crack in a foundation — small now, but the kind that travels.

"I'll talk to Jake," I said.

"Be careful. If it's him, we need to know why. If it's not him, we need to know who."

"And if we can't find out?"

"Then we build the house anyway and hope the crack doesn't spread."

I drove Sofia home. She lived in a small house in El Cerrito — a suburb that's really just Albany pretending to be somewhere else — with a yard she didn't have time to maintain and a front porch she'd told me she sat on approximately twice a year. I walked her to the door because my mother raised me to walk women to their doors and because the twelve extra seconds of proximity were worth the cold.

"Exciting night," she said.

"I'm sorry about the phone."

"Don't be sorry. This is what you're building. I knew that when I sat down." She looked at me with the direct gaze that was so specifically Sofia — unguarded, assessing, kind. "Roger. Whatever's coming, and something is coming, I can feel it, don't let it change the thing you're building. The thing on the napkin. The 65 percent."

"I won't."

"Promise me."

"I promise."

She kissed me. First kiss. On her front porch in El Cerrito, in the cold, with my phone still buzzing in my pocket and the whole world about to find out that a carpenter from Albany was trying to take their money away. She kissed me and I kissed her and it lasted long enough for the porch light to click on — a motion sensor, not a judgment, and we pulled apart laughing.

"Tuesday," she said.

"Tuesday."

She went inside. I stood on the porch for a moment. The cold didn't matter. The phone didn't matter. The TechCrunch article and the unknown leaker and the trillion-dollar companies that were about to notice us, none of it mattered.

What mattered was Tuesday.

What mattered was the kiss.

What mattered was that in the middle of building something enormous, I had found something small and essential and mine.

I drove home. Opened the door. Biscuit was asleep on the couch. The whiteboards covered the walls. The napkin, the electric bill, the cease-and-desist, my wall of evidence,glowed in the dark.

My phone buzzed again. Jake:

Sign-ups are spiking. 200 new users in the last hour. Infrastructure holding. This is it, Roger.

I looked at the counter on my phone. Not the Ours counter — the sign-up counter Jake had rigged to update in real time. Two hundred. Two hundred and eight. Two hundred and fifteen.

Five hundred was becoming a thousand. A thousand would become five thousand. Five thousand would become the thing that couldn't be ignored.

I sat down at the kitchen table. Opened my laptop. The "e" key stuck, like it always did.

Time to build.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 12

Fifty Million Dollars

The call came on a Monday. Not a Tuesday — God wouldn't be that on the nose. A Monday. The most predictable day for unpredictable things.

I was sanding. Not code — actual sanding. My mother's kitchen table, the one she'd had since 1974 — solid maple, Heywood-Wakefield from the shape of the legs, the champagne finish they used in the mid-century pieces — had developed a wobble and a water stain that offended her on a spiritual level. I'd driven to her apartment in Arbor Hill, brought my tools, and was working through the grits, 120, then 180, then 220,while she stood in the kitchen doorway telling me I was doing it wrong, which is what mothers do when their sons fix things — supervise incorrectly, with authority.

"Más fino," she said. "The grain is too rough."

"Ma, I'm on 220. The grain is perfect."

"I don't have gray hair. I have silver hair. There is a difference."

"The difference is marketing."

"The difference is RESPECT."

My phone rang. Unknown number, 650 area code. Silicon Valley. I almost didn't answer — I'd been getting calls from journalists since the TechCrunch article, and I'd learned that talking to journalists was like playing poker with someone who could see your cards. But something — instinct, curiosity, something about a 650 number on a Monday morning — made me pick up.

"Mr. Grubb?" The voice was female. Professional. A voice trained to sound warm without being warm, the vocal equivalent of a luxury hotel lobby.

"Speaking."

"My name is Rachel Torres. I'm calling on behalf of Zeta Platforms. We'd like to schedule a conversation with you regarding your platform, Ours."

My mother was watching me. She couldn't hear the other end of the call, but she could read my face, because mothers can always read your face, even when you think you're not making one.

"¿Qué pasa?" she mouthed.

I held up a finger. "Regarding what, specifically?"

"A potential strategic partnership. We're impressed with what you've built and we'd like to explore how we might work together."

Work together. Sure. The way a nail and a hammer "work together."

"Who would I be meeting with?"

"David Park. Senior Vice President of Product Strategy."

I'd heard the name. After the TechCrunch article, I'd done what any sensible person does when trillion-dollar companies start paying attention to them: I researched the people who might be sent to deal with me. David Park was Zeta's fixer. MIT computer science, Stanford MBA, fourteen years at the company. He was the person they dispatched when something needed to be acquired, absorbed, or eliminated, and he was known for doing all three with the same polite smile.

"When?"

"Mr. Park can make himself available at your convenience. He's happy to come to you."

To me. A Senior Vice President of Zeta, worth, I'd looked it up — roughly $40 million in stock options alone, was offering to come to my apartment in Albany. This was either respect or reconnaissance, and I was pretty sure it wasn't respect.

"Next Thursday," I said. "Two o'clock. I'll send the address."

"Wonderful. Mr. Park looks forward to it."

The call ended. My mother was still watching me.

"Ma."

"Yes?"

"Facebook wants to buy my project."

She looked at me for a long time. My mother is eighty-six years old. She's four feet eleven inches tall and weighs approximately as much as Biscuit. She has survived the daughter-of-immigrants life, poverty, a husband who drank himself into the ground and then somehow clawed his way back out, a son who followed the same path before finding his own way back, and the quiet endurance of being a German-Russian woman in California who never quite lost the accent she inherited from her own mother. Nothing surprises her. But the thing she did next surprised me.

She sat down at the table I was sanding. Put her hands flat on the rough surface. And said, very quietly: "How much?"

"I don't know yet."

"Will it be enough to finish?"

"Finish what?"

"The thing you're building. Will it be enough to finish it?"

I looked at her. My mother, who didn't understand social media, who called everything "the Facebook," who had needed forty-five minutes to create an Ours account. She didn't know what an algorithm was. She didn't know what a CPM was. She didn't know what venture capital or revenue-sharing or programmatic ads meant.

But she knew her son was building something. And she knew that when people offer to buy what you're building, it means you're building something worth buying. And she knew the question that mattered wasn't how much they'd offer — it was whether the money would help him finish or stop him from finishing.

"No, Ma," I said. "It won't be enough to finish. It'll be enough to stop."

She nodded. Stood up. Went back to the kitchen. Over her shoulder: "Then don't sell."

Eighty-six years old. Four feet eleven. The clearest thinker in any room she enters.

David Park arrived in a black car — a Genesis G90, I clocked from the grille before it stopped, the $90,000 Korean luxury sedan that says "I know what a Mercedes costs and I think it's gauche." Not a limousine — something more subtle, the kind of car that costs as much as a limousine but doesn't announce it. He was alone, which surprised me. I'd expected a team — lawyers, strategists, the entourage of money. But he came alone, and when he knocked on my door, he was carrying two cups of Blue Bottle coffee — the paper cups with the minimalist logo, probably from the one on Webster Street.

"Mr. Grubb. David Park." He extended his hand. His grip was precise — firm enough to communicate confidence, brief enough to communicate efficiency. He was younger than I expected. Mid-forties. Korean American. Dressed in the Silicon Valley uniform — Outlier Slim Dungarees in dark navy, a Patagonia Better Sweater quarter-zip in forge grey, and on his feet the shoes that gave him away: Nike Killshots, the J.Crew collaboration, the shoe that every tech VP between San Jose and San Francisco wears because it straddles the line between casual and serious the way their entire lives do.

But it was the eyes I noticed. Not cold: that would have been too easy to read. Warm. Deliberately warm. The kind of warmth you see in people who've studied how warmth works and can produce it on command. The smile reached his eyes at exactly the right moment, held for exactly the right duration, and departed at exactly the right time. Everything about David Park was calibrated. And something in that calibration made the hair on the back of my neck stand up, the way it does when a foundation looks perfect but the grade is wrong.

"Come in."

He walked into my apartment and I watched him see it. The whiteboards. The folding table. The cables. The napkin on the wall. The framed cease-and-desist letter. The electric bill with Mia's words. The mixing bowl Jake had left in the sink.

His expression didn't change, and that's what bothered me. Not that he hid his reaction: that he didn't seem to have one. He looked at my apartment without empathy for any of it. Reading the space, understanding what was happening in it, mapping the work to the environment. But not feeling it. Not the way a person who builds things feels the evidence of building.

"You've been busy," he said.

"Carpenter's habit. Idle hands."

He sat where I gestured — the couch, next to Biscuit, who evaluated him with the thoroughness of a customs agent before deciding he was acceptable and placing his chin on David's thigh. David scratched Biscuit's ear without hesitation, which told me either that he liked dogs or that he'd been briefed on the presence of a dog and had practiced being comfortable with it. Biscuit usually knows. But Biscuit's judgment, I'd learn, wasn't infallible.

Marcus was there. Jake was there. I'd wanted them present because I'm not foolish enough to take a meeting with Zeta alone, and because Marcus could hear the math behind whatever offer was coming and Jake could hear the technical implications.

David looked at all three of us. Studied us, really — a quick, comprehensive assessment that sorted and filed us before we'd said a word. Then he said something I didn't expect.

"I think what you're building is right."

Silence. The whiteboards hummed. Biscuit's tail thumped once against the couch.

"I've been at Zeta for fourteen years," David continued. "I've watched us build the most sophisticated attention-capture system in human history. And I've watched the economics of that system concentrate wealth in ways that I personally find" — he paused, selecting the word with surgical care — "unsustainable."

"Unsustainable," Marcus repeated. Flat.

"The current model — extracting value from user attention without compensation — works financially. For now. But the social contract is fraying. Regulation is coming. Public sentiment is shifting. TikTok's ban, the congressional hearings, the youth mental health crisis — the window in which platforms can operate the way we operate is closing. Companies that don't adapt will be regulated into adaptation. I'd rather lead the change than be dragged into it."

Smooth. Measured. Perfectly pitched. The words of a man who had been thinking about this for a long time, or who had been briefed to sound like he had.

"So why are you here?" I asked.

"Because you've proven something we've theorized. Your engagement metrics in the Market — the 4.7% CTR, the twenty-two-second average view time — validate the Willing Attention Thesis. Voluntary, compensated engagement outperforms involuntary, uncompensated engagement. We know that now because you proved it. At five hundred users." He smiled. "Imagine what those numbers look like at five hundred million."

"I don't have to imagine. Marcus has modeled it."

"I'm sure he has. Mr. Webb, your financial models are impressive. We've seen the projections."

Marcus didn't ask how they'd seen the projections. Neither did I. We both knew. The same way the cease-and-desist had arrived — someone was watching, someone was listening, and the walls of a 740-square-foot apartment are not thick enough to contain a revolution.

"Here's what I'm authorized to offer," David said. He reached into his bag — a leather bag, the kind that ages beautifully because it was expensive to begin with, and produced a single sheet of paper. One page. He set it on the kitchen table.

I looked at it. The number was printed in the center of the page, in a font size that suggested it was meant to be read from a distance, which suggested it was meant to be impressive, which suggested it was.

$50,000,000.

Fifty million dollars.

For the full acquisition of Ours, the technology, the brand, the team, the user base, the Willing Attention Thesis, and all intellectual property associated with the platform.

Fifty million dollars. More money than I would earn in ten lifetimes of carpentry. More money than Marcus would earn in twenty lifetimes of teaching. More money than Jake's student debt multiplied by a hundred.

Fifty million dollars to stop.

The room was silent. Jake was staring at the number. I could see his jaw working, not speaking, just processing. A hundred and eighty thousand dollars in student debt, and the number on the table was fifty million. The math of that, the sheer, violent liberation that fifty million dollars would bring to a twenty-six-year-old drowning in debt — was visible on his face like sunlight through a window.

Marcus was still. The Marcus stillness that means every neuron is firing but nothing is reaching the surface. He was looking at the number but seeing past it, through it, to the structure underneath.

David waited. He was good at waiting. He'd placed the number on the table and let it sit there, confident that the amount itself would do the arguing.

"What happens to the users?" I asked.

"They'd be migrated to a Zeta product. We'd integrate Ours's payment model into Instagram or Facebook — a new feature, a new revenue stream, a new engagement mechanism."

"At sixty-five percent?"

"The revenue share would be determined during integration."

"What percentage?"

David paused. Half a second. "The final structure would reflect market realities and platform economics."

"What percentage, David?"

Another pause. "Likely in the range of five to ten percent."

Five to ten percent. Not sixty-five. Not the napkin. Not the promise. Five to ten percent, dressed up in "market realities and platform economics," which is corporate language for "we'll take what you built and make it ours in the other sense of the word."

"And the Market?"

"The Market concept would likely be integrated into existing ad infrastructure."

"So the ads go back in the feed."

"The ad placement strategy would be optimized across—"

"The ads go back in the feed."

Silence.

"Yes," David said. "Likely, the ads would be placed in the feed."

I looked at the napkin on the wall. The original napkin, the one from Peet's Coffee, the one I'd written on in forty-five seconds with a borrowed pen. 65% to users. The feed is sacred.

I looked at the cease-and-desist letter, framed next to it.

I looked at Mia's words on the electric bill: On TikTok I never made anything. This pays me.

I looked at Marcus. He gave me nothing. Not a nod, not a shake, not a signal. This was my decision. He'd made that clear before the meeting: "I'll be there for the math. The answer is yours."

I looked at Jake. Jake was looking at the number. Fifty million dollars. His lips were slightly parted. I could see the debt in his eyes — the weight of it, the years of it, the freedom that fifty million dollars represented. He was twenty-six. He had his whole life ahead of him. And the number on the table could remove every obstacle between him and that life.

He looked up. Met my eyes. And in his expression, I saw something I recognized — the look of a man standing at a crossroads, knowing that the easy path leads somewhere he doesn't want to go.

He didn't say anything. He didn't have to.

I looked at David Park. Senior Vice President. MIT. Stanford. Fourteen years at Zeta. The man they sent to make problems disappear.

"David."

"Yes?"

"I appreciate the offer. I appreciate you coming here. I appreciate the coffee." I picked up the sheet of paper. The fifty million dollars. The weight of it was nothing — a single sheet, a few grams, less than a napkin. "But this isn't for sale."

I handed it back.

David took the paper. His expression didn't change. Not surprise, not anger, not frustration. The absence of reaction was, itself, a reaction — the look of a man who had expected this answer and had another play already prepared.

"I understand," he said. "The offer stands. Indefinitely."

"It can stand as long as it wants. I'll be busy."

He stood. Shook my hand. Shook Marcus's hand. Shook Jake's hand, and held Jake's handshake a beat longer than the others, which I noticed and filed away. He scratched Biscuit's ear one more time.

At the door, he turned.

"Mr. Grubb. One more thing."

"Yeah?"

"You're right about the economics. Willing attention is more valuable. The user-compensation model works. The math is elegant. But being right isn't enough. Being right with a thousand users and no funding and a three-person team, that's not a competitive advantage. That's a vulnerability. We have thirty-seven thousand engineers. We have $120 billion in revenue. We have three billion users. You have a napkin on a wall."

He said it without malice. Without threat. With the calm, clinical precision of a man stating facts. Which made it worse.

"The napkin is enough," I said.

"We'll see." He smiled. Professional. Warm without being warm. "Enjoy the coffee."

The door closed. The car pulled away. The apartment was quiet except for Biscuit, who sneezed once and then fell asleep, because dogs have the emotional resilience that humans spend years in therapy trying to achieve.

Jake spoke first. "Roger. That was fifty million dollars."

"I know."

"Fifty. Million."

"I can read, Jake."

"You turned it down."

"I did."

He was quiet for a moment. Then: "Good."

Marcus was at the whiteboard, writing. He'd already moved past the offer, past the number, past the meeting. He was drawing a timeline. On one end: TODAY. On the other: where we needed to be. Between them, a series of milestones, each one a step further from the apartment and closer to the thing on the napkin.

"We need to move fast," he said. "David was right about one thing — being right with no resources is a vulnerability. We need users. We need money. We need both, and we need them now. Before they come back with something other than an offer."

"What would be other than an offer?"

Marcus turned from the whiteboard. The professor was gone. The Goldman quant was gone. What was left was the man underneath — the man who'd survived Wall Street and cocaine and the destruction of everything he'd built, and who knew exactly what powerful people do when polite offers are refused.

"War," Marcus said. "They'll bring war."

I looked at the napkin. At the cease-and-desist. At Mia's words. At the whiteboards covered in math and architecture and the accumulated evidence of three months of work by three people who had no business competing with the most powerful companies on earth.

Then I looked at the kitchen table. The same table where I'd eaten spaghetti alone for seven years. The same table where Marcus and I had scribbled on the back of an electric bill. The same table where Jake had built the first counter that ticked from zero to one cent. The table where everything had started.

I sat down. Opened my laptop. The "e" key stuck.

"Then we'd better be ready," I said.

End of Act One

↑ Back to Contents
ACT TWO: THE FIGHT
CHAPTER 13

The War Room

I want to tell you what happens when you say no to fifty million dollars.

Nothing. For about two weeks, absolutely nothing. The phone stops ringing. The journalists lose interest. The sign-up rate from the TechCrunch article tapers off to a trickle and then a drip and then the quiet that comes after any spike — the internet's attention span is roughly that of a golden retriever, which is to say enthusiastic, absolute, and brief.

We used those two weeks. Jake scaled the infrastructure. Marcus refined the financial model. I rebuilt the Market interface based on what our users, now hovering around 1,200,were actually doing, which was different from what I'd imagined they'd do, because users are not imaginary and they never behave the way you expect.

For example: I'd designed the Discovery Cards to be swiped left or right, like a dating app for products. Users ignored the left swipe entirely. They swiped right on everything. Every card. Every ad. Because swiping right meant engagement, and engagement meant money, and money meant the counter ticked up. They weren't evaluating the ads — they were harvesting them.

This was a problem. Advertisers were paying for genuine attention, and our users were treating the Market like a slot machine — pull the lever, collect the payout, repeat. The engagement metrics looked spectacular on the surface, but underneath, the quality of attention was degrading.

Jake saw it in the data first. "Average view time on Discovery Cards has dropped from twenty-two seconds to six seconds. Users are speed-swiping."

"Six seconds isn't terrible," I said.

"It's terrible if we promised advertisers twenty-two. We sold them on the Willing Attention Thesis — genuine, voluntary, sustained engagement. If users are gaming the system, we're no better than the platforms we're replacing. We're just paying for bad attention instead of stealing it."

He was right. And the solution — the solution that would define Ours and distinguish it from every platform that came before — came from Marcus.

"Attention verification," Marcus said. He was at the whiteboard, drawing something that looked like a flowchart had an argument with a maze. "We don't count a swipe as engagement. We count engagement as engagement."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning the counter only ticks when the user demonstrates genuine interaction. For a Discovery Card: minimum view time of ten seconds AND either a tap-through, a save, or a scroll to the details. For a Story Experience: completion of at least 70% of the video. For a Try-It Tool: actual use of the interactive feature. The payment isn't for seeing the ad. The payment is for engaging with the ad."

"Users will hate that," Jake said. "They'll feel like they're being tested."

"Good. Because they are. And the honest ones — the ones who actually look at the content, who actually consider the products — they'll earn more per interaction. We're rewarding quality attention, not quantity. Advertisers get what they're paying for. Users who engage genuinely earn more than users who game. The system self-selects for the behavior we want."

I stood at the whiteboard. Looked at Marcus's flowchart. Thought about it the way I think about a construction problem, not abstractly, but physically. Where does the weight go? Where does the stress concentrate? Where will it crack?

"It'll lose us users," I said. "The speed-swipers will leave."

"Let them leave. A thousand users who genuinely engage are worth more to advertisers than ten thousand who speed-swipe. This isn't a user-count game. This is a user-quality game. We're selling attention. The attention has to be real."

Jake implemented it in three days. The update rolled out on a Wednesday. By Thursday morning, we'd lost 180 users, 15% of our base. They signed up, saw the new engagement thresholds, realized they couldn't mindlessly harvest anymore, and left.

The remaining 1,020 users stayed. And their behavior changed dramatically. Average view time climbed back to nineteen seconds. Click-through rates hit 5.2%. The Market became what I'd always imagined — a place where people went on purpose, engaged with intention, and were compensated fairly for the real value of their real attention.

Average daily earnings dropped from fifty-one cents to thirty-eight cents. But, and this was the number that mattered, average earnings per interaction went UP. Each engagement paid more because each engagement was worth more. Users who took their time, who actually looked, who actually considered, earned sixty cents a day. Users who rushed earned twenty.

The system taught people to pay attention. And it paid them to learn.

That's what we were doing when the war started. Building. Refining. Getting better. A thousand people in the East Bay, earning thirty-eight cents a day, learning that their attention mattered. Small. Quiet. Honest.

The war didn't start with a bomb. It started with a paragraph.

I should have seen it coming. In real estate, there's a play called "the spoiler listing." When an agent has a client whose property threatens to steal all the attention in a market — a beautifully staged home at a competitive price in a desirable neighborhood — the competing agents don't just sit there. They rush their own listings to market. Not better listings. Just louder ones. Bigger photos, more aggressive pricing language, "COMING SOON" signs that go up the day after yours. The goal isn't to sell a better house. The goal is to dilute your attention. To make sure that when a buyer goes looking, they see ten options instead of one, and the signal gets lost in the noise.

Zeta was about to spoiler-list us. Not with a better product. With a louder announcement.

Bloomberg, Wednesday morning. Buried in a longer piece about Zeta's quarterly earnings, which had beaten analyst expectations by three cents per share, sending the stock up 4%:

"In a notable strategic shift, Zeta announced development of 'Zeta Rewards,' a new feature that will share a portion of advertising revenue directly with users. The feature, expected to launch in Q3, represents Zeta's response to growing regulatory pressure and consumer demand for transparent data compensation. Sources inside the company describe it as 'the most significant product evolution since the News Feed.'"

I read it three times. Marcus called before I finished the third reading.

"You saw it."

"I saw it."

"They're copying us."

"They're not copying us. They're building a weapon that looks like us."

Marcus was right. I knew it immediately, the way you know a board is warped before you measure it — something in the grain, something in the way it sits. Zeta wasn't building Ours. Zeta was building a thing that looked like Ours — the language was identical, the framing was identical, the promise of "revenue sharing" and "transparent data compensation" was identical, but I'd spent enough years watching people copy the appearance of quality without the substance to know what this was.

It was a facade. A Potemkin village. A model home with no plumbing.

"Call Jake," I said. "We need to figure out what they're actually building."

Jake found it in the developer documentation. Zeta had published early API specs for Zeta Rewards — standard practice for a new feature, to give third-party developers time to integrate. Jake read the specs the way I read blueprints: not for what they showed, but for what they revealed.

"The revenue share is real," he said. "Technically. Users will receive a portion of ad revenue generated by their engagement."

"How much?"

"It's not specified in the docs. But the payout structure tells you everything." He pulled up a diagram. "To qualify for Zeta Rewards, a user needs to meet minimum engagement thresholds — two hours of daily active use, minimum 50 interactions per session, enrollment in 'Zeta Verified' which requires government ID and a linked bank account."

"Two hours a day," Marcus said. "That's the minimum?"

"To earn anything at all. Below two hours, you get nothing. And the payout isn't per-interaction like ours. It's a monthly pool. Total Zeta Rewards budget divided by total qualifying users. So your individual payout depends on how many other people qualify."

"Which means as more users join, each user earns less," Marcus said.

"Exactly. And the total budget — the amount Zeta allocates to Rewards — is at their discretion. Not a percentage of revenue. A fixed budget."

I felt the cold anger. The building kind. "So they announce '50% revenue sharing' or whatever the headline will be. But the 50% is 50% of a pool they control, not 50% of actual revenue. And the pool gets diluted as more users join. And you need to spend two hours a day, two hours of your life,to qualify for anything at all."

"The math works out to roughly... hold on." Marcus had his calculator. His fingers moved. "Assuming Zeta allocates $5 billion annually, which would be generous, roughly 3% of ad revenue, and assuming 500 million users qualify... that's $10 per user per year. Eighty-three cents a month."

"Eighty-three cents a month for two hours of daily use," Jake said. "That's... four cents an hour."

"Less than prison labor," Marcus said quietly.

"And they'll announce it like they're saving the world," I said.

The announcement came two weeks later. Zeta's CEO himself, in a pre-recorded video posted to all Zeta platforms simultaneously, blue sweater, soft lighting, the rehearsed sincerity of a man who has practiced being human in front of a mirror.

"Today we're announcing Zeta Rewards, the most significant step forward in our commitment to sharing value with the people who make our platforms great. For the first time in the history of social media, users will receive a direct share of the advertising revenue their engagement generates. Because we believe that when people create value, they deserve to share in it."

He was quoting me. Not directly — he couldn't quote me, because I hadn't given any public statements. But the language, the framing, the moral architecture of the pitch was lifted directly from the napkin. "When people create value, they deserve to share in it." That was Ours. That was the mission. And he'd put it in his mouth like it was his own thought.

The internet erupted. Not with the nuanced analysis of what Zeta Rewards actually was, but with the broad, shallow enthusiasm of people who read headlines and not articles. "ZETA TO PAY USERS." "ZETA JOINS THE REVENUE-SHARING REVOLUTION." "IS THIS THE END OF FREE SOCIAL MEDIA?"

Our sign-ups dropped to zero. Not slowly — instantly. Why would you join a platform with 1,020 users that promised to pay you, when Facebook with three billion users was promising the same thing?

Jake watched the real-time dashboard. The sign-up line, which had been a modest but steady upward slope, flatlined. Then it began to drip downward. Existing users deactivating. Not many — twelve the first day, eight the second, but each one a person who'd decided that the giant's promise was more credible than the carpenter's.

"They're leaving," Jake said.

"I know."

"What do we do?"

I looked at the napkin. At the numbers. 65% to users. Not a pool. Not a budget. Sixty-five percent of actual revenue, paid per interaction, in real time.

"We tell the truth," I said. "We show the math."

Marcus built the comparison in forty-eight hours. A single page. Two columns. Clean as a blueprint.

OURS vs. ZETA REWARDS

Jake built a calculator into the Ours website — plug in your daily screen time, see what you'd earn on Ours versus Zeta Rewards. The math was devastating. At every level of usage, Ours paid more. Not a little more. Twenty to fifty times more.

I posted the comparison on every platform that would let me. Not with anger. Not with self-righteousness. Just the numbers. Two columns. The truth.

"Don't let the headline fool you," I wrote. "Zeta Rewards isn't revenue sharing. It's a press release designed to make you stop asking questions. Here are the numbers. You decide."

The post went nowhere for six hours. Then a journalist at The Verge — the same publication that had covered the TechCrunch piece — picked it up. Then Wired. Then The New York Times tech section. Then it was everywhere.

The headline that mattered, the one that changed everything,was in Wired:

"A Carpenter From Albany Just Exposed Zeta's Fake Revenue Sharing in One Chart"

The sign-up line unstuck. It didn't just resume its previous slope — it spiked. Hard. 500 new users the first day. 800 the second. 1,200 the third.

By the end of the week, Ours had 5,000 users.

Five thousand people who'd seen the math and chosen the carpenter over the corporation.

David Park, I learned later, was in a meeting when the Wired article hit. Someone showed it to him on a phone. He read it, set the phone face-down on the table, and said — according to three people who were in the room — six words:

"He's smarter than we thought."

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 14

Priya

The thing about being broke and under legal attack and trying to build a technology platform from your kitchen table is that it clarifies your priorities with the precision of a table saw. Everything non-essential falls away. Sleep becomes optional. Meals become fuel. Social life becomes the people in your apartment who are also trying not to drown.

And design — the thing that makes the difference between a product people tolerate and a product people love — becomes something you can't afford but can't live without.

I'd been building the interface myself. I'm proud of what I built, the same way I'm proud of a bookshelf I'd make from scrap lumber — it's functional, it holds books, it does the job. But it doesn't make your heart jump when you walk into the room. It doesn't make you reach out and touch it. It doesn't have the thing that separates a house from a home, which is not construction quality but emotional resonance. The feeling that someone cared about every detail, including the ones you'll never notice, because caring is the point.

Ours needed that. The counter ticked, the Market swiped, the feed scrolled — but it all looked like what it was: a platform built by a carpenter, a quant, and an engineer, none of whom had ever studied design, color theory, typography, or the alchemy that turns pixels into feelings.

We needed a designer. And like everything else about this project, we needed one who would work for free.

Priya Chandrasekaran found us. Not the other way around.

She sent an email — one of two hundred we'd received since the TechCrunch article, most of which were from journalists wanting quotes, investors wanting meetings, or people who wanted to tell us we were going to fail. Priya's email was different. It was four sentences long.

"I read about Ours. Your product economics are revolutionary. Your interface looks like it was designed by engineers. I can fix that."

I wrote back: "You're not wrong. What do you want?"

She wrote back: "Equity and purpose. I have enough of the second to work without the first, but the first would be nice eventually."

Priya was twenty-nine. Born in Chennai, raised in Edison, New Jersey, educated at RISD — Rhode Island School of Design, which Marcus informed me was "the MIT of design," and which Jake informed me was "where the people who make things beautiful go to learn how." She'd spent four years at a design agency in Brooklyn, creating interfaces for apps that, as she put it, "helped rich people order things faster." She'd quit six months before she found us, not because the work was bad but because the work was meaningless, and meaninglessness is a kind of poison that talented people metabolize slower than mediocre people, which means it hurts more.

She came to Albany on a Wednesday. Walked into my apartment, looked at the whiteboards, looked at the screens I'd built, and said nothing for seven minutes. I counted. Seven minutes of a small woman with enormous glasses walking from whiteboard to whiteboard, screen to screen, touching nothing, saying nothing, seeing everything.

Then she opened her laptop and started redesigning the entire interface.

"Don't take this personally," she said, not looking up.

"I'm trying not to."

"Your layouts are structurally sound. The information architecture is intuitive. The user flow makes sense." She paused. "But it has no soul. It looks like a banking app. And you're not building a banking app. You're building a revolution. Revolutions need to feel like revolutions."

Over the next seventy-two hours — three days in which she barely slept, eating only what I put in front of her, which was mostly pierogi and coffee because those were the two things I could produce in volume — Priya transformed Ours.

The color palette changed. My blues and grays — corporate, safe, forgettable — became warm ambers, deep greens, clean creams. Colors that felt like earth and wood and sunlight. Colors that said: this was made by humans, for humans.

I recognized what she was doing. Not from tech — from staging.

Fifteen years of preparing houses for sale had taught me that people don't buy properties. They buy feelings. They walk through a door and in the first seven seconds — seven seconds, the studies all agree — they decide how they feel about the space. Not whether the plumbing works or the foundation is solid or the roof will last. Whether they can imagine their life here. Whether the space feels like theirs.

Staging was the art of creating that feeling. You'd place a cashmere throw on a couch to say comfort. You'd put fresh flowers on a kitchen island to say life happens here. You'd light a candle that smelled like vanilla and cedar because those scents triggered memories of home in 73% of buyers — I had the data, I'd tracked it across hundreds of open houses. The house was the same house with or without the staging. But the feeling was entirely different. And the feeling was what sold.

Priya was staging an app. She was taking a functional space and making it feel like somewhere you wanted to be. The same principles applied: warmth over sterility, texture over flatness, humanity over machinery. She didn't know she was staging — she called it "emotional design" — but I knew. I'd done the same thing with throw pillows and strategic lighting for fifteen years.

"You're making it feel like a place people want to live," I said.

She looked at me with surprise. It was, I think, the first time anyone on the team had articulated what she was doing in terms she hadn't used herself.

"That's exactly right. Most apps feel like airports. Functional, efficient, designed to move you through. I want this to feel like a neighborhood coffee shop. You come because you want to, you stay because it feels good, and you leave planning to come back."

"I used to stage houses for a living," I said. "Different medium. Same principle. You're not designing features. You're designing the feeling of arriving somewhere that was made for you."

The surprise in her face softened into something else. Recognition. The same thing that had passed between me and Miguel, between me and Sofia's hands, between me and every craftsperson who discovered that someone else spoke their language.

"I'm going to like working with you," she said. "Most founders just want things to look 'clean' and 'modern.' You want them to feel like something."

"Feeling is the whole game. The counter can be technically perfect. If it doesn't make you feel paid, it doesn't work."

The typography changed. My system fonts — the defaults, the nothing-fonts that every app uses because they're free and they're fine — became carefully chosen pairs. A serif for headlines that carried weight and history. A clean sans-serif for body text that felt modern without feeling cold.

The counter changed. My counter had been a number in the corner. Priya made it a living thing. The digits were warm gold on a dark background. When the number incremented, it didn't just change — it pulsed. A subtle animation, barely visible, like a heartbeat. The number breathed. It was alive. It was your money, and it was growing, and Priya had made it feel like watching a plant push through soil.

"The counter is the soul of this app," she said. "Everything else exists to support it. The feed is where you live. The Market is where you earn. The counter is where you feel it."

She redesigned the Market. My flat grid of cards became a warm, magazine-like experience. Discovery Cards had rounded corners and subtle shadows that made them feel physical, like something you could pick up. Story Experiences had cinematic transitions. The whole Market felt like walking into a boutique — curated, beautiful, intentional.

She added what she called "moments of delight." When you cashed out, the counter didn't just reset — it released a spray of tiny golden particles that dispersed across the screen like dandelion seeds. When you referred a friend and they signed up, a small animation played: two hands clasping. When your daily earnings passed a dollar for the first time, a message appeared: "Your first dollar. Many more to come."

"Is the dandelion thing too much?" I asked.

"It's the exact right amount of too much. People need to feel rewarded. Not with dopamine — with warmth. The difference between manipulation and delight is whether the user knows you're doing it. We're not hiding anything. We're celebrating."

Jake integrated her designs in real time. He'd watch her create a screen in Figma, then build it in code before she'd finished the next one. They developed a rhythm — Priya designing at the small desk in my workshop on a 12.9-inch iPad Pro with an Apple Pencil, Jake coding at the folding table, both wearing AirPods Pro, communicating entirely through a shared screen and occasional pointing. They didn't speak for hours. Then one of them would say something like "the border radius on the card component should be twelve not eight" and the other would nod and they'd return to silence.

Marcus watched this from his corner and said to me, quietly: "We have a team now."

He was right. We did. Four people. A carpenter, a quant, an engineer, and a designer. Four people with combined savings of less than a used car, working from a 740-square-foot apartment, eating pierogi for dinner three nights a week, building something that a $900 billion company had just declared war on.

Four. Against thirty-seven thousand.

But ours looked better.

I took Priya's redesign to Sofia on a Tuesday. We were at Rinconcito — where else — and I handed her my phone the way you hand someone a newborn: carefully, proudly, slightly terrified of their reaction.

She opened Ours. Looked at the feed. Swiped into the Market. Watched the counter tick.

Her eyes went to the counter immediately. The golden numbers. The subtle pulse.

"This is different," she said.

"Priya redesigned everything."

"It feels different. Before, it felt... correct. Like a well-built house. Now it feels..." She searched for the word. "It feels like someone left the light on for you."

I almost cried. I'm not ashamed to say that. A veterinary surgeon in a Salvadoran restaurant holding my phone and saying it felt like someone left the light on — that was the thing. That was the design working. Not beautiful for the sake of beauty, but warm for the sake of welcome. The way a well-built house isn't just structurally sound but feels like home.

"That's exactly what it is," I said. "A light left on."

Sofia looked at me over the phone. "You're building something real, Roger."

"I know."

"No. I mean really real. Not an app. Not a platform. Something that people are going to need. The way they need a porch light when they come home in the dark."

She handed the phone back. Our fingers touched on the exchange. The charge of a touch that means more than the thing being handed.

"How's the legal situation?"

I told her about the patent trolls. The three letters. The shell companies tracing back to Zeta. The impossible math of defending against litigation with no money.

She listened. The way she always listened.

"My clinic got sued once," she said. "First year. A client's dog died during surgery — a twelve-year-old Labrador with a tumor the size of a grapefruit. The surgery was textbook. The dog was too sick. But the owner sued anyway. Said I was negligent."

"What happened?"

"I couldn't sleep for four months. I replayed the surgery every night. I checked my work, my notes, my decisions. I was certain I'd done everything right, and I was terrified I'd done something wrong. Both at the same time."

"How'd it end?"

"The case was dismissed. The vet board reviewed my records and found no negligence. The owner's lawyer withdrew. But the four months — those four months changed me. Because I learned that being right doesn't protect you. The system can be used against you even when you've done nothing wrong. The only defense is to be so clearly, undeniably right that the truth becomes louder than the accusation."

"Is that what we need to do?"

"That's what you're already doing. The napkin. The math. The counter. The engagement data. You're building evidence. Every user, every transaction, every metric — it's all evidence that your system works and theirs doesn't. They can file all the patent claims they want. The truth is on the wall of your apartment. You just need to make sure the world sees it before the lawyers bury it."

I looked at her across the table. This woman who'd built a clinic from one exam room. Who'd survived a lawsuit that kept her awake for four months. Who understood what it meant to be right and under attack simultaneously, because she'd lived it.

"I love you," I said.

It came out the way truth comes out when you're sixty-two and tired of pretending things don't exist — bluntly, inelegantly, in a Salvadoran restaurant over the remains of a pupusa.

Sofia didn't flinch. She didn't do the thing. She looked at me with her steady eyes and said: "I know. I've known for three Tuesdays."

"And?"

"And I love you too. But I need you to understand something."

"What?"

"I'm not going to be the woman who waits at home while you fight a war. If you're in this, I'm in this. Not as a spectator. As a partner. The same way I'd be in a surgery — hands in, eyes open, all the way."

"I don't want you in the crossfire."

"Then you don't understand me yet. I've been in crossfire my whole life. My parents crossed a border. I opened a clinic. I operate on animals that can bite my hand off. Crossfire is where I live."

She picked up her horchata and took a long sip, like we'd just agreed on what to have for dessert instead of pledging to face a trillion-dollar company together.

"Tuesday," she said.

"Tuesday," I said.

But it was already more than Tuesday. It was every day now. And both of us knew it.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 15

The Lawyer Who Worked for Free

His name was Theodore "Teddy" Vasquez, and he looked like a man who had been in a fight with the legal system for thirty years and had won most of the rounds but lost a few teeth.

Marcus found him. Of course Marcus found him — Marcus, whose network stretched from community college classrooms to Goldman Sachs trading floors to a half-dozen internet rabbit holes where people who fight patent trolls exchange battle stories. Marcus found a law review article from 2019 titled "Patent Assertion Entities and the Suppression of Innovation: A Case for Reform," read it in one sitting, and called the author.

Teddy Vasquez, partner at Vasquez & Lim, a two-person firm in Stamford, Connecticut, that specialized exclusively in defending small companies against patent trolls. His partner, June Lim, handled the research. Teddy handled the courtroom. Together, they'd taken on forty-seven patent troll cases in twelve years and won thirty-nine of them. Their fee structure was unusual: they charged standard rates for companies that could pay, reduced rates for companies that could partly pay, and nothing — pro bono, full stop — for cases they believed had the potential to set legal precedent.

"Tell me about the patents," Teddy said. He was on speakerphone. His voice sounded like gravel being polished — rough, warm, the voice of someone who'd spent decades talking in rooms where talking mattered. "I need the exact patent numbers and the specific claims of infringement."

I read him the letters. All three. He was quiet through the first two and laughed — actually laughed, a bark of recognition — during the third.

"I know this one," he said. "Patent 9,842,117. 'System and Method for Real-Time User Compensation in Digital Advertising Environments.' Filed in 2016 by an entity called Broadleaf Innovation Group. Broadleaf is a shell. I've seen them before. They file dozens of broadly worded patents, sit on them, and wait for someone to build something that vaguely resembles the patent description. Then they sue."

"Can they win?"

"The patent is garbage. It's so broadly written that it could apply to any digital advertising system that compensates users in any way. That includes loyalty programs, cashback apps, survey platforms. If this patent were enforceable, every company that gives users reward points would be infringing. But enforceability isn't the point."

"What's the point?"

"Cost. It costs them almost nothing to file a claim. It costs you two to five million to defend it. Most startups settle. They pay $200,000, $500,000, whatever the troll asks, because it's cheaper than going to court. The troll gets paid. The startup survives but weaker. The patent remains in force, ready to be weaponized again."

"So the system rewards the trolls."

"The system IS the troll. Patent trolls don't exist because the law allows them. They exist because the law was designed for a world where patents meant something — where they protected real inventions from real theft. The trolls exploited the gap between the law's intention and its implementation. It's like building a house with a great foundation but leaving the windows open. The foundation works. The burglars just come in through the windows."

I liked this man immediately. Anyone who explains the law through construction metaphors is speaking my language.

"What would it cost to fight all three?"

"If you retained a standard IP defense firm — Fenwick, Fish & Richardson, one of the big shops — you'd be looking at $3 to $5 million for all three cases combined. Eighteen to twenty-four months of litigation. Discovery, depositions, motions, counterclaims, possible trial. Even if you win, the legal fees would bankrupt you three times over."

"And if we retained you?"

Silence. The Teddy silence, which was different from the Marcus silence. Marcus went quiet when he was calculating. Teddy went quiet when he was deciding.

"If you retained me," he said, "it would cost you nothing. Because your case is the case I've been waiting for."

Teddy drove to Albany on a Saturday. He arrived in a car that was older than Jake — a 2002 Honda Accord with a rear bumper held on by what appeared to be optimism — and walked into my apartment carrying a banker's box full of documents and a sandwich he was actively eating.

He was fifty-eight. Puerto Rican — his family was from Ponce. Short, thick, with a mustache that belonged in a different decade and eyebrows that moved independently of each other like two caterpillars having separate conversations. He wore a suit that had been expensive once — Hart Schaffner Marx, I guessed from the lapel width and the canvas construction visible where the lining had separated at the cuff — a tie that had never been expensive, and shoes that told the truth about how much money he made fighting patent trolls for free: Johnston & Murphy, the Melton cap toe, resoled twice, polished with the discipline of a man who respects what he can't afford to replace.

He looked around my apartment. Saw the whiteboards. Saw the napkin. Saw the cease-and-desist. Saw the three new letters spread on the table like a losing hand of cards.

"Nice office," he said. Not sarcastically. Warmly. The way someone says it when they recognize that important things happen in small rooms.

He sat at the kitchen table — everyone sat at the kitchen table; it was the UN General Assembly of our operation — and opened the banker's box. Inside: printouts of the three patents, case law precedents, Federal Circuit rulings, inter partes review filings, and a handwritten legal strategy on a yellow legal pad that was, I realized with a start, his own version of our napkin.

"Here's what we're going to do," he said. He spread the legal pad on the table next to the napkin. Two plans. Two men. One table.

"We're not going to defend. We're going to attack."

Marcus leaned forward. Jake looked up from his laptop. Priya, who'd been working silently in the corner on a redesign of the Market's card layout, took off her headphones.

"The patents they're using against you are invalid. All three. They're broadly written, they have significant prior art, and they fail the Alice test — the Supreme Court standard that says you can't patent an abstract idea just by adding 'on a computer.' Compensating users for attention is not a novel invention. It's an economic concept. You can't patent 'paying people.'"

"But they filed the patents. They were granted."

"The patent office grants roughly 400,000 patents a year. Their examiners have an average of nineteen hours to evaluate each application. They don't catch everything. They don't catch most things. Getting a patent granted doesn't mean it's valid. It means nobody looked hard enough."

He pulled out three sheets of paper. Each one was a timeline.

"Patent one: Broadleaf Innovation. Filed 2016. Prior art: six existing systems that compensated users for ad engagement, dating back to 2011. This patent should never have been granted. We file an inter partes review with the Patent Trial and Appeal Board. The PTAB cancels the patent. Case dismissed."

"Patent two: Horizon Digital Holdings. Filed 2017. This one's cleverer — it covers 'real-time micropayment aggregation in social media environments.' But the claims are so broad that they'd cover any batched payment system, including PayPal and Venmo. We argue overbreadth. The court narrows the claims to the point of irrelevance."

"Patent three: NovaTech IP Solutions. Filed 2018. This is the one that made me laugh. The patent describes a 'user-facing earnings counter that updates in real-time based on advertising engagement.' Know what that is?"

"What?"

"A scoreboard. They patented a scoreboard. Every video game, every stock ticker, every sports broadcast has a real-time counter that updates based on events. This patent is as valid as patenting the concept of counting."

He leaned back. The mustache twitched. The eyebrows had separate opinions about what they'd heard.

"Three invalid patents. Three shell companies. One source. They're not trying to win in court. They're trying to win in your bank account. They want you to settle or fold because you can't afford to fight. What they didn't expect is a Puerto Rican patent lawyer from Stamford who hates bullies and works for free."

The room was quiet. Not the awkward quiet — the charged quiet. The quiet of people who've just been handed a weapon they didn't know existed.

"Teddy," I said. "Why free?"

He put the sandwich down. First time since he'd arrived that both his hands were empty. He looked at the napkin on my wall — really looked at it, the way people look at things that remind them of something they've been carrying for a long time.

"My father had a machine shop in Ponce. Small place — six employees, CNC lathe, precision parts for medical equipment. He invented a coupling mechanism for surgical instruments. Nothing fancy. A better joint. The kind of thing a machinist thinks of because he's been holding metal in his hands for thirty years and he knows how pieces should fit together." He glanced at my hands. "You know what I mean."

I knew exactly what he meant.

"He filed a patent. Spent $8,000 he didn't have. Got it granted. Started selling the coupling to a surgical supply company in New Jersey. Good money — not rich money, but food-on-the-table money. Feed-your-kids money. The kind of money that a man with a machine shop in Ponce dreams about."

Teddy's eyebrows — the two caterpillars — went still. That was the tell. When Teddy's eyebrows stopped moving, he was somewhere he didn't like being.

"Eighteen months in, he got a letter. A company in Delaware — an address, a letterhead, no employees, no products, no building you could visit — claimed his coupling infringed on their patent.
