The patent was filed six months after his. Six months after. But their lawyers were faster, their pockets were deeper, and the legal system doesn't care who was first. It cares who can afford to prove it."

"What happened?"

"He fought for two years. Mortgaged the shop. Borrowed from family. My mother worked doubles at the hospital. He won — eventually, technically, the patent was invalidated — but by the time the ruling came, the legal fees had eaten everything. The shop closed. The six employees lost their jobs. My father went to work in someone else's factory, making someone else's parts, and he never invented anything again. Not because he stopped having ideas. Because he learned what ideas cost when the system is rigged against the people who have them."

He picked up the sandwich. Took a bite. Chewed slowly, the way people chew when they're swallowing more than food.

"That's why free. Because my father couldn't afford a lawyer, and the people who stole from him could afford a hundred. Because every startup you destroy with a fake patent is someone's machine shop in Ponce. Because the system that was supposed to protect inventors is being used to destroy them, and somewhere there needs to be a man with bad shoes and a 2002 Honda who fights back."

Nobody spoke. Priya had taken off her headphones at some point during the story and was sitting very still, her enormous glasses reflecting the overhead light. Jake was looking at Teddy the way you look at someone you're seeing for the first time even though you've been in the same room for an hour. Marcus was doing the thing Marcus does when he's moved — absolute stillness, the poker face that isn't a poker face but a container for feeling too large to let out.

"Your father," Marcus said quietly. "What was his name?"

"Ernesto. Ernesto Vasquez. He died in 2019. He never saw me win a case." Teddy paused. "He also never saw me lose one I should have won. Which is the better legacy, I think."

He smoothed the yellow legal pad. The plan. His version of the napkin.

"Now. Shall we discuss strategy?"

Teddy filed three inter partes review petitions within the week. The filings were precise, devastating, and written in the careful legal prose that reads like a polite person calling someone a liar — technically respectful, substantively savage.

He also filed something else. A motion to identify the real parties in interest behind the three shell companies. This was the legal equivalent of pulling back a curtain — forcing Broadleaf, Horizon, and NovaTech to reveal who actually owned them, who funded the litigation, who was pulling the strings.

"They'll fight the disclosure," Teddy said. "They'll file motions to quash, claim trade secrets, invoke every procedural delay in the book. But the Federal Circuit has been getting tougher on anonymous patent trolls. The judges are tired of ghost companies clogging their dockets. We'll get the disclosure. And when we do..."

"Zeta."

"Zeta. And when the court record shows that the world's largest social media company is using shell entities to file invalid patents against a startup with four employees and a dog, the narrative changes. You go from defendant to victim. And victims, in the court of public opinion, are very difficult to destroy."

Marcus was grinning. Not the professor grin or the quant grin but the wolf grin — the one that meant he could see the board five moves ahead and the opponent couldn't.

"You're not just fighting the patents," Marcus said. "You're building a story."

Teddy pointed at him with the sandwich. "Now you're thinking like a lawyer."

"God forbid."

"Every legal battle is two battles. The one in the courtroom, which is about law. And the one in public, which is about narrative. The courtroom battle we'll win because the patents are invalid. The public battle we'll win because David is Goliath and you're a carpenter from Albany. The press will eat this alive."

I looked at the wall. The napkin. The cease-and-desist. The three patent letters. Mia's words on the electric bill.

"We're going to need a bigger wall," I said.

That night, after Teddy left and Marcus went home and Jake fell asleep on the couch and Priya disappeared into whatever quiet space designers retreat to when the day is done, I sat at the kitchen table with Biscuit at my feet and thought about something Teddy had said.

Every legal battle is two battles.

I'd known this before he said it. Not from law — from real estate.

Fifteen years of closing tables had taught me that every transaction is two transactions. The one on paper, which is about property and money and contractual obligations. And the one in the room, which is about power and fear and who blinks first. I'd watched sellers lie about foundation cracks and buyers fabricate pre-approval letters and agents on both sides smile through closing documents that neither party had fully read. I'd watched a widow get lowballed on her husband's house by an investor who'd researched the death notice in the obituary section and timed his offer to arrive during the funeral week. I'd watched a young couple lose their earnest money because a seller's agent found a higher bidder during the inspection period and manufactured a technicality to void the first contract.

The real estate industry was full of trolls. They didn't call themselves that — they called themselves investors, flippers, wholesalers, "market makers." But the mechanism was identical to what Teddy described: they exploited the gap between the system's intention and its implementation. Housing law was designed to facilitate fair exchanges between willing parties. The trolls used that framework to extract maximum value from parties who couldn't afford lawyers, couldn't understand contracts, and couldn't fight back because fighting cost more than surrendering.

Broadleaf Innovation Group. Horizon Digital Holdings. NovaTech IP Solutions. Three shell companies with impressive names and no employees and no products and no purpose except to weaponize a system designed to protect innovators against the innovators themselves. I'd seen this movie. I'd sold houses in this movie. The names were different and the stakes were higher but the script was the same: someone with more resources using the legal system as a profit center against someone with fewer resources.

The difference was that in real estate, I'd been a bystander. I'd watched the predation happen and done my job and gone home and told myself it wasn't my fight. I'd steered my clients away from the worst deals and explained the contracts they didn't understand and once — just once — I'd refused to represent a buyer who I knew was planning to evict elderly tenants from a rent-controlled building. But I hadn't fought the system. I'd worked within it. I'd taken my commission and moved on.

Not this time. This time the predators were coming for something I'd built. And this time I had a Puerto Rican lawyer from Stamford who hated bullies and a napkin on my wall and the cold anger that had been building since the night I watched my granddaughter work for free.

It occurred to me that everything about Ours was two things. The counter was money and it was dignity. The Market was advertising and it was choice. The feed was content and it was community. The napkin was a business model and it was a manifesto. The company was a startup and it was a movement.

And the fight — the fight was legal and it was moral. It was about patents and it was about power. About who gets to build things and who gets to stop them. About whether a carpenter with an idea can stand up to a company with an army and say: this is mine, and you can't have it.

I picked up my phone. Opened Ours. Looked at the counter.

$12.47. My total earnings since we'd launched the beta. Twelve dollars and forty-seven cents, accumulated one fraction of a penny at a time, from an app that four people had built in my kitchen.

It wasn't much. But it was mine. And I'd fight for it.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 16

The Money Arrives

JP Beaumont called at 6 AM on a Saturday. I know this because I was already awake — I've been waking at 5:30 since my carpentry days, and the habit lives in my bones the way sawdust lives in my truck — and because the phone displayed a country code I didn't recognize.

"Roger Grubb?" The voice was deep, accented — French layered over something else, a rhythm I couldn't place. It sounded like expensive cognac would sound if cognac could talk.

"Who's this?"

"My name is Jean-Pierre Beaumont. People call me JP. I read the Wired article. I read your comparison chart. I had my team verify the mathematics. They are correct. I would like to invest in your company."

I sat up in bed. Biscuit, who had been using my legs as a pillow, groaned at the disruption to his sleeping arrangement.

"How did you get this number?"

"I am a resourceful person, Mr. Grubb. Finding a phone number is the least interesting thing I do."

"It's 6 AM."

"It is noon in Paris. I apologize for the time difference. I do not apologize for the call."

JP Beaumont. The name sounded familiar in the way that expensive things sound familiar — you've heard of them without being able to afford them. I'd learn the full biography later, but what I gathered in that first call was this: French-Senegalese, seventy years old, made his fortune in telecommunications across West Africa, currently lived between Paris and Dakar, had a net worth that Forbes estimated at $2.3 billion and that JP said was "impolitely close to accurate."

"Why Ours?" I asked.

"Because I spent forty years building telecommunications infrastructure in countries where the infrastructure was built to extract, not to serve. The colonial model — build the network, charge the people, send the profits to Paris. I did it differently. I built networks that the communities could own. Not entirely — I am a businessman, not a saint — but substantially. Majority local ownership. Revenue that stayed in-country. It made me very rich, which was the point, but it also made two hundred million people connected, which was the other point."

"And you see a parallel."

"I see more than a parallel. I see the same fight. Your social media platforms are colonial infrastructure. They extract attention — the most valuable resource in the digital economy — from billions of people and send the profits to California. You are proposing to change the ownership model. This is not a technology project, Mr. Grubb. This is an independence movement."

At 6 AM on a Saturday, sitting in my bed in Albany with a pit bull on my legs, a seventy-year-old French-Senegalese billionaire was calling my social media platform an independence movement. The universe was getting creative.

"How much?" I asked.

"Five million dollars."

I nearly dropped the phone. Not because five million was more than fifty million — it wasn't. But because fifty million had come with chains. It had come with "full acquisition" and "integration into existing infrastructure" and the polite dismantling of everything on the napkin.

"For how much of the company?"

"Eight percent."

I did the math instantly. Five million for eight percent valued Ours at $62.5 million. More than Zeta's offer, but in valuation rather than cash. And I'd keep 92%.

"That's generous," I said.

"It is accurate. Your comparison chart generated approximately $40 million in earned media coverage in one week. Your user base grew 400% in seven days. Your engagement metrics, which I have had independently verified, are four to five times the industry standard. The Willing Attention Thesis is not theory anymore. It is data. And data at your stage, with your trajectory, is worth what I am offering."

"Why not more? You could ask for more."

"I could. But I have found, in forty years of investing, that the most profitable partnerships are the ones where the founder retains control. I do not want to run your company, Mr. Grubb. I want to fund it. There is a difference."

"Catherine Chen offered me two million for thirty-five percent."

"Catherine Chen is a predator who wears good shoes. She would have dismantled your vision within eighteen months and sold the pieces to the highest bidder. You were right to refuse her."

"How do you know I refused her?"

"Because you are still building. People who take predatory money stop building. They start managing. There is a difference."

I liked him. I liked him immediately, which made me suspicious, because I've learned that the people you like immediately are either genuine or very good at performing genuineness. But there was something in JP's voice — a weight, an authority, the weight of a man who has built things that matter and knows the cost — that felt earned rather than manufactured.

"I need to talk to my team."

"Of course. I will send the term sheet today. It is simple — three pages. I do not believe in complex legal documents. Complexity is where dishonesty hides."

"I like that."

"I stole it from my father. He was a fisherman in Saint-Louis. He said: a good net has wide knots. You can see through it."

The term sheet arrived by email at 8 AM. Three pages, as promised. Marcus read it in twenty minutes and said four words: "This is remarkably clean."

No board seats demanded. No veto rights on major decisions. No anti-dilution clauses that would let JP's eight percent grow at our expense. Just money — five million dollars — in exchange for eight percent of the company, with standard pro-rata rights to invest in future rounds.

"He's betting on us," Marcus said. "Not on control. On us."

"Is that real? Do investors actually do that?"

"Good ones do. Rare ones. The ones who've built their own things and understand that founders need room to work." He paused. "Roger, this isn't just money. This is validation from someone whose track record makes our thesis credible. When JP Beaumont backs something, the financial world pays attention. His name on our cap table is worth as much as his check."

Jake had a different concern. "Five million sounds like a lot. How long does it last?"

Marcus was ready for this. He pulled up a spreadsheet — the operating model he'd been refining for months. "At our current burn rate — which is essentially zero because none of us takes a salary — five million gives us eighteen months of runway with full engineering hires, infrastructure scaling, legal defense fund, and initial marketing."

"Legal defense fund?"

"The cease-and-desist was a warning shot. There will be more. We need a war chest."

"How much for legal?"

"Five hundred thousand."

"Half a million dollars for lawyers?"

"Half a million dollars for survival. The next attack won't be a letter. It'll be a lawsuit. And if we can't defend ourselves, it doesn't matter how good the product is."

I looked at the term sheet. Three pages. Five million dollars. Eight percent. A fisherman's philosophy about wide knots.

I called JP at 9 AM. Paris time, 3 PM. He answered on the first ring.

"Mr. Beaumont."

"JP."

"JP. We accept."

"Excellent. I will have the wire sent Monday. And Roger?"

"Yeah?"

"Stop calling me Mr. Beaumont. I am seventy years old. My friends call me JP. My enemies call me Mr. Beaumont. Choose carefully."

"JP it is."

"Good. Now go build something beautiful."

The money landed on Wednesday. Five million dollars, wired from a bank in Paris to a business account at Wells Fargo in Albany that Marcus had opened the previous Friday with a minimum deposit of $100. The teller, who processed the incoming wire, called Marcus three times to confirm it wasn't an error.

Five million dollars in a Wells Fargo account in Albany, California. Next to my personal savings of forty-seven thousand dollars. The juxtaposition was absurd enough that I laughed when Marcus told me, and then I stopped laughing because five million dollars is not funny. Five million dollars is terrifying. It's the difference between dreaming and doing, between theory and commitment, between a napkin on a wall and a company that exists in the world and can be killed.

I'd had money before. Not this money, but money. The tech options had cashed out at just over six figures — a small fortune to a carpenter, gone within two years to lawyers and a security deposit and the cost of starting over. The real estate commissions had been steady — good years brought in six figures, lean years brought in forty. The staging company had topped half a million in its best year, which sounds like a lot until you subtract the warehouse rent and the furniture inventory and the insurance and the employees and the part where you're driving a truck full of other people's couches across the Bay Bridge at 5 AM.

Money had always been transactional. I did something, money came. I stopped doing it, money stopped. It was wages with better clothes — a direct exchange of effort for payment, and the payment belonged to whoever wrote the check.

This was different. This was someone betting five million dollars that the thing inside my head was real. Jake's code was better than mine would ever be. Marcus's math was bulletproof. The thing. The vision. The conviction that attention has value and people deserve to be paid. JP wasn't buying labor or product or services. He was buying a belief. And the terrifying part was that the belief was mine, which meant if it failed, the failure would be personal in a way that no commission check or vested option had ever been.

In real estate, when a deal falls through, you lose a commission. When a startup fails, you lose someone else's five million dollars and the part of yourself that promised it would work.

I made a decision that morning that I didn't tell anyone about for weeks. I pulled every dollar I had from my real estate investments — three rental properties in Jacksonville that had been quietly paying me $2,800 a month for the past seven years. I sold them. Below market, fast, to a cash buyer. I netted $340,000 and put it in the Ours operating account.

When Marcus found out, he was furious. "You sold your safety net? Roger, that's—"

"Skin in the game. JP put in five million of his money. I'm putting in everything of mine."

"That's not financially rational."

"Neither is building a social media platform from your kitchen table. We passed rational a long time ago."

Marcus stared at me for a long time. Then he nodded, slowly, and added the $340,000 to the operating model without another word. He understood. When you ask other people to bet on you, you'd better be betting on yourself.

We hired. Carefully, slowly, the way I'd hire subcontractors on a construction site — looking at their work before looking at their resume, watching how they think before asking what they know.

Priya was already running the design side like she'd been there since the napkin. Her Market redesign — the warm earth tones, the carved counter, the interface that felt like a place instead of a tool — had become the face of Ours. Users screenshotted that counter. Posted it on other platforms. Made it their wallpaper. A number, ticking upward, looking like it was carved from the earth. Priya had given the app its soul. Now, with real money behind us, she could give it a body.

We hired two more engineers — both from Jake's network, both willing to take below-market salaries in exchange for equity and the chance to build something that wasn't designed to manipulate people. We hired a community manager named Dev who'd worked at Reddit and was tired of what he called "the toxicity treadmill." We rented a small office — two rooms above Tony's Barbershop on Solano Avenue, $1,400 a month, first and last — because my apartment, despite its many qualities, could not physically accommodate eight people and a dog.

The office smelled like Barbicide and Clubman Pinaud aftershave from Tony's below, which was an upgrade from the smell of my apartment, which was spaghetti and Biscuit. We bought four IKEA Bekant desks — the sit-stand ones, $549 each, which Marcus negotiated down to $479 by buying the floor models — and a secondhand Herman Miller Aeron chair that Jake found on Craigslist for $380 and treated like a religious artifact. We put the napkin on the wall. We put the cease-and-desist on the wall. We put Mia's words on the wall. The wall of evidence traveled with us.

JP called once a week. Never to check on the money. Never to ask about metrics or milestones. He called to listen. To hear what we were building, how we were thinking, what problems we were solving. He'd offer occasional advice — always framed as suggestion, never as instruction — and then he'd say the same thing at the end of every call:

"Build something beautiful."

We were trying. God, we were trying.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 17

The Carpenter Testifies

The subpoena arrived by certified mail on a Thursday. I signed for it the way you sign for anything that arrives by certified mail — with the vague dread of a person who knows that good news doesn't require a signature.

United States Senate. Committee on Commerce, Science, and Transportation. Subcommittee on Consumer Protection, Product Safety, and Data Security.

"You are hereby commanded to appear and testify..."

I called Marcus.

"I got subpoenaed."

"By whom?"

"The Senate."

Silence. Then: "Which committee?"

"Commerce. Consumer protection subcommittee. Something about 'the economics of digital attention and user compensation models.'"

"That's Senator Whitfield's committee."

"Is that good or bad?"

"It's both. Whitfield has been pushing for user-data compensation legislation for two years. He wants to regulate platforms into paying users. Your existence proves it's possible. He's going to use you as Exhibit A."

"And the platforms?"

"Will use the hearing to argue it's impossible. That you're a novelty. That it can't scale. That attempting to mandate user compensation would destroy the internet as we know it."

"Would it?"

"No. But they'll say it with very expensive PowerPoints."

I have never been to Washington, D.C. This is not because I lack curiosity but because Washington has always felt like a place for people who talk about building things, not people who actually build them. The distinction matters to me. There are people who design houses and people who frame them, and while both are necessary, only one has splinters.

I flew for the first time in eleven years. The last time was Kevin's college graduation in Boston, and I'd driven back because the flight made me feel like a piece of cargo with opinions. This time, Marcus insisted. "You cannot drive six hours to testify before the United States Senate and show up smelling like truck."

"I don't smell like truck."

"Roger. You smell like truck."

Jake drove me to the airport in his Honda Civic — a 2016 LX, I'd clocked from the alloy wheels and the chrome strip the first time he parked it outside my apartment. 140,000 miles, a Stanford parking sticker still on the windshield like a ghost from another life. In the car, he was quiet — the wrong kind of quiet. Not the focused silence of a coder processing a problem, but the compressed silence of a person holding something in. I'd noticed it for weeks. Small things. Jake arriving late. Jake leaving early. Jake on his phone in the hallway — an iPhone 13 in a case so thin it was basically decorative — speaking in low tones that stopped when anyone approached.

"You okay?" I asked.

"Fine. Nervous for you."

"Don't be nervous for me. I've testified before."

"Before what?"

"Before a building inspector who thought my load calculations were wrong. Which they weren't. Senators can't be worse than building inspectors. Building inspectors have tape measures."

He smiled. But the smile didn't reach his eyes, and the silence that followed had a texture I recognized — the texture of a person carrying weight they can't put down. I knew that texture. I'd carried it for fifteen years, until the weight broke me and I put it down in a church basement in Albany with a cup of bad coffee and the words "my name is Roger and I'm an alcoholic."

I filed it away. Not forgotten. Filed.

The hearing room was smaller than I expected. Television makes everything look bigger — the camera adds ten feet to a room the way it adds ten pounds to a face. The actual room was paneled in dark wood, which I appreciated because I'm a carpenter and dark wood is serious wood. Oak, stained walnut. Someone had built this room to convey weight, and they'd done it right.

The senators sat in a curved row behind a raised platform — the kind of arrangement designed to make the person testifying feel small, which it did, but I've spent my life looking up at things I was about to build and feeling small hasn't stopped me from building yet.

Senator Whitfield was in the center. Tall, silver-haired, a face made for C-SPAN. He had the practiced gravity of a man who knows he's being filmed and has opinions about his angles. To his left, Senator Margolis — younger, sharper, known for being hostile to tech regulation. To his right, Senator Park-Williams — no relation to David Park, though the name coincidence made me uneasy — who was known for asking the questions nobody else would ask.

There were other witnesses. A Zeta executive — not David Park, someone more polished, more practiced at saying nothing in complete sentences. A Google representative. A professor from MIT who studied digital economics. And me.

The Zeta executive went first. His name was Thomas Brennan, and he spoke for twenty minutes about Zeta Rewards with the fluency of someone who had rehearsed every syllable. Revenue sharing. User empowerment. Innovation. Responsibility. He used the word "ecosystem" eleven times. He used the word "community" nine times. He used the word "value" fourteen times. He never once said a specific number.

Senator Whitfield asked: "Mr. Brennan, what percentage of Zeta's advertising revenue is allocated to Zeta Rewards?"

"Senator, the Zeta Rewards program is designed to scale with user participation, and the budget reflects our commitment to meaningful—"

"What percentage?"

"The precise allocation is determined by a proprietary formula that accounts for—"

"Mr. Brennan. A number. What percentage of Zeta's ad revenue goes to users through Zeta Rewards?"

A pause. The kind of pause where you can hear a lawyer's advice being remembered and ignored simultaneously.

"Approximately three percent."

The room shifted. Three percent. After all the press releases, all the Zeta promo videos, all the headlines about "the revenue-sharing revolution" — three percent.

Senator Park-Williams leaned into her microphone. "Three percent. And your company generated $132 billion in ad revenue last year. Three percent of that is approximately $4 billion, divided among — how many qualifying users?"

"Approximately 480 million."

"So roughly eight dollars per user per year."

"Senator, the program is designed to grow—"

"Eight dollars. Per year. For a program that requires two hours of daily active use to qualify. That's roughly four cents per hour of a user's time." She removed her glasses. "Mr. Brennan, the federal minimum wage is $7.25 per hour. You're paying four cents. Is that what Zeta means by 'sharing value'?"

I could hear Marcus in my earpiece — he'd insisted I wear one, connected to a phone in his pocket in the gallery — whispering: "Four cents an hour. Write that down."

I was already writing it. On a legal pad, in pencil, the way I'd take notes on a job site. Four cents an hour. The number that would matter.

My turn.

I walked to the witness table. Sat down. Adjusted the microphone, which was too high because the person before me was taller and I am five-foot-ten, which is average, which is what I am — average in every way that matters except for the thing I was about to say.

"Mr. Grubb, please state your name and occupation for the record."

"Roger Grubb. Carpenter. Founder of Ours."

"Mr. Grubb, you are not a technology executive."

"No, sir."

"You have no background in computer science, venture capital, or digital media."

"No, sir."

That was the answer for the record. The answer in my head was longer.

I'd spent ten years building construction management software — enterprise systems that coordinated how buildings got built across budgets, timelines, and regulatory frameworks. I'd vested stock options in a technology company and walked away understanding more about how tech creates and captures value than most people who call themselves "in tech." I'd spent fifteen years in real estate learning to read human behavior under financial pressure — what people do when the money is real and the stakes are personal and the decision is the biggest one they'll make in a decade. I'd built a staging company that turned emotional psychology into revenue and an investment company that turned market insight into returns.

But none of that was what he was asking. He was asking whether I had a Stanford degree and a Sand Hill Road pedigree and the kind of résumé that made venture capitalists feel safe. The answer to that question was no. And the answer to that question was the point.

"You taught yourself to code at the age of fifty-five."

"From YouTube, sir. Mostly."

A small ripple through the room. Not quite laughter. The recognition of something unexpected.

"Mr. Grubb, in your own words, can you explain what Ours is and how it works?"

I'd prepared a statement. Marcus had written it — polished, precise, full of the language that congressional committees expect. I had it in front of me, printed on paper, double-spaced, ready to read.

I didn't read it.

"Senator, I'm going to skip the prepared statement, if that's all right."

Marcus in my ear: "Roger. What are you doing."

"My granddaughter is twelve years old. Her name is Mia. She spends about two hours a day on TikTok. She makes videos. She watches videos. She comments, she shares, she likes. In those two hours, she generates data — about her preferences, her behavior, her attention, her interests — that TikTok sells to advertisers. Last year, TikTok made $23 billion in advertising revenue. Mia made zero."

The room was quiet.

"That's not complicated. You don't need a degree from MIT to understand it. A twelve-year-old girl is doing work — real work, generating real value — and she's being paid nothing. If she were picking strawberries for free, we'd call it child labor. She's picking data. We call it social media."

I looked at the senators. Some were writing. Some were watching. Senator Margolis, the tech-regulation skeptic, had her arms crossed.

"Ours is simple. We pay people for their attention. Sixty-five percent of every dollar an advertiser spends in our Market goes directly to the user whose attention generated that revenue. Not three percent. Not a pool. Not a discretionary budget that can be cut when shareholders get nervous. Sixty-five percent. Contractual. Automatic. Transparent."

"Mr. Grubb, Mr. Brennan testified that Zeta Rewards allocates approximately three percent—"

"Three percent is a tip, Senator. Three percent is what you leave when the service was bad. I'm not proposing tips. I'm proposing fair compensation for work performed."

Senator Margolis uncrossed her arms. "Mr. Grubb, with respect, you have approximately five thousand users. Zeta has three billion. How can you claim your model works at scale when you've never operated at scale?"

"Senator, in 1903, the Wright Brothers flew 120 feet. A skeptic could have said: 'That won't work at scale. A plane needs to fly thousands of miles, not 120 feet. You have no evidence this scales.' The skeptic would have been right about the evidence and wrong about the future. We have five thousand users. Our engagement metrics are four to five times the industry standard. Our click-through rates are five times Facebook's. Our users check the app eleven times a day, not because we manipulate them, but because we pay them. The economics work at every scale we've tested. We haven't tested at three billion. But the math doesn't change because the number gets bigger."

"The infrastructure requirements do."

"Yes, ma'am. That's an engineering problem. And engineering problems have engineering solutions. The question before this committee isn't whether it's technically possible to pay users — we've proven it is. The question is whether the people who profit from NOT paying users will be allowed to continue."

I could hear Marcus breathing in my ear. He wasn't whispering anymore. He was just listening.

Senator Whitfield leaned forward. "Mr. Grubb, one final question. Zeta offered to acquire your platform for fifty million dollars. You refused. Why?"

I hadn't planned to discuss this. The offer was private — or supposed to be. But someone had leaked it, the way everything gets leaked in Washington, and now it was on the record.

"Because fifty million dollars buys a company. It doesn't build a movement. I'm sixty-two years old, Senator. I've built a lot of things. Houses, tables, decks, cabinets. Fifty million would be more money than I'll ever need. But the thing I'm building isn't for me. It's for Mia. It's for every person who's ever been told their time has no value while someone else gets rich from it. You can't buy that. You can only build it."

The room was quiet. Not the quiet of boredom — the quiet of people deciding what they think.

Senator Whitfield nodded. "Thank you, Mr. Grubb."

I stood up. Walked back to my seat. Marcus was in the gallery. He wasn't doing the poker face. He wasn't doing the professor. He was just sitting there, looking at me, and — I swear this, and Marcus would deny it to his grave — his eyes were wet.

"Four cents an hour," he whispered when I sat down. "You just put that in the Congressional Record."

"I put a lot of things in the Congressional Record."

"Yes, you did." He paused. "You beautiful, stubborn, impossible carpenter."

The hearing was on C-SPAN. Nobody watches C-SPAN. But they watch clips. And the clip — those ninety seconds where a carpenter from Albany compared data harvesting to child labor and called three percent a bad tip — went everywhere. Not because it was polished. Because it was real.

By the end of the week, Ours had 25,000 users.

By the end of the month, we had 50,000.

The war was on. And the carpenter was winning.

For now.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 18

Zeta Rewards

# Chapter 18: Zeta Rewards

They launched it on a Wednesday. Because of course they did — Wednesday is the optimal day for tech product announcements. Monday is too early, people are still clearing their inboxes. Friday is too late, people have already checked out. Wednesday catches the maximum number of eyeballs at peak attention. I know this because Jake told me, and Jake knows this because Jake knows everything about how attention works, which is either his greatest skill or his curse, depending on the day.

Zeta Rewards. That's what they called it.

I was at Ace Hardware on Solano when Kevin texted me the link. I was buying wood glue — Titebond III, the waterproof one with the green label, not for the company, for my mother's table, which I was still refinishing because I am a man who finishes things even when the world is catching fire around him — and my phone buzzed with a link to The Verge.

"ZETA LAUNCHES ZETA REWARDS: USERS CAN NOW EARN MONEY FROM THEIR SOCIAL MEDIA ACTIVITY"

I stood in aisle seven of Lowe's, between the wood glue and the sandpaper, and read the article three times.

Zeta Rewards was, on its surface, exactly what Ours did. Users could earn money from their activity on Instagram and Facebook. A percentage of advertising revenue would be "shared back" with users based on their engagement. A counter — they actually built a counter — would display earnings in real-time. Users could cash out to PayPal, Venmo, or direct deposit.

The press release used the word "revolutionary" four times. It used the word "pioneering" three times. It used the phrase "putting users first" so often that I lost count. Nowhere in the press release did it mention Ours. Nowhere did it acknowledge that the concept it was "pioneering" had been built, tested, and proven in a 740-square-foot apartment in Albany by a carpenter and his team.

But the details — the details told a different story.

"Five percent," Marcus said.

He was at the table. He'd been there since I'd called him from the Ace Hardware parking lot, which was forty minutes ago, and in that time he'd read every article, every press release, every FAQ, every line of fine print associated with Zeta Rewards.

"Five percent. That's the revenue share. Not sixty-five. Not fifty. Not twenty. Five."

"Of what?"

"Of the advertising revenue 'attributable to the user's engagement.'" He held up air quotes. "Which is a meaningless phrase. They get to define what 'attributable' means. They get to define what 'engagement' means. They control every variable in the equation. A user generates a hundred dollars in ad revenue, Zeta keeps ninety-five, and the user gets five. Maybe. If the algorithm decides their engagement qualifies."

"How much would the average user earn?"

"I ran the numbers based on Instagram's published ad revenue per user. At 5% revenue share with Zeta's definition of qualifying engagement, the average US user would earn approximately..." He looked at his phone. "Three cents a day."

"Three cents."

"Three cents. Per day. Roughly ninety cents a month. Eleven dollars a year."

Jake was pacing. Jake paced when he was angry, which was different from his normal movement, which was a kind of caffeinated vibration. Pacing Jake meant his brain was producing thoughts faster than his mouth could release them.

"It's not a product," Jake said. "It's a vaccine. They're inoculating the market against us. The headline says 'Zeta pays users.' Every journalist writes it up. Every user thinks, 'Oh, Instagram pays me now, cool.' Nobody reads the fine print. Nobody calculates the actual number. They've taken our idea, gutted it, put a Zeta logo on the corpse, and waved it at the world like it's alive."

"It's alive enough to kill us," Priya said quietly. She was on the couch with her laptop, scrolling through social media reactions. "Twitter is trending #ZetaRewards. Instagram influencers are posting about it. TikTok — TikTok — has videos celebrating it. 'Instagram finally pays creators!' 'Get paid to scroll!' People are excited."

"They're excited about three cents a day," I said.

"They're excited about the concept. They don't know the number yet. By the time they figure out the number, the narrative will be set. Zeta Rewards will be the 'user payment platform,' and Ours will be the thing that's 'just like Zeta Rewards but smaller.'"

She was right. And she was saying what I'd been thinking since the Lowe's parking lot: David Park hadn't just launched a competing feature. He'd stolen our narrative. He'd taken the idea — the very idea that made Ours revolutionary — and pre-empted it. First-mover advantage, in reverse. We'd built it first, but they'd announced it louder. In the attention economy, volume wins.

I called Sofia.

"Have you seen it?"

"I'm looking at it right now." Her voice was the steady one — the surgeon's voice, the one she used when the situation was serious and panic would help no one. "It says fifty percent revenue share on the main page."

"What?"

"The headline on Zeta Rewards. 'Up to 50% of revenue shared with our community.'"

I pulled up the page on my phone. There it was. In enormous text, across the top of the Zeta Rewards landing page:

UP TO 50% OF AD REVENUE SHARED WITH OUR COMMUNITY

My chest went tight. Fifty percent. They were advertising FIFTY percent. That was close to our sixty-five. That was competitive. That was—

"Read the footnote," Sofia said.

I scrolled down. Beneath the fold, in text so small it was practically a whisper:

"50% figure represents maximum potential community share across all qualifying engagement categories combined. Individual user share is calculated based on proprietary engagement metrics, content quality scoring, and community impact assessment. Average individual user share may vary. See terms of service for complete details."

"Average individual user share may vary," I read aloud.

"May vary from what?"

"From fifty percent. Down. Way down. To three or five percent, according to Marcus. The fifty percent is a ceiling that almost nobody hits. It's a theoretical maximum, not a promise."

"That's fraud."

"That's marketing. There's a legal difference, apparently."

But Sofia was right in spirit if not in law. I'd seen this exact play in real estate, dozens of times. "Starting at $399,000!" the signs would say, and when you walked in, the $399,000 unit was a studio facing the dumpster with no parking. The units people actually wanted started at $625,000. The number on the sign existed to get you through the door. It had a relationship to reality the way a fishing lure has a relationship to an insect — it resembles the real thing closely enough to deceive, and deception is its entire purpose.

I'd fought this in my realtor years. I'd argued with brokers who wanted to advertise "ocean views" for properties that could see a one-inch sliver of bay if you stood on a chair in the bathroom. I'd refused to list a house as "recently renovated" when the renovation consisted of painting over water damage. I'd lost listings over it. Lost commissions. Lost standing with agents who thought I was naive or sanctimonious for insisting that the words on the sign should mean what the words on the sign said.

"Up to 50%" was "ocean views from the bathroom." It was technically defensible and practically dishonest, and the people who wrote it knew the difference and chose the dishonesty because the dishonesty worked better.

"We need to show people the real number," I said.

"From fifty percent. Down to—"

"Down to whatever they want. The fifty percent is a collective number. They're saying fifty percent of revenue goes to ALL users combined. Each individual user gets their fraction of that fifty percent, divided by three billion users, filtered through an algorithm they control. The fifty is a lie wrapped in math."

I sat down. The wood glue was still in the bag on the counter. The world outside my window was Albany on a Tuesday afternoon — fog, quiet, indifferent.

"It's brilliant," I said. "I hate it, but it's brilliant."

"It's not brilliant. It's dishonest. And dishonesty has a half-life."

"How long?"

"Long enough to hurt you. Short enough to destroy them. But only if you expose it."

Marcus did the math. Because Marcus always does the math, and the math is always the weapon.

He built a comparison chart. Not a slide deck, not a presentation — a single page. Clear, devastating, impossible to misread.

ZETA REWARDS vs. OURS: THE REAL NUMBERS

"Three cents versus fifty-one cents," Marcus said. "Ninety cents a month versus fifteen dollars. Eleven dollars a year versus a hundred and eighty-four. These aren't rounding differences. This is the difference between a tip and a wage. Between a gesture and a commitment."

"How do we get this in front of people?"

Jake looked up. He'd stopped pacing. "We put it in the app."

"What?"

"We build a comparison tool. Right in Ours. A user opens the app and there's a banner: 'How does Ours compare to Zeta Rewards?' They tap it, they see the chart. Real numbers. Real math. Side by side. Let them decide."

"Zeta will claim we're spreading misinformation."

"It's not misinformation if it's math. The numbers are derived from their own published data. Their SEC filings, their ad revenue reports, their terms of service. Every number in this chart is sourced from Zeta's own disclosures. They can't call their own filings misinformation."

I looked at the chart. The simplicity of it. The clarity. Two columns. Two realities. One platform that pretended to pay people. One platform that actually did.

"Build it," I said.

Jake built it in four hours. Priya designed it — a clean, warm comparison screen that didn't feel aggressive or competitive but simply informative. Two columns. Two sets of numbers. A button at the bottom: "See the math." Tap the button and it expanded to show every source — SEC filings, Zeta's published ad revenue per user, Zeta Rewards terms of service, Ours's public revenue-sharing documentation.

We pushed the update at 9 PM on a Thursday. By Friday morning, screenshots of the comparison were everywhere. Twitter. Reddit. Instagram — on Instagram itself, which was either ironic or poetic or both. People were posting the chart with captions like "wait, Zeta Rewards pays THREE CENTS?" and "there's another app that pays 65%?" and the one that made me sit down and put my head in my hands:

"my grandpa was right lol"

Someone — I never found out who, though I suspect it was one of Marcus's students — had posted about Ours with the caption: "my friend's grandpa literally built this in his kitchen and it pays you 20x what Instagram does."

Grandpa. I'm sixty-two. But in the demographic that was now discovering Ours — college students, young professionals, the generation that lived on their phones — sixty-two was grandpa. And somehow, being grandpa made it better. Grandpa wasn't a Silicon Valley tech bro trying to get rich. Grandpa was a carpenter who got mad because his granddaughter wasn't getting paid. Grandpa was authentic in a way that no amount of corporate messaging could manufacture.

The sign-ups spiked. Not spiked — erupted. Two thousand in twenty-four hours. Five thousand in forty-eight. Jake's infrastructure held — barely, with the kind of engineering heroics that involved him not sleeping and consuming enough Red Bull to concern a cardiologist.

David Park's vaccine had failed. The virus was spreading.

Marcus called me at midnight on Saturday. I was in bed — a rare occurrence during that period, when the bed was more of a suggestion than a habit.

"Roger. We just hit ten thousand users."

"Ten thousand."

"Ten thousand. And the growth is accelerating. The comparison chart is the most shared piece of content in Ours's history. It's been screenshotted and reposted on every major platform. We're getting organic press — Engadget, Mashable, The Verge is doing a follow-up piece. TechCrunch wants an interview."

"What's the retention?"

"Seventy-four percent at Day 7. Higher than our beta."

"What's the average daily earnings?"

"Fifty-three cents. Slightly up from the beta. The programmatic ads are performing even better at scale."

I lay in my bed in my apartment in Albany and stared at the ceiling. Ten thousand people. Ten thousand real human beings who'd downloaded the app I'd sketched on a napkin, who were using the Market Priya had redesigned, who were watching the counter Jake had built, who were earning money that Marcus had modeled.

Ten thousand people whose attention was, for the first time in the history of social media, being treated as what it was: labor. Valuable. Compensable. Theirs.

"Marcus."

"Yeah."

"How many employees does Zeta have?"

"Approximately 67,000."

"We have four people and a dog."

"Five people. Teddy counts."

"Five people, a dog, and a lawyer."

"Against 67,000."

We were quiet. The kind of quiet that isn't empty — the kind that's full of something neither person needs to say because they're both thinking it.

"Goodnight, Marcus."

"Goodnight, Roger. Sleep well. Tomorrow's going to be a big day."

"They're all big days now."

"Yeah. They are."

I hung up. Biscuit was on the bed, which he was technically not allowed on but which neither of us pretended to enforce. His tail thumped once. His eyes were closed. He was dreaming — about what, I couldn't say, but his paws twitched in the way that meant he was running.

Good. Keep running.

We all should.

But I made a mistake that week. My mistake. Nobody else's.

The podcast was called "Disrupt or Die." Two million listeners, mostly the tech-curious crowd that treats Silicon Valley like a spectator sport. The host reached out the day after the comparison chart went viral, and Marcus said no, and Teddy said definitely no, and I said yes because I was riding the wave and the wave felt good and I'd forgotten the first rule of carpentry: measure twice, cut once.

I didn't measure at all. I just cut.

The interview started well. The host — young, enthusiastic, the kind of tech journalist who treats founders like athletes — lobbed softballs about my background, the napkin, the vision. I told the stories I'd told before, and they landed the way they always landed, and somewhere around minute twenty I stopped being careful.

"So Roger, the comparison chart. Three cents versus fifty cents. You really went for the throat."

"I went for the truth. There's a difference."

"Some people are saying Zeta Rewards is still a step in the right direction. At least they're paying something."

And here's where I should have nodded and pivoted and said something diplomatic about market forces and user choice. Here's where Marcus would have steered me toward safe harbor.

Instead, I laughed.

"They're paying three cents a day. That's not a step in the right direction. That's a publicity stunt designed to inoculate people against the real thing. It's like a company dumping toxic waste and then planting one tree and calling themselves environmental pioneers."

"That's quite an accusation."

"It's not an accusation. It's math. Look at their terms of service. Look at how they define 'qualifying engagement.' They built a system that LOOKS like revenue sharing but is engineered to pay as little as possible while claiming to pay as much as possible. The fifty percent number on their landing page is a lie."

"Lie is a strong word."

"False advertising is a strong practice. What would you call it when you put '50% revenue share' in giant letters and then bury in the footnotes that each individual user gets a fraction of a fraction?"

The host was leaning forward now. His eyes had that gleam that journalists get when someone says something they shouldn't.

"Are you saying Zeta is committing fraud?"

"I'm saying—"

And I should have stopped there. I should have said "I'm saying you should read their terms of service." I should have backed down, qualified, added the word "allegedly" that Teddy had drilled into my head.

But I was Roger Grubb, the carpenter who'd testified before Congress, the grandpa who'd built an app in his kitchen, and I was winning, and winning felt like a permission slip.

"I'm saying that if a used car dealer advertised 'up to fifty miles per gallon' and the actual car got three, we'd call that fraud. Same math. Different product. The only reason we don't call it fraud when Zeta does it is because nobody regulates attention the way we regulate automobiles. But we should. And we will. And when we do, history will call it what it is."

The podcast dropped on Friday. By Saturday morning, Marcus was at my door.

"You called them frauds."

"I didn't use the word fraud—"

"You said 'we'd call that fraud.' You said 'history will call it what it is.' You implied, on a podcast with two million listeners, that Zeta is committing fraud."

"I was making a comparison—"

"You were making a mistake." He sat at the kitchen table — the same table where I'd sketched the napkin, where I'd turned down fifty million dollars, where I'd built something that mattered. Now it was the table where I'd learn what hubris costs. "Roger, Zeta has a legal department larger than most law firms. They have been waiting for you to slip. And you just handed them everything they need."

"For what?"

"For a defamation suit. For a cease-and-desist. For a campaign to paint you as a reckless provocateur making false claims about a publicly traded company." He pulled out his phone. "They've already issued a statement. 'Mr. Grubb's reckless and defamatory statements demonstrate a fundamental misunderstanding of Zeta Rewards and a willingness to spread misinformation. We are exploring all legal options to protect our company and our three billion users from baseless attacks.'"

My stomach dropped. The same feeling as when you cut a board too short — the irreversibility of it, the moment where you realize you've wasted material and time and can't go back.

"Teddy—"

"Teddy is already working on it. He thinks he can get them to back down — there's no actual falsehood, you were making rhetorical comparisons — but this is going to cost us two weeks and fifty thousand dollars in legal fees that we don't have, fighting a battle that we didn't need to fight because you couldn't keep your mouth shut."

"Marcus—"

"I'm not finished." He wasn't angry. That was worse. Marcus angry was manageable. Marcus cold was a reckoning. "You're the founder. You're the face. Everything you say reflects on the company. The hearing worked because you told the truth carefully — you spoke plainly but you didn't overreach. The comparison chart worked because it was facts, sourced, documented, defensible. This? This was you believing your own press. This was you thinking that being right gives you permission to be reckless."

I sat down. Biscuit came to me — he always comes when the room goes wrong — and pressed his head against my leg.

"You're right," I said.

"I know I'm right. The question is whether you know it."

"I know it."

"Good. Then here's what happens next. Teddy handles the legal. You go silent — no interviews, no podcasts, no social media posts that aren't reviewed by someone with more impulse control than you have. And you remember this feeling. The feeling of having handed your enemies ammunition with your own hands. Because they're going to use it. And the next time you want to shoot your mouth off on a podcast, you remember that this war isn't won by soundbites. It's won by outlasting. And you almost made it very easy for them to outlast us."

He stood up. Walked to the door. Turned back.

"I know you think I'm being hard on you."

"You are being hard on me."

"I'm being accurate with you. Which is different. And which is what you pay me for." He almost smiled. "Get some sleep. Tomorrow we rebuild the trust you burned today. It's not fatal. But it's a wound, and wounds need tending."

He left. I sat at the table, Biscuit's head on my knee, staring at the napkin on the wall — the original napkin, framed now, the six words that had started everything.

USERS GET PAID. ATTENTION HAS VALUE.

I'd believed those words with everything I had. But believing something doesn't give you permission to be careless about it. The truth needs defending, not just declaring. And I'd confused declaration with defense, and now Teddy was billing hours and Marcus was disappointed and somewhere in Menlo Park, David Park was reading my words and smiling.

Sofia called that night.

"I heard the podcast," she said.

"I'm an idiot."

"You're not an idiot. You're a person who got high on winning and forgot that winning isn't a destination. It's a process."

"That sounds like something Marcus would say."

"Marcus did say it. He called me. He wanted to make sure I knew, and he wanted to make sure I'd tell you what I'm about to tell you."

"Which is?"

"You're not your mistakes. But you are responsible for them. Fix this. Learn from it. Don't do it again." A pause. "And call your granddaughter. She saw the comparison chart and she wants to tell you she's proud of you. She doesn't need to know about the podcast."

"Sofia—"

"Tuesday. Normal time. Pupusas. And between now and then, be humble. Humble doesn't come naturally to carpenters. But it comes when you let it."

She hung up. I sat with Biscuit in the dark kitchen, listening to the refrigerator hum and the clock tick and the sound of my own breathing — slower now, calmer, the adrenaline of victory fading into the quieter work of repair.

Some lessons you learn in books. Some you learn in AA meetings. And some you learn by opening your mouth on a podcast and watching everything you've built wobble because you thought being right was the same as being careful.

It's not. I know that now.

I'll know it forever.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 19

Cracks

I need to tell you about Jake's hands.

Not because they were remarkable — they weren't. They were programmer's hands, long-fingered, pale from indoor work, nails bitten to the quick. The kind of hands that had never held a hammer or a scalpel, that had spent their entire working life touching nothing but keyboards. There's no callus on a coder's hands. No scar. No history written in the skin.

But hands tell you things even when they're unmarked. And Jake's hands had started shaking.

Not dramatically — you wouldn't notice if you weren't looking. A tremor in the left hand when he reached for his coffee. A slight vibration when he typed, visible only if you watched from a certain angle. The kind of shaking that means stress, or caffeine, or fear, or all three in a cocktail that the body processes as vibration.

I noticed because I'm a carpenter. I watch hands. I watch them the way Sofia watches animals — for what they reveal, for the story the body tells when the mouth stays quiet.

Jake's mouth had been staying quiet for weeks. Since before the Senate hearing, since before the Zeta Rewards exposure, since before JP's money landed. A slow withdrawal, like a tide going out so gradually you don't notice the beach getting wider until suddenly there's a hundred feet of exposed sand where water used to be.

He was still brilliant. His code was still clean, still fast, still structured with the precision of someone who thought in systems. But the energy was different. The 2 AM sessions had stopped. The enthusiasm that had powered him through the early months — the full-face grin, the "this is the most dangerous thing I've ever seen" — had dimmed to something more careful. More guarded. More like a man doing a job and less like a man on a mission.

Marcus noticed too. Of course he did. Marcus notices everything; it's the Goldman Sachs survival instinct — read the room, read the people, read the silence between the words.

"Something's off with Jake," he said one evening. We were in the office — the two rooms above Tony's Barbershop, which now smelled less like Barbicide and more like the Domino's Jake's team ordered every night. Everyone else had gone home. It was just us, the way it had been in the beginning, before the money and the team and the Senate and the five-thousand-user milestone that was now fifty thousand and climbing.

"I know," I said.

"Do you know what?"

"No. But I know the shape of it. He's carrying something."

"The debt?"

"Maybe. But $180,000 of student debt doesn't make your hands shake. Something new is pressing on him."

"Have you asked?"

"Not yet. He's not ready to tell me."

"How do you know?"

"Because I spent fifteen years not being ready to tell anyone that I was drinking myself to death. I know what 'not ready' looks like. You don't push it. You stay close and you wait."

Marcus was quiet. Then: "We might not have time to wait."

"What do you mean?"

He pulled up a chart on his laptop. User growth over the past month — the beautiful upward curve that had been the best news of my life. But underneath the growth chart, another metric: server costs.

"We're scaling faster than Jake's architecture can handle," Marcus said. "The system was designed for ten thousand concurrent users. We're hitting thirty thousand at peak. Jake needs to rebuild the infrastructure — the database, the payment processing, the event stream. It's a six-week project at minimum, and he needs to be running it."

"And he's not running it?"

"He's delegating. To the two junior engineers. They're good but they're not Jake. The architecture decisions — the ones that will determine whether we can scale to a million users or collapse at a hundred thousand — those need to be his."

"I'll talk to him."

"Roger—"

"I'll talk to him."

I found Jake on the roof. Tony's building had a flat roof accessible by a fire escape, and Jake had claimed it as his thinking space — a patch of gravel and tar paper with a view of the Albany skyline, which is not impressive but which has the rough beauty of any city seen from above, where the mess of streets and buildings resolves into a pattern that makes sense only from a distance.

He was sitting on an overturned bucket, staring at his phone. When he heard me on the fire escape, he turned the phone face-down on his knee with a speed that told me what I needed to know: whatever was on that screen, he didn't want me to see it.

"Hey," I said.

"Hey."

I sat on another bucket — a five-gallon Homer from Home Depot, the bright orange ones, which Tony had left up here after some roof patching job he'd never finished. The rooftop was cold — November in Albany, the sky the color of concrete. Below us, Tony's exhaust fan hummed. The Solano Avenue streetlights were coming on one by one — sodium vapor, the old yellow kind, not the LED replacements the city had been installing. My brain filed it automatically: High-Pressure Sodium, 150-watt, GE Lucalox from the bulb shape. Even on a rooftop having the most important conversation of my professional life, the identification game doesn't stop. It never stops. Somewhere south, a siren.

"Your hands are shaking," I said.

He looked at his hands. Curled them into fists. Opened them. The tremor was there, faint, a whisper of motion in the fingers.

"I know," he said.

"Talk to me."

"It's nothing."

"Jake, I'm a recovering alcoholic. 'It's nothing' is a phrase I used about four thousand times before I collapsed on my kitchen floor. I know what 'it's nothing' means. It means 'it's everything but I can't say it yet.'"

He didn't respond. But he didn't leave, which in the geometry of difficult conversations is the equivalent of an opening.

We sat in silence for a long time. I've learned — from AA, from carpentry, from raising two children who are now adults with their own silences: that the most useful thing you can do for a person who's holding something heavy is be present without demanding they put it down. The weight has its own timeline. It'll come when it comes.

"I owe $180,000," Jake said finally. "You know that."

"I know."

"The interest rate is 6.8%. That's $12,240 a year in interest alone. My minimum payment is $1,100 a month. I've been making minimum payments since I left Stanford. In four years, I've paid $52,800. My balance has gone from $170,000 to $180,000. I've paid fifty-two thousand dollars and I owe ten thousand more than when I started."

The math was obscene. I didn't need to be Marcus to see it — the numbers had a specific cruelty, the cruelty of a system designed to extract value from the people it was supposed to serve. Like a social media platform. Like the thing we were fighting.

"Jake—"

"Wait. Let me finish." He took a breath. "When I joined Ours, I was making zero. I accepted that. I believed in what we were building. I still believe in what we're building. But we're eight months in. I have equity that's worth nothing until there's a liquidity event. I have a salary now, thanks to JP,but it's $85,000, which in the Bay Area is enough to live and make my loan payments and have nothing left. I am twenty-six years old and I will be paying this debt until I'm fifty. That is not a complaint. That is arithmetic."

"I can talk to Marcus about adjusting—"

"It's not about the salary." He paused. Looked at the phone on his knee. Picked it up. Turned it over. Showed me the screen.

An email. From a recruiter at Google. Subject line: Senior Infrastructure Engineer — Compensation Package: $450,000 base + RSU.

Four hundred and fifty thousand dollars a year. Plus restricted stock units that, at Google's current valuation, would be worth another $200,000 annually. Total compensation: $650,000.

Enough to pay off his student debt in three years. Enough to buy a real apartment. Enough to stop living in a closet, stop eating rice and beans, stop waking up at 3 AM with the particular anxiety of a person who owes more money than they can conceptualize.

"I haven't responded," Jake said. "I want you to know that. I haven't responded."

"But you've been thinking about it."

"Every day for three weeks."

I looked at the skyline. Albany in November. The Bay Bridge lit up in the distance. The water somewhere below the buildings, invisible but present, the way important things often are.

"Jake. Listen to me."

"I'm listening."

"If you need to go, go. I mean that. I'm not going to guilt you into staying. I'm not going to tell you that leaving makes you a bad person. You're twenty-six. You have debt that would crush anyone. The fact that you've stayed this long, that you've built what you've built, that you've worked for free and then for less than you're worth — that's not obligation. That's character. And character is not a cage."

His eyes were red. Not crying — holding. The tension of a man gripping something he doesn't want to drop.

"But I want to tell you something," I continued. "Something I've learned from building things."

"What?"

"Every project hits a moment — usually about halfway through, usually when the hardest work is ahead — where the smart move is to walk away. The foundation is poured but the framing hasn't started. The commitment is real but the reward is theoretical. It's the moment when everything you've invested feels like a sunk cost and everything ahead feels like a gamble. Every builder I've ever known has stood at that moment. The ones who stayed aren't smarter or braver. They just decided that finishing mattered more than leaving."

"That's easy to say when you don't owe $180,000."

"You're right. It is. And I won't pretend to understand what that weight feels like. But I know what unfinished feels like. I know what it's like to look back at something you walked away from and wonder what it would've been. I've got two or three of those in my life, and they hurt more than any debt."

I also knew something about watching people make decisions under financial pressure. Fifteen years of closing tables had shown me every version of this moment. The young couple stretching for a house they couldn't afford because the Zillow estimate told them it was a good investment. The widower selling the family home at a loss because the grief made the walls feel like they were closing in and he needed out more than he needed the money. The investor who walked away from a deal that would have tripled his money because his wife was pregnant and the risk suddenly felt different with a child coming.

Money doesn't just create pressure. It creates a lens. Everything looks different when you're looking through the lens of debt, or poverty, or financial fear. Opportunities look bigger than they are. Risks look smaller. The bird in the hand — $400,000 a year, benefits, the Google name on your resume like a golden stamp — looks enormous and certain and real. The bird in the bush — equity in a startup that might be worth nothing or might be worth everything — looks theoretical and distant and stupid.

I'd watched smart people make terrible decisions through that lens. And I'd watched smart people make brilliant decisions by putting it down for five minutes and asking themselves the question that money can't answer: What do I actually want to build?

Jake was quiet. The phone sat between us, the Google email glowing on the screen like a door into a different life.

"I'm not going to ask you to stay," I said. "But I'm going to ask you to decide. Not today. Not this week. But soon. Because the worst thing — worse than leaving, worse than staying — is standing in between. The shaking. The phone calls you take in hallways. The distance. You're half-in, Jake. And half-in isn't fair to you and it isn't fair to us."

He nodded. Slowly. The nod of a person who has heard something true and is deciding whether to accept it.

"How long do I have?"

"Take a month. Talk to someone you trust. Do the math, not just the money math, the life math. And then decide."

"And if I decide to leave?"

"Then I'll shake your hand and thank you for building something extraordinary and I'll wish you well at Google. And I'll mean every word."

"And if I decide to stay?"

"Then I need you all the way in. Not half. Not with one foot at the door. All the way. Because what's coming next, the scaling, the attacks, the war Marcus keeps warning about — I need my builder. And my builder needs to want to be here."

Jake picked up the phone. Closed the email. Put the phone in his pocket.

"A month," he said.

"A month."

We sat on the roof for another ten minutes. Neither of us spoke. Below, Tony closed up for the night — I could hear the click of his deadbolt, the rattle of the security gate. The street quieted. The city did what cities do at dusk — softened, blurred, became a place where anything might happen.

I didn't tell Marcus about the Google offer. Not yet. Some things need to stay between the people who share them, at least until the person holding the weight decides where to set it down.

Sofia noticed before I said a word.

We were at Rinconcito that Tuesday, our booth, pupusas and horchata, the rhythm of us. I hadn't mentioned Jake. Hadn't planned to. But Sofia put down her fork halfway through a sentence about a Great Dane with a torn cruciate and looked at me the way she looks at an animal she's about to diagnose.

"Something's wrong."

"It's work stuff. I'm handling it."

"That's not what I asked. Something's wrong and you're carrying it in your jaw. You clench when you're holding something you can't fix. You've been clenching since you sat down."

I rubbed my jaw. She was right. I hadn't noticed.

"It's Jake," I said. And because it was Sofia, because she deserved honesty the way she demanded it, I told her. Not about the Google offer: that was Jake's to share. But about the shaking hands. The hallway phone calls. The distance.

Sofia was quiet for a long time. She moved her horchata in a slow circle on the table, watching the condensation trail it left on the wood.

"Roger. I need to tell you something, and you're not going to like hearing it."

"Okay."

"I've seen this before. Not with a coworker. With someone closer." She didn't say his name — the ex, the one who'd lied like it was breathing, the man whose deceptions had driven her to a career where her patients couldn't deceive her because animals don't know how. She never said his name. She just called him before. "Before had the same pattern. Small withdrawals. Explanations that were slightly too smooth. Eye contact that shifted, not away from you, but through you. Like he was looking at something behind your face that you couldn't see."

"Jake's not lying to me."

"I didn't say he was lying. I said he's hiding something. Those are different. Lying is active, you construct a false version. Hiding is passive — you just don't show the real one. The body language is similar but the energy is different. A liar leans toward you because the performance requires proximity. A person who's hiding pulls back. Creates space. Finds reasons to be on the roof instead of at the table."

I thought about Jake on the bucket. The phone turned face-down. The smile that didn't reach his eyes in the car to the airport.

"How do you know this?" I asked. Though I knew.

"Because I spent four years learning it the hard way. I trusted someone whose words never matched his weight. He'd say one thing and his body would say another, and I'd believe the words because words are what people are supposed to use, and I ignored the body because the body was telling me something I didn't want to know." She looked at me across the table, and her eyes had that particular steadiness — the Sofia steadiness that came from having walked through deception and out the other side and decided, permanently, to live in the country of truth. "I chose animals after him. Because a dog in pain doesn't pretend to be fine. A cat with a broken leg doesn't tell you it's nothing. Animals can't construct the kind of elaborate performance that people can. They hurt honestly. And I needed honest hurt for a while, because dishonest comfort had nearly destroyed me."

"Sofia—"

"I'm not saying Jake is like that. I'm saying watch. Don't confront, don't accuse, don't push. Watch the way I watch — for the gap between what someone says and what their body does. If the gap is small, he's struggling. If the gap is wide, he's decided something and he's not telling you what."

I reached across the table and took her hand. The surgeon's hand. The hand that healed things by understanding exactly where they were broken.

"Thank you," I said.

"Don't thank me. Act on it. The worst thing I did with before was notice the signs and explain them away. Don't explain away what you're seeing. Trust it. Your hands know when a joint is wrong before your brain does. Trust what your hands know about Jake."

She picked up her fork and returned to the pupusa as if she hadn't just given me the most important diagnostic tool of my life. That was Sofia — she delivered truth the way she delivered surgery: precisely, completely, and then moved on to the next thing that needed attention.

But I'll tell you this: I went home that night and I sat at my kitchen table — the same table, always the same table, and I looked at the napkin on the wall and I felt something I hadn't felt since the early days.

Fear. Not of Zeta. Not of the war. Not of failure.

Fear of losing someone. Fear that the thing I was building required sacrifices from other people that I had no right to ask for. Fear that the carpenter's dream was becoming someone else's nightmare.

Biscuit put his chin on my knee. I scratched his ear. The one that flops.

"We might lose him," I said to the dog.

Biscuit looked up at me with an expression that communicated nothing and everything simultaneously, which is the dog's gift, to be present without judgment, to listen without advice, to stay without being asked.

I went to bed. I didn't sleep.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 20

The Crack

I'm going to tell you about Jake's betrayal now, because you deserve the truth and because I've put it off long enough. Not because it's painful, though it is,but because betrayal is complicated when you love the person who did it, and I loved Jake Morrison the way you love a younger version of yourself: fiercely, protectively, and with the understanding that they're going to make mistakes you can see coming but can't prevent.

Here's what you need to know about debt.

I've been broke. Most of my life, actually. I've been the kind of broke where you calculate whether you can afford both gas and lunch, and you choose gas because you can skip lunch but you can't skip the job site. I've been the kind of broke where Christmas presents come from the workshop — handmade cutting boards, wooden toys, bookshelves built from scrap. I've been the kind of broke that's embarrassing at parent-teacher conferences and invisible at AA meetings, because in AA, everyone's been broke. Being broke is a symptom, not a disease.

But I've never been $180,000 broke. I've never been the kind of broke where the number isn't just large — it's alive. It accrues interest. It grows while you sleep. It sends you letters that say things like "minimum payment due" and "delinquency warning" and "collection activity may begin." It's a debt that doesn't care about your ideals or your principles or your belief in a better internet. It wants money. Your money. Every month. Without exception. Without mercy.

Jake was twenty-six years old with a debt that would take him, at his current earning rate of zero dollars because he was working for equity in a startup that had no revenue — approximately forever to repay. His interest alone was $1,400 a month. Every month. Fourteen hundred dollars appearing on a statement like a bill from the universe for the crime of wanting an education.

He never talked about it. In the five months he'd been with us, eating rice and beans in my apartment, sleeping on a couch or a folding cot or occasionally the floor when Marcus was on the couch, Jake Morrison had never once complained about money. Never mentioned the debt. Never asked when the equity might be worth something. He coded. He built. He solved problems at 2 AM with a focus so intense it was almost violent. And every month, the number grew.

I should have seen it. I should have asked. A good leader — a good foreman, which is what I was, whether I used that word or not — checks on his crew. Not just the work. The people. You walk a job site and you don't just look at the framing. You look at the guys. Who's tired. Who's distracted. Who's got something at home that's eating them alive. You notice because noticing is how you prevent accidents.

I didn't notice. I was too busy fighting a war to see that one of my soldiers was drowning.

The phone call happened at 11 PM on a Thursday. I know this because I was in bed — actually in bed, attempting actual sleep, which during this period was as rare as a calm day on the internet. Biscuit was on the bed. The apartment was quiet. Marcus had gone home. Jake was at the folding table, doing what Jake always did at 11 PM, which was code in the half-light with headphones on, the screen reflecting in his glasses like a second pair of eyes.

I wasn't asleep yet. I was in that state between waking and sleeping where your mind replays the day's problems with the volume turned down — patent filings, user growth, server costs, the comparison chart's viral spread, the TechCrunch interview request I hadn't answered. Background noise. The white sound of entrepreneurship.

Through the wall, I heard Jake's phone ring. Not a text buzz — an actual ring, the default iPhone marimba tone he'd never bothered to change, which told you something about Jake: he cared about the code, not the interface. People Jake's age don't get phone calls. They get texts, DMs, Slack messages, notifications. A phone call, at 11 PM, to a twenty-six-year-old, means something.

His voice was low. I couldn't hear words — just the murmur of someone speaking quietly in a room they think is private but isn't, because the apartment was 740 square feet and privacy was a concept, not a reality.

The call lasted seven minutes. I know this because I looked at the clock when it started and again when it ended. Seven minutes. Not long. Long enough.

After the call, I heard Jake's chair scrape against the floor. His footsteps — bare feet on hardwood, the specific pad-pad-pad of someone walking carefully, trying not to wake someone. The front door opened. Closed. He was on the stoop, or in the hallway, or somewhere that wasn't in my apartment, having a conversation or a cigarette or a crisis.

He came back in twelve minutes. Sat down. Started typing. The normal Jake rhythm: fast, precise, productive. As if nothing had happened.

I lay in bed and stared at the ceiling and told myself it was nothing. People get phone calls. People step outside. People have lives beyond the project, beyond the apartment, beyond the war.

But the thing about spending forty years building things, houses, systems, businesses,is that you develop an ear for sounds that don't belong. The creak that shouldn't be there. The settling that's too heavy. The joint that's not quite right. Fifteen years in real estate teaches you that same thing about people: the pause before the lie, the too-quick agreement, the voice that drops half a register when the truth gets expensive. Something in the architecture of that phone call — the timing, the hushed voice, the careful footsteps — sounded wrong.
