I filed it. The way you file a concern about a load-bearing beam — you don't panic, you don't tear open the wall, but you don't forget it either. You watch. You listen. You wait for the next creak.

The next creak came two weeks later.

We were growing. Fifteen thousand users, then twenty, then twenty-five. The comparison chart was still circulating. Press inquiries were coming in daily, not just tech press anymore but mainstream outlets. CNN's tech correspondent wanted a segment. NPR was developing a story. A producer from 60 Minutes had emailed Marcus.

And the data was getting better. Not just the engagement metrics, the advertiser data. Jake had built a dashboard that showed me, in real time, what advertisers were paying for Market placements through our programmatic connections. The CPMs were climbing. $12... $15... $18... Advertisers were bidding MORE for Ours's Market inventory because the click-through rates justified the premium. The Willing Attention Thesis was proving itself with every dollar.

This data was our most valuable asset. More valuable than the code, more valuable than the user base, more valuable than the brand. Because this data proved, empirically and irrefutably, that paying users for attention was more profitable than stealing it. This data was the gun on the table. The thing that made Zeta afraid.

And someone was leaking it.

Marcus found the first evidence on a Monday. A blog post on a minor tech site — one of those sites that exists in the space between journalism and speculation, written by people with opinions and internet connections but no editorial standards — contained specific numbers from our internal dashboard. Not the public numbers. Not the stuff we'd shared with press or put in the comparison chart. Internal numbers. Server costs. User acquisition metrics. Revenue per user at different engagement tiers.

Numbers that lived in a database that four people had access to: me, Marcus, Jake, and Priya.

"Someone's talking," Marcus said. He showed me the blog post on his phone. His voice was flat — the dangerous flat, the one that means the math inside his head has already calculated something he doesn't want to say out loud.

"Could they have reverse-engineered the numbers from public data?"

"The server costs? No. The engagement tier breakdown? No. These are internal metrics. Someone gave them this."

"Who would benefit from leaking our internal data to a tech blog?"

"Someone who wants to demonstrate knowledge of our system to a third party. Someone who's proving they have access."

We looked at each other across the kitchen table. The table where everything happened. The table that had seen the napkin, the business model, the prototype, the cease-and-desist, the $50 million offer, and now this — the realization that someone inside our four-person team was feeding information to someone outside it.

"It's not Priya," Marcus said. "She doesn't have access to the financial data."

"It's not you."

"It's not me."

"And it's not me."

Which left Jake. The twenty-six-year-old with $180,000 in debt and a phone that rang at 11 PM.

I didn't confront him. Not yet. Because I'm a carpenter, and carpenters know that you don't tear open a wall because you heard one creak. You could be wrong. You could cause more damage investigating than the problem itself would cause. You watch. You listen. You gather evidence. And when you're sure, SURE,you act.

I asked Marcus to set up monitoring on the database. Logging. A record of who accessed which data, when, from where. Standard security practice. Something we should have done from the beginning but hadn't, because when you're four people in an apartment, you trust each other the way a family trusts each other — completely, implicitly, without infrastructure, because the infrastructure of trust is proximity and shared purpose and that's supposed to be enough.

It's not enough. That's one of the things this story teaches, and I'm sorry to be the one teaching it.

Marcus set up the logging on a Wednesday. By Friday, we had the data.

Jake had accessed the financial dashboard seventeen times in the past month. Normal — he maintained the system, he needed access. But four of those accesses were between 11 PM and 1 AM, and each one was followed by an export. A download. A CSV file containing exactly the kind of data that had appeared in the blog post.

Four late-night exports. Four files. Sent somewhere.

I sat with this information for twenty-four hours. The longest twenty-four hours of my life, including the ones in early sobriety when every minute was a decision not to drink. Because what I was holding was worse than a craving. It was a certainty I didn't want. The knowledge that someone I trusted — someone I'd brought into my home, fed from my kitchen, watched sleep on my couch — was betraying me.

On Saturday morning, I called Marcus. "Come over. Don't tell Jake."

Marcus arrived in twenty minutes. We sat at the table. I told him what the logs showed.

He was quiet for a long time. Then: "We need to find out who he's talking to before we confront him."

"Why?"

"Because if it's a journalist, that's one thing. If it's Zeta, that's another. If it's Catherine Chen or another investor, that's a third. The leak matters less than the destination."

"How do we find out?"

"We ask him."

"Just ask?"

"Roger. He's twenty-six. He's drowning in debt. He's been eating your rice and beans for five months. Whatever he did, he did it because he's desperate, not because he's evil. And desperate people, when they're confronted with compassion instead of accusation, tell the truth. It's the only thing they have left."

Marcus was right. He usually was. But being right didn't make it easier. Because I was about to sit across from a young man I cared about and tell him I knew he'd broken my trust, and I didn't know which would be worse — his excuse or his absence.

I waited until Sunday. Tuesdays were for Sofia. Sundays were for truth.

Jake came to the apartment at 10 AM, as usual. Coffee. Laptop. Headphones. The routine that had become as familiar to me as the sound of my own saw — the familiar rhythm of Jake Morrison preparing to build.

"Jake. Sit down."

He looked at me. Read my face. Sat down.

I put the access logs on the table. The four late-night exports. The timestamps. The file sizes.

He looked at them. His jaw tightened. His hands — young hands, quick hands, the hands that had built the counter and the Market and the payment system — went still on the table.

"Who are you sending the data to?" I asked.

Silence. Ten seconds. Twenty. I counted, because counting is what I do when I need to stay calm.

"I can explain," he said.

"I'm listening."

"It's not what you think."

"Jake. I don't think anything yet. I'm asking."

He looked at the ceiling. Then at the wall — the napkin, the cease-and-desist, Mia's words. The artifacts of what we'd built together. What he'd helped build. What he was, apparently, also dismantling.

"They contacted me after the TechCrunch article. An intermediary. Not Zeta directly — a consulting firm that does competitive intelligence. They said they wanted to 'understand the landscape.' They offered to pay for insight into emerging social platforms."

"How much?"

"Five thousand a month."

Five thousand dollars a month. Three and a half times his student loan minimum payment. The difference between drowning and breathing.

"And you took it."

"I took the first payment. Then the second. Then they wanted more specific data. Internal metrics. Financial projections. Server architecture. And I—" His voice cracked. Not theatrically. Genuinely. The sound of a foundation splitting. "I gave it to them. Because the number kept getting bigger and the debt kept getting bigger and I couldn't—"

He stopped. Put his hands over his face. His shoulders shook once. Once.

"Roger, I'm sorry. I know that doesn't—"

"Stop." I held up my hand. Not angrily. The way you hold up a hand to stop traffic — firmly, clearly, with authority. "I need to ask you one more question, and I need the truth."

He lowered his hands. His eyes were red. He was twenty-six years old and he looked twelve.

"Did you know the consulting firm worked for Zeta?"

Long pause. The longest pause of the conversation.

"Yes," he whispered. "I figured it out after the second month. The questions they were asking — they weren't market research questions. They were competitive intelligence questions. Specific to our architecture. Specific to our vulnerabilities."

"And you kept going."

"I kept going. Because by then I'd taken ten thousand dollars and I was afraid that if I stopped, they'd expose what I'd already done. And I was afraid that if I told you, you'd—"

"Fire you."

"Hate me."

The word hung in the air. Hate. Such a small word for such a large feeling. Four letters. One syllable. The sound of a young man who had confused fear with betrayal and betrayal with survival and survival with necessity, the way people do when the walls close in and the only door open is the wrong one.

I looked at Jake Morrison. The kid who'd responded to my GitHub message in fourteen minutes. Who'd driven to Albany from Palo Alto in a car he couldn't afford to fill with gas. Who'd built the counter that ticked from zero to one cent — the first cent, the proof of concept, the penny that started everything. Who'd slept on my couch and eaten my food and poured his brilliance into a project he believed in while the debt ate him alive.

I didn't hate him. I couldn't. Because I understood something about Jake Morrison that most people wouldn't — something that went deeper than the debt, deeper than the desperation, all the way down to the foundation of who he was and why the foundation had cracks.

Jake's mother called him every Sunday. I'd heard the calls through the thin walls of the apartment, not the words, but the rhythm. Her voice was the only sound in those conversations. Long, unbroken streams of talking that went on for twenty, thirty, forty minutes. Jake's contributions were monosyllables. Yes. No. Okay. I understand. I will. His voice during those calls was the voice of a person who'd learned that speaking was not the same as being heard, and that the safest response to authority was compliance.

She'd raised him alone. I knew that much. And she'd raised him with a ferocity that she probably called love and that Jake probably couldn't distinguish from control. Every decision made for him. Every path chosen by her. Stanford wasn't Jake's dream, it was hers. Computer science wasn't his passion — it was the field she'd determined would provide the security she'd never had. He was brilliant enough to succeed at anything, and she'd aimed that brilliance like a weapon at the target she chose, and he'd hit it, and it had cost him $180,000, and now he was drowning in debt from a life she'd designed.

The thing about growing up with someone who makes every decision for you is that you never develop the muscle for making them yourself. Right and wrong, for Jake, weren't internal signals — they were external instructions. He did what he was told. By his mother. By his professors. By the consulting firm that called at 11 PM and told him they'd pay for information. He did what he was told because doing what you're told is the only survival skill a domineering parent teaches. The ability to say no — to weigh a choice against your own values, to feel the resistance of conscience and follow it — that's a skill you learn by practice, by being allowed to make mistakes, by being trusted with your own judgment. Jake had never been trusted with his own judgment. His mother had never given him the chance to develop one.

So when a voice on the phone said "We'll pay you five thousand a month for data," Jake heard what he'd always heard: an authority figure telling him what to do. And he did it. Not because he was evil. Not because he didn't love what we were building. Because he was a twenty-six-year-old with extraordinary talent and a moral compass that had never been calibrated, because the one person who should have helped him find north had spent his whole life pointing him in whatever direction she chose.

I'd been him. Not the specifics — I'd never sold information to a competitor. But the powerlessness. The feeling of being shaped by forces you couldn't resist. My father's drinking shaped me. Jake's mother's control shaped him. I'd spent fifteen years doing the wrong thing with a bottle in my hand, and the only difference between my wrong thing and Jake's was that mine was a response to chaos and his was a response to order. Too much order. The suffocating kind.

"You're not fired," I said.

His head came up. "What?"

"You're not fired. But you're done with them. Today. Now. No more calls, no more data, no more money. And you're going to tell me everything they asked and everything you gave them. Every question. Every export. Every piece of information."

"Roger—"

"And you're going to help me figure out how to use what they know against them. Because they think they bought a mole. What they actually bought was a map of what they're afraid of."

Jake stared at me. Tears on his face. The face of a brilliant, terrified, ashamed twenty-six-year-old who had made the kind of mistake that defines a life, not because it's the worst thing he'll ever do, but because it's the first time he'll learn what it means to be forgiven.

"Why?" he asked. "Why aren't you firing me?"

"Because I'm an alcoholic, Jake. I know what it's like to do the wrong thing for what feels like the right reason. And I know that the only thing that saved me was someone who should have given up on me and didn't."

He cried. Really cried. Crying that comes from below the chest, from the place where shame lives, from the basement of a person where they keep the things they can't show anyone.

I let him cry. Then I made him coffee. Then we sat at the kitchen table, that table, always that table — and he told me everything.

And the everything was worse than I expected. And more useful.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 21

The Old Man's Money

Jean-Pierre Beaumont was seventy years old when I met him, and he'd been rich for forty-five of those years, which meant he'd had plenty of time to decide what money was for and had arrived at an answer most rich people never reach: money is for finishing things.

I didn't find JP. Sofia found JP. Or rather, Sofia's mother found JP, because Rosa Reyes had a network that stretched from her church in Albany to every Salvadoran community center, restaurant, and social organization in the Bay Area and beyond, and one of those connections knew a woman who worked as a personal chef for a French-Senegalese billionaire in Woodside, and that woman had heard her employer talking about a social media platform that paid users, and she'd mentioned it to Rosa, who'd mentioned it to Sofia, who'd mentioned it to me over pupusas on a Tuesday.

"A billionaire," I said.

"A telecom billionaire. French-Senegalese. Lives in Woodside. My mother's friend cooks for him."

"And he's interested in Ours?"

"He's interested in things that disrupt. Apparently, he made his money building telecom infrastructure in West Africa and then spent twenty years watching American tech companies use that infrastructure to extract data from African users without paying them. He has opinions about this."

"What kind of opinions?"

"The kind that come with checkbooks."

JP Beaumont's house in Woodside was not a house. It was an estate — a word I use reluctantly because it sounds pretentious, but there's no other word for a property with a gate, a driveway that takes forty-five seconds to drive, and a front door that could accommodate a giraffe. The architecture was old California money — stone and redwood and clay tile, the kind of construction I recognized from decades of working on houses that wealthy people built to look like they'd been there forever.

I brought Marcus. I brought the deck — Marcus's financial model, the Willing Attention Thesis, the engagement data, the growth charts, everything. I wore the same khakis and button-down from the First Light meeting because they were my only meeting clothes and because at this point I'd stopped apologizing for not looking like Silicon Valley.

JP met us at the door himself. No butler, no assistant, no intermediary. A tall man — six-three, maybe six-four — with the build of someone who'd been athletic once and had maintained the frame if not the muscle. Dark skin, close-cropped gray hair, a face that combined French sharpness with West African warmth in a way that was immediately striking. He wore linen pants — Ermenegildo Zegna, I guessed from the drape and the selvedge, the kind that cost what I pay in rent — and a white shirt with the sleeves rolled up, showing forearms that suggested he'd done physical work at some point in his life, which I liked immediately, because I trust forearms more than I trust résumés. On his wrist: a Jaeger-LeCoultre Reverso, the classic rectangular one, which my brain clocked in under two seconds because I'd spent a decade in real estate watching rich men's watches tell me more than their words ever would.

"Mr. Grubb. I'm told you're a carpenter."

"Retired."

"Nobody retires from carpentry. They just find different wood." He smiled. It was the smile of a man who'd said clever things for decades and still enjoyed it. "Come in. I've made coffee. It's terrible — I've never learned to make good coffee, which is embarrassing for a man who lived in Dakar for fifteen years."

The inside of the house was what I expected and wasn't. The architecture was grand — high ceilings, wide rooms, the kind of proportions that make you feel both welcome and small. But the furnishings were modest. Not cheap — considered. A couch that was comfortable, not fashionable. Bookshelves that were full, not decorative. Art on the walls that was personal — photographs, mostly. A young JP standing next to a telecom tower in what looked like Senegal. An older JP shaking hands with a president I didn't recognize. A woman — his wife, I'd learn — laughing on a beach.

He led us to a study. A real study, with a desk that had been used and a window that looked out onto gardens that someone else maintained. He sat behind the desk. We sat in front of it. He poured terrible coffee from a French press and handed us cups without apologizing, which I respected.

"Tell me," he said.

I told him. The whole thing. Not the pitch — the story. From Mia scrolling TikTok to the napkin to the prototype to the beta to the growth to the patent trolls to Zeta Rewards to ten thousand users and counting. I told it the way I'd tell it to a friend — direct, unpolished, with the rough edges left in because the rough edges are where the truth lives.

Marcus supplemented with numbers when numbers were needed. The Willing Attention Thesis. The engagement data. The revenue projections. The comparison with Zeta Rewards. The math that proved, line by line, that willing attention was worth more than stolen attention.

JP listened the way rich men listen when they've heard a thousand pitches and are tired of being sold to — with patience, with skepticism, with the attention that separates curiosity from politeness. He didn't interrupt. He didn't ask questions during the presentation. He drank his terrible coffee and he watched and he waited.

When I finished, he was quiet for twenty seconds. I counted. Then he said something I didn't expect.

"I built my first telecom tower in 1983. In Thiès, Senegal. My mother's hometown. The government wouldn't fund it. The international agencies wouldn't fund it. The World Bank said the return on investment was 'insufficient for the region's development trajectory,' which is a fancy way of saying they didn't think African people needed telephones."

He stood. Walked to the window.

"I funded it myself. Sold my apartment in Paris. Borrowed from my father-in-law, who thought I was insane but loved my wife enough to bet on her husband. Built the tower with local workers — welders, electricians, men who'd never seen telecom equipment but who could build anything you showed them how to build."

He turned back to us.

"That tower connected a hundred thousand people. Not to the internet — the internet didn't exist yet. To each other. To the city. To the world outside their village. For the first time, a farmer in Thiès could call his brother in Dakar. A mother could hear her daughter's voice at university. A small business could reach customers beyond walking distance."

He sat back down.

"And then, twenty years later, I watched American companies use the infrastructure — MY infrastructure, the towers I built — to extract data from those same people. Facebook Free Basics. Google's connectivity initiatives. They gave African users 'free' internet that routed through their servers, collected their data, and sold their attention to advertisers. The same people my towers were built to connect became products. Without their knowledge. Without their consent. Without a single dollar flowing back."

He looked at me. Not at the deck, not at the numbers. At me.

"You're building the correction."

"Yes."

"You're building the thing I've wanted someone to build for twenty years."

"Yes."

"How much do you need?"

Marcus started to speak — the pitch, the projections, the carefully prepared funding request. JP held up his hand.

"Not you. Him." He pointed at me. "How much do you need, carpenter?"

I thought about it. Not the financial model. Not the projections. The actual number. The what-do-we-need-to-survive-and-fight-and-build number.

"Five million dollars."

"For what percentage?"

"Eight percent."

JP smiled. Not the clever smile from the door. A different one. The smile of a man who has just heard exactly what he wanted to hear.

"Every founder I've funded has asked for the minimum and offered the maximum. Five million for eight percent values your company at sixty-two and a half million. You have ten thousand users and no revenue. By every standard metric, you're overvaluing yourself by a factor of ten."

"I'm not valuing the company. I'm valuing the idea. And the idea is worth more than any metric you can apply to it, because the idea — if it works at scale — restructures the economics of the entire internet. You know that. That's why you're listening."

"And if it doesn't work at scale?"

"Then you've lost five million dollars and I've lost seven years of my life and we'll both have learned something about the limits of optimism."

He laughed. A real laugh. The laugh of a man who had built telecom towers in West Africa with borrowed money and understood, at a cellular level, what it meant to bet everything on something that the world said couldn't work.

"I'll give you five million for eight percent," he said. "On three conditions."

"Name them."

"One: the sixty-five percent is permanent. Written into the corporate charter. Irrevocable. No future board, no future investor, no future acquirer can reduce the user share below sixty-five percent. It's a constitutional amendment, not a policy."

"Done."

"Two: you never sell to Zeta. Or Google. Or any platform whose business model depends on extracting user data without compensation. I didn't build towers in Africa so American companies could steal from my people. I'm not funding a platform so American companies can buy it and gut it."

"Done."

"Three." He paused. Poured more terrible coffee. "Three: you let me help. Not as a board member — I'm too old for board meetings. As an advisor. A phone call when you need it. A door opened when you can't open it yourself. I have friends in telecom, in government, in the EU regulatory apparatus. The war you're fighting isn't just American. It's global. And I've been fighting global wars since before you picked up a hammer."

I looked at Marcus. Marcus gave the nod. The full Marcus nod, not the micro-nod. The one that means: this is right.

"Done," I said.

JP Beaumont reached across the desk. We shook hands. His grip was strong — not performatively strong, not the power-handshake, but the grip of a man who'd helped build telecom towers with his own hands forty years ago and hadn't forgotten what holding on feels like.

"Five million dollars," he said. "I'll have it wired by Friday. My lawyer will send the term sheet by end of day. It'll be three pages, not thirty, because I don't believe in legal documents longer than the relationship they describe."

"Thank you," I said. And I meant it in a way that I'd never meant thank you before — not as politeness, not as obligation, but as recognition. Recognition that an old man in Woodside had seen the same thing I'd seen at a Peet's on Solano Avenue, and had decided it was worth five million dollars and eight percent and a phone call whenever I needed it.

We drove back to Albany in Marcus's car. Marcus drove. I sat in the passenger seat and stared out the window at the Peninsula landscape scrolling by — green and wealthy and manicured, so different from Albany, so different from the world that Ours was being built for.

"Five million dollars," Marcus said.

"Five million dollars."

"Do you know what that means?"

"It means we can fight the patent trolls. It means we can hire engineers. It means we can scale the infrastructure. It means we can survive."

"It means more than that." Marcus was smiling. Not the wolf smile. A different one. The rare one. The one that meant something good was happening and he was allowing himself to feel it. "It means we're funded. We're a real company with real money from a real investor who believes in the mission. When the press picks this up — and they will — the narrative changes. We're not three guys in an apartment anymore. We're a funded startup with a billionaire backer who has telecom experience and global connections. The story just got bigger."

"The war just got bigger too."

"Yes. It did. But now we have ammunition."

I thought about JP's conditions. The sixty-five percent, permanent. The no-sale clause. The advisory role.

"Marcus. He gave us five million dollars and only asked for eight percent. Catherine Chen offered two million for thirty-five. First Light wouldn't invest at all. What's different about JP?"

Marcus drove in silence for a mile. Then: "JP isn't investing in the company. He's investing in the correction. He watched his infrastructure get co-opted to exploit the people it was built to serve. He's been carrying that for twenty years. You gave him a way to fight back. The five million isn't an investment. It's ammunition in a war he's been waiting to join."

I understood this better than Marcus might have expected. Every career teaches you something about how money relates to trust, and every career teaches you a different lesson.

In carpentry, the relationship was simple. A client gave you money. You built them a house. If the house was good, they trusted you. If the house was bad, they didn't. The money flowed one direction — from them to you — and the trust flowed with it. Straightforward. Transactional. Honest.

In tech, money and trust were separated by layers of abstraction. Your salary came from a company that made money from clients you'd never meet, building products for users you'd never talk to. The trust wasn't personal — it was institutional. You trusted the company to pay you, the company trusted you to perform, and nobody looked anybody in the eye while the exchange happened. When I vested my options, nobody shook my hand. An email appeared. Numbers changed in a brokerage account. I cashed out and walked away and the whole thing felt like a business arrangement between two accounting systems rather than between two people.

In real estate, money and trust were in constant tension. Every deal involved two parties who each wanted the best price, which meant one of them was going to be disappointed, and both of them were trusting their agents to tell them when the price was right. The best deals happened when both sides believed the other was acting in good faith. The worst deals — the ones that fell apart at the closing table, that generated lawsuits, that left scars — happened when someone's trust was betrayed. Usually over money. Always over money.

JP's five million was something I'd never encountered in any career. It was money given not in exchange for something, but in alignment with something. He wasn't buying a product or a service or a percentage that he'd flip at the next round. He was buying into a belief — the same belief that was on my napkin, the same belief that had cost me fifty million dollars when I turned down Zeta. He was saying, with five million dollars and eight percent and a charter clause: I believe what you believe, and I'm willing to bet on it.

That's not an investment. That's an alliance.

I looked out the window. The highway stretched ahead of us, pointing north toward Albany, toward the apartment, toward the whiteboards and the napkin and the kitchen table where everything happened.

Five million dollars. Enough to build. Enough to fight. Enough to turn a napkin into a platform and a platform into a movement.

I called Sofia.

"How'd it go?"

"Five million. Eight percent. And he made us write the sixty-five percent into the charter."

Silence. Then, very quietly, the sound of Sofia Reyes crying. Not sad crying. The other kind. The kind that happens when someone you love has been fighting with nothing and suddenly has something, and the relief is so vast it needs an exit.

"I'm making pierogi tonight," I said.

"I'll bring horchata."

"It's not Tuesday."

"I know. Some things don't wait for Tuesday."

She was right. Some things don't.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 22

The Hit Piece

The article appeared in Business Insider on a Monday morning with the precision of a weapon that had been aimed for weeks. The headline was a question, which is the journalistic technique of saying something devastating without being accountable for it:

"Is OURS a Scam? Inside the Social Media Startup That Promises to Pay Users"

A question isn't an accusation. It's worse. An accusation can be denied. A question lives in the space between truth and suspicion, and that space is where reputations go to die.

I read it at 6:47 AM, standing in my kitchen in my underwear, holding a cup of coffee that I forgot to drink for the next twenty minutes. Biscuit was at my feet, waiting for breakfast. The article was 3,200 words long. I read every one of them, and each one felt like a nail being driven into something I'd built with my hands.

The byline was Brian Jeffries, a Business Insider tech reporter who specialized in what the industry called "accountability journalism" and what I would call "finding the worst possible interpretation of everything and presenting it as concern." His previous work included a series on crypto scams that had been genuinely useful, which gave him credibility, which made the hit piece on Ours that much more effective.

Here's what the article said, and here's what it meant.

What it said: "Ours promises users 65% of advertising revenue, but independent analysis suggests the platform's actual payouts may represent a fraction of total ad revenue flowing through the system."

What it meant: They couldn't prove the 65% was a lie, so they implied it might be, using the word "may," which is the journalist's equivalent of a weasel sneaking through a hole it didn't dig.

What it said: "The platform's founder, Roger Grubb, 62, has no background in technology, finance, or business management. Prior to founding Ours, Grubb worked as a residential carpenter in Albany, California."

What it meant: How could a carpenter possibly build something legitimate? The framing was deliberate, not "Roger Grubb, a serial entrepreneur" or "Roger Grubb, who spent a decade in construction technology before fifteen years in real estate." Just "a carpenter." Stripped of three careers' worth of context. Reduced to a single job title from thirty years ago. As if the trade I'd started in was evidence of incompetence rather than a foundation for everything that came after.

What it said: "Financial experts have raised concerns about the sustainability of Ours's business model, noting that paying users 65% of revenue leaves insufficient margin for platform operations, development, and growth."

What it meant: "Financial experts" were unnamed. The "concerns" were generic. Any business model can be questioned by anonymous experts if you don't name them or quote them specifically. And the actual math, the math Marcus had stress-tested under twelve different scenarios, the math that showed 20% operational margin was sustainable at scale — was absent from the article entirely.

What it said: "Several former beta users, speaking on condition of anonymity, described the platform as 'promising but unproven' and expressed concerns about the security of their financial information."

What it meant: Anonymous sources. The journalistic silver bullet. You can say anything if you don't have to attribute it. "Several" could be two. "Former beta users" could be people who downloaded the app once and deleted it. "Concerns about financial information" could be manufactured by the writer or fed to sources by interested parties.

And then the paragraph that made my blood go cold:

"Questions have also been raised about the platform's internal security practices. Sources familiar with the company suggest that Ours lacks basic data protection protocols, and that sensitive user financial data may be vulnerable to unauthorized access."

Sensitive user financial data. Vulnerable to unauthorized access. Sources familiar with the company.

Someone had told Brian Jeffries about Jake's data exports. Not directly — they'd weaponized the fact of the leak without revealing themselves as the cause. Zeta's intelligence operation had extracted data from us through Jake, and now they were using the fact of that extraction as evidence that our security was weak. They'd broken into our house and then called the newspaper to report that our locks were bad.

The elegance of it was nauseating.

The reaction was immediate and devastating. Not because people believed the article, most of our users didn't,but because the article gave ammunition to everyone who was already skeptical. The comment sections filled with the predictable chorus: "I knew it was too good to be true." "How can they afford 65%?" "A carpenter building a tech platform? Right." "This has Ponzi scheme written all over it."

I knew what this was. I'd lived through a version of it in real estate.

When you run a staging company, you compete with other staging companies. And most of that competition is healthy — better designs, better prices, better service. But once, just once, a competitor decided to compete dirty. I'd won a contract to stage a twenty-unit condo development in Walnut Creek — the biggest job I'd ever landed, worth about $80,000. Two weeks after I signed the contract, the developer got an anonymous email claiming my company used furniture "sourced from liquidation sales of pest-infested warehouses." The email included photos of a warehouse that wasn't mine and invoices that weren't mine and just enough plausible detail that the developer called me, confused and concerned, asking for an explanation.

The explanation was simple: none of it was true. The photos were from a storage facility in Stockton that I'd never been to. The invoices were fabricated. The email came from a burner account that traced, eventually, to a staging company in Pleasanton run by a woman named Cynthia who'd bid on the same contract and lost.

I kept the job. Cynthia's company went under within a year, not because of me, but because people who spend their energy attacking competitors don't have enough left over to serve their clients. But the three weeks between the email and the resolution, the three weeks of phone calls and explanations and the sick feeling of having your reputation questioned by someone with no evidence and no consequences — those three weeks taught me something that Brian Jeffries's article was teaching me again.

It's not the accusation that does the damage. It's the question. The accusation can be answered. The question, "Is Ours a scam?",hangs in the air like smoke. You can't grab smoke. You can't nail it to a wall. You can only wait for it to clear and hope the room still looks the same when it does.

Our sign-ups didn't stop, but they slowed. The growth curve that had been steep and climbing bent, the way a beam bends under unexpected weight, not breaking, but flexing in a direction you don't want.

Marcus was clinical. "Our growth rate dropped from 23% week-over-week to 11%. That's significant but not fatal. The existing user base is stable, retention is holding at 72%. The people who use Ours know it works. The people who haven't tried it are being told it doesn't."

"So how do we fight a narrative?"

"With a better narrative."

Priya designed the response in an hour. Nobody reads press releases. A page on our website called "The Truth About Ours." Clean, simple, beautiful. Three sections.

Section One: The Math. Our complete financial model, simplified for a general audience. Revenue in. Revenue out. 65% to users. 20% to operations. 10% to creators. 5% to reserve. Every number sourced. Every calculation shown. Transparent down to the penny.

Section Two: The Security. A full description of our data protection practices, including the encryption protocols Jake had implemented, the payment processing partners we used, and the specific certifications and compliance standards we met. This was the section that mattered most, because the accusation of weak security was the most dangerous — in a platform that handles money, trust is the foundation.

Section Three: The Carpenter. My story. A letter. Written by me, in my voice, addressing the implication that a carpenter couldn't build a technology platform.

I sat down to write it in the office above Tony's. Same table. Same chair. Same sticky "e" key. And I wrote:

To anyone who read the Business Insider article and wondered whether a carpenter can build a technology platform:

Yes. But I'm not just a carpenter. I'm also the other things they didn't mention.

I framed houses from twenty to thirty, two hundred of them,until my back said enough. Then I spent a decade in construction technology, building the software systems that coordinate how buildings get built. I vested my stock options and walked away. Then fifteen years in real estate — as a realtor, where I learned more about human nature across a closing table than most people learn in a lifetime. I watched money make good people desperate and desperate people cruel. I watched first-time buyers weep with joy and longtime owners weep with loss. I built that into a staging company, because I'd learned what makes people feel something when they walk through a door. And I built that into an investment company, because by then I understood the whole machine: the properties, the people, the capital, the fear, the hope.

The article called me "a carpenter." I am a carpenter. I'm also a technologist, a realtor, a business builder, and a man who's spent forty years constructing things and studying the people who live inside them.

Carpentry taught me how things fit together: that every joint is a decision and every structure is a system. Tech taught me how systems scale. Real estate taught me people — what drives them, what breaks them, how money brings out the best and the worst. All of those educations are inside Ours.

I learned to code at fifty-five. I built Ours from my kitchen table. I have a team of five people, a pit bull named Biscuit, and a napkin on my wall that says 65% — the percentage of advertising revenue that goes to you, the user. Not "up to." Not "in qualifying categories." Not "subject to algorithmic assessment." Sixty-five percent. Every dollar. Every user. Every time.

The article suggests I don't have the background for this. Here's what I have: forty years of building things that hold up. Twenty-one years of sobriety that taught me honesty isn't optional — it's structural. A granddaughter who spends two hours a day creating value for platforms that pay her nothing. And a belief — stubborn, irrational, deeply American: that if something is wrong, you fix it. You don't wait for permission. You don't wait for credentials. You pick up your tools and you build.

That's what I'm doing. That's what my team is doing. That's what our 30,000 users are doing — building something together that the biggest companies in the world don't want to exist.

If you want to know whether Ours is real, download it. Watch your counter tick. See your money grow. Then decide for yourself whether a carpenter knows how to build.

The wood doesn't care about my résumé. Neither should you.

— Roger Grubb* *Founder, Ours* *Albany, California

We published the letter at 2 PM on Wednesday. Priya put it on the site with a design so clean and warm that reading it felt like being invited into someone's home. The font was the serif she'd chosen — the one that carried weight. The background was the amber warmth of the Ours brand. My headshot — taken by Jake on his phone in the apartment, me leaning against the whiteboard wall, arms crossed, looking like exactly what I am — was at the bottom.

The letter went viral in a way the Business Insider article hadn't. Not because people love defending underdogs, though they do,but because the letter was honest in a way that corporate communications never are. It didn't use marketing language. It didn't have talking points. It was a carpenter talking about building, and people recognized the voice of someone who meant what he said because he'd lived what he said.

The responses came in waves. Twitter first, then Reddit, then Instagram, then the press. The narrative shifted, not completely, not permanently, but enough. Enough that the question "Is Ours a scam?" was answered, in the public mind, by a louder question: "Why is a trillion-dollar company so afraid of a carpenter?"

The growth curve bent back. Sign-ups climbed. Twenty-five thousand users became thirty. Thirty became thirty-five. The letter brought people in not just as users but as believers, people who didn't just want to earn money from their attention but who wanted to be part of something that was being fought for. Who saw the Business Insider article and the patent trolls and the Zeta Rewards copycat and said: these people are under attack, and I want to stand with them.

Marcus called me that night. His voice had the quality it gets when he's been running numbers and the numbers have told him something he needs to say aloud to believe.

"Roger. The letter generated fourteen thousand sign-ups in twenty-four hours. That's more than any single day since launch. More than the TechCrunch article. More than the comparison chart."

"Why?"

"Because people don't follow products. They follow stories. The TechCrunch article told them about a product. The comparison chart told them about economics. The letter told them about a person. And people follow people."

"I'm not a person worth following."

"You're a carpenter who turned down fifty million dollars and is being attacked by the largest social media company on earth and wrote a letter that said 'the wood doesn't care about my résumé.' You're the most followable person on the internet right now."

I sat at the kitchen table. The napkin on the wall. The cease-and-desist. Mia's words. The patent letters. And now, pulled up on my laptop, the letter that had turned a hit piece into a rally cry.

The wall wasn't just getting full. It was becoming a story. Every piece of paper was a chapter. Every artifact was evidence. Of what? Of something I was only beginning to understand: that the platform wasn't the revolution. The story was. The platform was the tool. The story was the weapon. And the story — the real, messy, human story of a carpenter from Albany fighting for something that mattered — was the thing that no amount of money or lawyers or hit pieces could buy or kill or fake.

They could attack the platform. They could question the math. They could hire journalists and file patents and launch copycat products.

But they couldn't out-story a carpenter with a napkin and a dog.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 23

One Hundred Thousand

There's a number in construction called the critical load. It's the amount of weight a structure can support before the physics change — before the materials stop behaving like solids and start behaving like something else. Below critical load, a beam holds steady. At critical load, it flexes. Above critical load, it fails.

Platforms have critical loads too. Not weight — users. Below a certain number, you're a toy. A proof of concept. A science project held together with duct tape and conviction. Above that number, you're infrastructure. You're a system that people depend on. You're a thing that, if it breaks, leaves a hole.

The number, for a social media platform, is somewhere around a hundred thousand. Below a hundred thousand, nobody cares. Above a hundred thousand, everybody cares. A hundred thousand is the line between "interesting experiment" and "competitive threat." Between "cute startup" and "something we need to deal with."

We crossed that line on a Saturday in late spring, and everything changed.

Jake saw it first. He'd built an internal dashboard that tracked sign-ups in real time — a counter for the counter, a meta-measurement that was pure Jake in its recursive elegance. The dashboard was projected onto one of the whiteboards in my living room, which meant that at any given moment, any of us could glance up from whatever we were doing and see the number.

The number had been climbing. After the letter, after the comparison chart, after the Business Insider article backfired, after the organic press and the Reddit threads and the TikTok videos and the slow, steady burn of word-of-mouth recommendations from real users to real friends, the number climbed. Fifty thousand. Sixty. Seventy. Each thousand a milestone that felt enormous and tiny at the same time — enormous because every number was a person, tiny because the platforms we were fighting measured their users in billions.

On that Saturday morning, I was making breakfast. Scrambled eggs for me, scrambled eggs for Jake (who'd slept on the cot again), and a separate plate of scrambled eggs for Biscuit, who was not supposed to eat human food but who had developed a negotiation strategy so sophisticated — staring, whimpering, placing his chin on my foot with precisely calibrated pathos — that I'd surrendered months ago.

"Roger."

Jake's voice had a quality I hadn't heard before. Not excited — awed. The voice of someone watching something happen that they'd imagined but hadn't fully believed in.

I turned from the stove. Jake was pointing at the dashboard.

99,847. 99,851. 99,856.

The numbers were ticking up in real time. Not one by one — in clusters. Threes and fives and eights, appearing like heartbeats on a monitor. Each tick was a person. Someone in Memphis downloading the app. Someone in Portland creating an account. Someone in Miami opening the Market for the first time and watching the counter tick.

99,912. 99,937. 99,968.

I turned off the stove. The eggs could wait. Some moments are more important than eggs.

Jake stood. I stood. We watched the dashboard the way you watch a countdown — except it was counting up. Up toward the number that meant we were real. That meant we couldn't be ignored. That meant the war we'd been fighting with five people and a dog was now a war that a hundred thousand people had enlisted in.

99,989. 99,994. 99,997.

"Call Marcus," I said.

Jake was already dialing. He put it on speaker. Marcus picked up on the first ring, which meant he was watching too.

"I see it," Marcus said. His voice was the quiet one. The reverent one. The voice he used at AA meetings when someone shared something that mattered.

100,000.

The number appeared on the dashboard. Six digits. One hundred thousand. A hundred thousand people who had chosen — actively, voluntarily, with full knowledge of what they were joining — to be part of something that didn't exist eight months ago. That started on a napkin. That was built on a kitchen table. That was attacked by the most powerful companies on earth and survived.

Jake's phone buzzed. Priya: Is this real?

Jake texted back: It's real.

My phone buzzed. Sofia: I'm watching the counter. Congratulations, mi amor.

Mi amor. She'd started calling me that three weeks ago. My love. In Spanish, because some things are said better in the language your mother taught you, and Sofia's mother had taught her that love is a thing you name out loud.

Marcus, on speaker: "Roger. I need to tell you something."

"What?"

"At our current growth rate, with our current retention, with the CPMs we're generating from willing attention... the financial model projects first-month profitability at 250,000 users."

"Profitability."

"Positive unit economics. Revenue per user exceeding cost per user. The platform sustaining itself. No more burn. No more living on JP's money. The thing feeding itself."

"When do we hit 250?"

"At current trajectory — six weeks."

Six weeks from profitability. Six weeks from the thing that every startup chases and most never reach. The moment when the engine runs on its own fuel.

I sat down at the whiteboard station. The whiteboard glowed with the dashboard projection. Jake was standing in the living room with his phone pressed to his ear, talking to someone — Priya, probably, or one of the new engineers we'd hired with JP's funding. Marcus was still on speaker, running through numbers, projecting, modeling, stress-testing.

And I was sitting at the table where it started, looking at a napkin on the wall, feeling something I didn't have a name for.

It wasn't pride. Pride is what you feel when you build a good bookshelf. This was bigger. It wasn't joy. Joy is what you feel when your granddaughter laughs. This was heavier. It wasn't relief. Relief is what you feel when the test comes back negative. This was more complicated.

What I felt was weight. The weight of a hundred thousand people who had trusted me. Trusted a carpenter who'd learned to code, who'd written a number on a napkin, who'd turned down fifty million dollars, who'd been attacked and smeared and threatened and who had, against every probability, built something that worked.

I'd felt versions of this weight before. Every career comes with its own gravity of responsibility.

In carpentry, it was literal. Load-bearing walls carry the house. Get the header wrong, undersize the beam, misjudge the span, and the structure fails. People live inside the things you build. Their safety is in your joinery. I'd lain awake a few nights over the years, second-guessing a calculation, until I'd drive back to the site in the morning and check it with a level and a tape measure and confirm that yes, the math was right, the beam was sufficient, the house would hold.

In tech, the weight was systemic. The software I'd built coordinated construction projects — schedules, budgets, material deliveries, subcontractor coordination. When the system worked, buildings went up on time and on budget. When it didn't — when a bug delayed a material order or a scheduling error left a crew standing idle — the cost cascaded. I'd seen a single software glitch add $40,000 to a project because a concrete pour was scheduled for the wrong week and the crew showed up to an unprepared site.

In real estate, the weight was personal. People made the biggest financial decision of their lives across a table from me, and they trusted me to tell them the truth. The house with the beautiful kitchen and the cracked foundation — do I tell them about the foundation, knowing it might kill the deal and my commission? The answer was always yes, and the answer always cost me something, and the cost was always worth it. Because the alternative — letting someone walk into a house that would slowly fail them, taking my commission and looking the other way — was a weight I couldn't carry. I'd seen agents do it. I'd watched them cash checks on transactions they knew were wrong, and I'd watched the knowledge settle into their faces over the years like water damage: invisible at first, then unmistakable, then structural.

A hundred thousand people was a different magnitude. But the principle was the same. They trusted me with something valuable — their attention, their data, their small daily accumulations of money. And the question was the same question it had always been: will the thing I built hold? Will it do what I said it would do? Will the structure I designed protect the people inside it?

I looked at the napkin. 65/20/10/5. The load calculation. The foundation. The promise.

A hundred thousand people were counting on me. Literally — their counters were ticking, their money was accumulating, their attention was being valued for the first time. If I failed, a hundred thousand counters would go dark. A hundred thousand small, steady streams of income would dry up. A hundred thousand people who had believed the carpenter would be betrayed by the carpenter.

That weight. That specific, beautiful, terrifying weight.

The press covered it. Of course they did — a hundred thousand was the magic number, the number that turned "niche platform" into "phenomenon." The headlines were different now. Not "Is Ours a Scam?" but "Ours Hits 100K Users: The Social Platform That Pays Is Growing Fast." Not "Can a Carpenter Build a Tech Company?" but "The Carpenter Who Took on Big Tech — And Is Winning."

The Verge: "Ours's 100K milestone validates the Willing Attention Thesis."

Wired: "Why 100,000 people chose to get paid for their attention."

NPR did the segment they'd been developing. Eight minutes on All Things Considered. They played a clip of Mia — Kevin had given permission, and Mia had agreed with the enthusiasm of a twelve-year-old who'd been told she'd be on the radio — saying the line that had started everything: "On TikTok I never made anything. This pays me."

60 Minutes called. Not a producer this time. The host. They wanted a segment. They wanted to come to Albany, to the apartment, to see the napkin and the whiteboards and the kitchen table. They wanted to tell the story.

Marcus wanted to say yes immediately. Jake wanted to rebuild the apartment first — "at least move the mixing bowl," he said, which was the first time he'd acknowledged that leaving his cereal bowl in the sink was not, in fact, a design choice.

I called Sofia.

"60 Minutes wants to film in the apartment."

"Good."

"Good? The apartment is a disaster. There are whiteboards everywhere. Biscuit's bed is in the middle of the living room. The bathroom shower still drips."

"Roger. That's the point. They don't want to see a Silicon Valley office. They want to see a kitchen table. They want to see a napkin on a wall. They want to see a man who built something real in a place that looks real. If you clean up the apartment, you clean up the story."

"The mixing bowl?"

"Leave the mixing bowl."

The 60 Minutes crew arrived on a Wednesday. Three cameras, a sound guy, a producer, and the correspondent — a woman named Diana Chambers who'd been covering technology for fifteen years and who walked into my apartment with the practiced eye of someone who'd seen a thousand startups and was calibrating where this one fell on the spectrum between genuine and performative.

She looked at the napkin first. Then the cease-and-desist. Then Mia's words on the electric bill. Then the patent letters. Then the comparison chart, still pinned to the wall. Then the dashboard, projecting the real-time user count: 112,847.

"May I?" she said, pointing at the whiteboard station.

"Of course."

She sat in my chair. The chair where I'd written the business model. Where I'd turned down fifty million dollars. Where I'd confronted Jake. Where I'd built the interface, screen by screen, the way I'd built houses, board by board.

"This is where it happened," she said. Not a question.

"This is where it happens. Present tense. It's still happening."

The interview lasted two hours. Diana asked everything — the napkin, the math, the beta, the cease-and-desist, Zeta Rewards, the hit piece, the patent trolls, JP Beaumont, the growth. She asked about my background. About carpentry. About sobriety. About learning to code at fifty-five. About Mia.

And then she asked the question that stopped me.

"Mr. Grubb. You've turned down fifty million dollars. You've been sued by patent trolls funded by Zeta. You've been the subject of a hit piece in Business Insider. You've had internal data stolen by a member of your own team. What makes you keep going?"

The cameras were on me. The apartment was quiet. Biscuit was asleep under the table, his chin on my foot, dreaming of whatever dogs dream of when the world is loud above them.

"My granddaughter," I said. "Mia. She's twelve. She spends two hours a day on her phone, making videos, scrolling, engaging, creating. She's producing value — real, measurable, economic value — and getting nothing back. Nothing. Not a penny. Not a thank-you. Not even the acknowledgment that what she's doing IS work."

I looked at the napkin on the wall.

"I keep going because Mia is twelve. And in six years she'll be eighteen. And I want to live in a world where, by the time she's an adult, the idea that companies can extract billions of dollars from people's attention without paying them is as absurd as the idea that companies can dump chemicals in rivers without cleaning them up. I want it to be obvious. I want it to be law. I want her to grow up in a world where her time has value and everyone knows it."

Diana was quiet. The cameras were still rolling. The producer was still. The sound guy was still. Even Biscuit was still.

"And if you fail?" Diana asked.

"Then Mia will grow up in the same world she's growing up in now. And she'll fight the fight herself. And she'll be better at it than I am, because she's twelve and she's brilliant and she's angry in the way that only a young person who's been underestimated can be angry." I paused. "But I'd rather she didn't have to."

The interview ended. The crew packed up. Diana shook my hand at the door and said something she probably wasn't supposed to say: "This is going to be a big segment."

"How big?"

"I've been doing this for fifteen years. I know when a story has legs. This one has legs."

She left. The apartment was quiet. Just me and Biscuit and the whiteboards and the napkin and the number on the dashboard: 113,204.

I sat at the table. Opened my laptop. The "e" key stuck.

A hundred thousand and climbing. The war wasn't over. It was barely beginning. But for the first time since the napkin, I felt something shift — not in the numbers or the press or the public sentiment, but in the ground under my feet. The foundation was holding. The structure was sound. The walls were going up.

This thing was going to stand.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 24

The Engineers

JP's money changed the apartment. I wasn't moving, not yet, not when the napkin was on the wall and the table was in the kitchen and the whole mythology of Ours was anchored to this specific 740 square feet of Albany real estate. But the money changed what happened inside the apartment, because the money meant we could hire people, and hiring people meant the folding table wasn't enough anymore.

We rented the apartment next door.

Mrs. Kowalski, my neighbor of eleven years, had moved to a retirement community in Walnut Creek three weeks earlier. She'd been talking about it for months — her daughter lived out there, the place had a garden, her knees couldn't handle the stairs anymore. I helped her carry boxes the day she left and she handed me a plate of pierogi wrapped in foil and said, "Take care of this building, Roger. It deserves better than it gets." I ate the pierogi that night and thought about what she'd said, because Mrs. Kowalski had a way of being right about things that sounded like they were about buildings but were actually about people.

Her apartment was the mirror image of mine — same layout, same 740 square feet, same dripping shower that played a note somewhere between B-flat and despair. The landlord was happy to rent it. I signed the lease and Jake knocked a doorway through the shared wall in forty-five minutes, which was technically illegal and definitely a lease violation and absolutely the most satisfying thing I'd watched happen to drywall since I'd stopped doing it professionally. He found a stud exactly where I told him it would be, because studs are studs whether the building is in Albany or Albuquerque, and moved the cut six inches east with the instinct of someone who'd been listening when I talked about load paths.

We now had 1,480 square feet. The Ours campus. My apartment was the "executive suite" — meaning it still had my bed, my kitchen, and Biscuit. Mrs. Kowalski's apartment was the "engineering center" — meaning Jake filled it with IKEA Bekant desks and Dell P2722H monitors from Costco, three for $680, and cables that ran across the floor like veins in a body, carrying signal the way veins carry blood. He hung a whiteboard on the wall opposite my napkin and wrote at the top: WHAT WE'RE BUILDING. Underneath he taped a printout of the user counter: 87,312.

We hired four engineers in the first two weeks. Jake screened them. Marcus vetted their backgrounds. I asked them one question: "Why do you want to work here instead of anywhere else?"

The answers told me everything.

Elena Voss, twenty-eight, backend engineer, formerly at Spotify. She had dark circles under her eyes that weren't from exhaustion but from a particular kind of moral fatigue that I recognized from the faces of people in early sobriety — the fatigue of knowing something is wrong and not being able to stop participating in it. "Because I helped build a system that pays artists fractions of a cent per stream while the platform takes billions. I wrote the code that rounds down. I want to write code that rounds up."

Darnell Washington, thirty-one, infrastructure engineer, formerly at AWS. Built like someone who'd played defensive line in college, which he had — three years at Howard before a torn ACL sent him to the library, where he discovered he liked making systems work more than he liked hitting people. "Because I spent five years helping companies scale systems that exploit people. I want to scale a system that pays them. Also, real talk — my grandmother uses Instagram six hours a day. She thinks the ads are part of the content. She's not stupid, she's seventy-three. Your platform treats people like her with dignity. That matters to me."

Aisha Patel, twenty-four, fresh out of Georgia Tech, first job. Small, quiet, with the nervous energy of someone who'd been the smartest person in every room since middle school and hadn't figured out yet that being smart wasn't a liability. "Because my mom is a home health aide and she uses Facebook four hours a day and nobody's ever paid her for it and she doesn't even know they should. I want to build the thing that tells her." She paused. "And because everyone at my CS program said this was a joke and you were going to fail and I've been underestimated my whole life and I know what that looks like and it doesn't look like you."

Tomás Herrera, thirty-three, security engineer, formerly at Stripe. Precise in the way that security people are — every word chosen, every handshake measured, every room entered with an instinctive scan for exits. I liked him immediately because I recognized the behavior: he was playing the same identification game I play, just with threats instead of objects. He'd cataloged my apartment in three seconds — noted the exits, the window locks, the router sitting exposed on the kitchen counter with its default admin password probably still set. "Because after reading your letter, I drove up from Palo Alto and sat in the parking lot of your building for twenty minutes before I had the courage to email you. I've never felt that way about a job posting. I've never felt anything about a job posting. The fact that I felt something told me something."

Four engineers. Different backgrounds, different specialties, same answer: I'm here because what you're building matters, and what I was building before didn't.

They joined our two-apartment headquarters with the energy of people who believe in what they're doing, which burns different from the energy of people who are just being paid. You can feel the difference. Everyone can.

Jake became a different person with a team. I'd known him as a solitary coder — brilliant, compulsive, working alone in the glow of his monitors like a monk in a cell. With a team, he was something else: a leader. He assigned tasks with precision. He reviewed code with rigor. He held daily standups — fifteen-minute meetings at 9 AM where each engineer reported what they'd done, what they'd do, and what was blocking them — with the authority of someone twice his age.

I recognized the transformation because I'd been through it myself. The first time I ran a job site, I was twenty-four. I'd been a laborer, then an apprentice, then a journeyman — always part of someone else's crew. When I got my own crew, something shifted. The work was the same. The responsibility was different. You stop thinking about the board in your hand and start thinking about the house on the horizon. You stop measuring your own cuts and start watching whether everyone else's cuts are true.

Jake watched his team the way I'd watched mine. Not with suspicion — with care. He noticed when Elena's code reviews got sloppy because she was tired and he'd say, "Go home, we're not Spotify, nobody here gets a medal for burning out." He noticed when Darnell was solving problems that were technically elegant but unnecessarily complex, and he'd sketch a simpler approach on the whiteboard and say, "This does the same thing in half the lines, what am I missing?",framing the correction as a question so Darnell could save face and learn at the same time. He noticed when Aisha, the youngest, the newest,was afraid to ask questions, and he made a point of asking HER questions, drawing out her thinking, showing her that her instincts were good.

He was going to be a great leader. He was already a great leader. He just didn't know it yet, the way I hadn't known it at twenty-four, standing on a job site in the rain, watching a house take shape, realizing for the first time that the building wasn't the accomplishment — the team was.

But there was something else in Jake now, too. Something that hadn't been there before the confrontation about the data leaks. A carefulness. A way of holding himself that was slightly apart from the group, as if he'd drawn an invisible boundary between himself and the people he was leading. The boundary of someone who had betrayed trust and was terrified of being trusted again.

I saw it. Marcus saw it. We didn't discuss it. Some wounds heal better when you don't poke them.

But something happened three weeks in that I've never told Jake I saw.

The coffee shop next to Tony's — Peet's on Solano, the one with the outdoor tables and the baristas who memorized your order — was where the engineers went for afternoon breaks. I'd learned to give them that space. The worst thing a founder can do is hover. Teams need room to complain, to vent, to say the things you can only say when the boss isn't listening. I remembered that from the construction days — the ten-minute breaks where we'd smoke (back when I still smoked) and talk about the foreman's latest idiocy, and how that release valve kept us from quitting.

I was walking back from Ace Hardware with a bag of wood screws when I heard Jake's voice. Not loud, Jake was never loud,but sharp in a way I hadn't heard since the confrontation. I stopped at the corner, behind a mailbox, and I did something I'm not proud of.

I listened.

"—don't understand what you're offering."

Another voice. Male. Unfamiliar. Professional. The voice of someone who wore a suit to coffee shops. "I'm offering you a solution, Mr. Chen. You have $180,000 in student debt. We're prepared to retire that debt in full. Today. In exchange for ongoing consultation."

"Consultation on what?"

"On technical architecture. Scaling challenges. Security infrastructure. General industry insights."

Jake was quiet. Long enough that I thought he'd stopped talking. Then:

"You're asking me to spy on Roger."

"We're asking you to consult. Discreetly. The insights would be generalized, anonymized—"

"Bullshit." Jake's voice wasn't sharp anymore. It was flat. The flatness of someone who's made a decision and is just waiting for the conversation to catch up. "You're Zeta. Or someone working for Zeta. You found out about my debt and you're trying to buy me. Again."

"Mr. Chen—"

"I already failed this test once. I talked to a recruiter six months ago because I was scared and broke and I thought maybe I could just take a meeting and nothing would happen. And something happened. Roger found out. And he didn't fire me, which is what I deserved. He looked at me like I was his son who'd done something stupid, and he said 'don't do it again,' and then he went back to work. Do you understand what that felt like? To betray someone and have them respond by trusting you more?"

"I don't see how that's relevant—"

"It's the only thing that's relevant. You're offering me money to sell out someone who forgave me when he shouldn't have. And you think I'm going to take it because my debt is high and my judgment is bad. But here's what you don't understand about debt: it's just money. Money comes and goes. What Roger gave me — the chance to be part of something that actually matters, the chance to prove I'm not the person I was six months ago — that's not something you can put a price on. Because you can't buy it back once you've sold it."

Silence. The professional voice said something I couldn't hear. Then footsteps, moving away.

I stayed behind the mailbox. Waited until Jake went back inside Tony's. Waited another ten minutes, staring at a bag of wood screws, thinking about what I'd witnessed.

Jake didn't know I'd heard. I never told him.

Some things you don't say. You just adjust, the way a carpenter adjusts after the foundation settles. You re-check the level. You plane the door until it fits. You don't make a speech about it.

From that day on, something shifted between Jake and me. I stopped watching for signs of betrayal. I stopped second-guessing his long phone calls. I stopped checking his code commits for anything that looked like data being moved where it shouldn't go.

Not because he'd earned my trust with words. Because I'd watched him pass the test when he thought no one was watching. And that's the only test that matters.

The team built at a speed that made my head spin. In the first month of funded operations, they accomplished what Jake and I had spent four months doing alone. The backend was rebuilt, not patched, not improved, REBUILT, on an architecture that could handle a million concurrent users. Elena designed it. Darnell stress-tested it by simulating traffic loads that made the old system look like a toy bridge being asked to carry a freight train. Aisha wrote the documentation, which nobody asked her to do and which was so thorough and clear that Marcus printed it out and said, "This is the first technical document I've read in thirty years that a human being could understand."

The payment system was upgraded from simulated to real, connected to Stripe's payment infrastructure through an integration that Tomás built with the care of someone who understood that this system would handle real people's real money. He tested it for two weeks before letting anyone else touch it. He ran security audits at 2 AM. He found a vulnerability in the API that would have allowed someone to inflate their counter by spoofing engagement events, and he patched it, and then he found two more, and patched those too, and then he wrote a memo that said, "The system is secure. I'm now going to try to break it." He spent another week trying. He couldn't. Then he relaxed, which for Tomás meant his shoulders dropped approximately one centimeter.

The Market was expanded. Programmatic ads were supplemented with direct advertiser relationships — fourteen brands in the first month, including three that had previously been spending exclusively on Zeta platforms. Jake and Marcus had done the outreach together, an unlikely pair — the twenty-six-year-old engineer and the fifty-eight-year-old quant — showing up at advertising agencies with the Willing Attention Thesis and the engagement data and the simple question: "Would you rather pay for attention that's stolen or attention that's given?"

The answer, from every advertiser who saw the data, was the same: given. Every time. Not because they were moral — advertisers are not in the morality business, but because willing attention converted better. Higher click-through rates. Higher purchase rates. Higher brand recall. The data was irrefutable. Willing attention wasn't just ethical. It was better.

The direct advertisers changed the economics. Programmatic ads paid $8-12 CPMs. Direct advertisers, seeing the engagement data, paid $18-25. Some paid more. One outdoor clothing brand — a company I won't name but whose products I'd worn on job sites for twenty years — paid $32 CPM for a week-long Market campaign and reported a conversion rate four times their Instagram average.

The money flowed. A stream. But a stream that was growing, and a stream that, for the first time, flowed in two directions: into the company AND into the users' accounts.

Average monthly earnings per user climbed. From $15 to $18 to $22. Not life-changing money for most people. Lunch money. Gas money. The phone bill. The co-pay. The things that sit between comfortable and precarious, that determine whether a person ends the month with a small sigh or a small knot in their stomach.

But for some users — for Delores, for Marcus's students, for the home health aides and the teachers and the nursing assistants and the people who worked sixty hours a week and still counted the days until payday — twenty-two dollars a month was a different feeling at the end of the month. A small sigh instead of a small knot. A cushion where there had been a cliff.

We hit two hundred thousand users on a Thursday. Then two-fifty on a Monday. Then, three days after the 60 Minutes segment aired — eleven minutes, prime time, to an audience of eight million viewers, Lesley Stahl sitting in our apartment asking me questions while Biscuit tried to climb into her lap — we hit five hundred thousand.

Half a million people.

I was at Sofia's house when Marcus called. We were in her kitchen — a kitchen that was, like everything about Sofia, organized and efficient and warm. The spices were alphabetized. The cutting boards were color-coded. A cat named Señor Bigotes, not hers, a neighborhood stray she'd been feeding for three years and who had decided, in the unilateral way cats decide things, that this was now his kitchen too, sat on the windowsill and observed us with the disdain of someone who'd seen better cooking.

Sofia was teaching me to make pupusas, which was her version of the pastele exchange — her recipe for mine, the culinary currency of our relationship.

"Five hundred thousand," Marcus said.

Sofia was kneading the masa. Her hands worked the dough the way they worked everything — precisely, with rhythm, with the unconscious mastery of someone who'd done the same motion ten thousand times.

"Five hundred thousand," I repeated.

She didn't stop kneading. She didn't need to. She knew what the number meant. She'd been there since Biscuit's stitches, since the pierogi in the exam room, since the first Tuesday. She'd watched the number climb from fifty to five hundred to five thousand to fifty thousand to five hundred thousand. She'd held my hand through the patent trolls and the hit piece and the betrayal and the moments at 3 AM when I wasn't sure any of it would work.

"Mi amor," she said, still kneading. "How does it feel?"

"Like weight."

"Good weight or bad weight?"

"Both. Like holding something that matters so much you're afraid to put it down and afraid to keep holding it."

She stopped kneading. Wiped her hands on a towel. Came to me and put those hands — her surgeon's hands, her masa-covered hands, the hands that had saved my dog and held my face and made pupusas in a kitchen where even the neighborhood cat felt safe — on my shoulders.

"You don't have to hold it alone," she said. "You have Marcus. You have Jake. You have Priya and Teddy and JP and Elena and Darnell and Aisha and Tomás. You have me. You have five hundred thousand people who chose to stand with you."

"Half a million."

"Half a million. And counting."

She went back to the masa. I went back to watching. Señor Bigotes yawned from the windowsill, unimpressed by half a million of anything. And something in the rhythm of Sofia's hands, steady, sure, relentless,told me what I needed to hear without her saying it.

Keep building. Keep kneading. The dough will hold.

↑ Back to Contents
CHAPTER 25

David's Mirror

I need to tell you what was happening on the other side of the wall. Not my wall — the wall between us and Zeta, between the apartment in Albany and Building 20 in Menlo Park, between the carpenter and the machine.

David Park was losing.

Not officially. Officially, Zeta Rewards was a success. The press release said so. The earnings call mentioned it. An analyst at Morgan Stanley wrote a note calling it "a decisive strategic response to the emerging attention-economics space," which is a sentence that uses eleven words to say nothing and gets paid handsomely for the effort. The internal metrics — the ones designed to tell the story the company wanted told — showed adoption. Fifty million Instagram users had "activated" Zeta Rewards. The word "activated" was doing heavy lifting in that sentence, because "activated" meant "saw the prompt and tapped 'Yes' without reading the terms," which is what most people do when Instagram asks them anything, because the alternative is reading a terms-of-service agreement that's longer than some novels and less engaging than all of them.

But behind the press release and the earnings call and the Morgan Stanley note, a different story was unfolding. The story was told in a number that David Park stared at every morning from the glass office his team called the fishbowl: average daily engagement with Zeta Rewards content.

1.3 seconds.

Users who had "activated" Zeta Rewards were spending an average of 1.3 seconds engaging with the revenue-generating content in their feeds. Not the 22 seconds that Ours's Market generated. Not even the 1.7 seconds that Instagram's regular ads generated. 1.3 seconds. Less than a regular ad. Because Zeta Rewards ads were labeled differently — they had a small "$" icon indicating they were payment-eligible — and users had learned, with the hard-won intelligence of people who've been manipulated by algorithms for a decade, that anything Instagram flagged as "special" was actually worse.
