I was going to hand the keys to anyone who wanted them and trust that the principle would survive even if the company didn't.

Sofia would say I was being brave. Marcus would say I was being strategic. Teddy would say I was being an idiot, or he'd say I was being a visionary, depending on which Teddy showed up.

But what was I actually being?

I thought about it for a long time. The East Bay lights below me. The sound of Biscuit sleeping inside. The smell of sawdust that never quite left my clothes, even now, even after all this time.

And I understood, finally, what I was doing.

I was letting go.

Not giving up. Not surrendering. Letting go of the thing I'd held so tightly that my knuckles had gone white. Letting go of the need to be the one who owned this, controlled this, defended this. Letting go of the fantasy that one carpenter in one apartment could hold off trillion-dollar companies forever.

The fantasy had been beautiful. And necessary — you can't build something from nothing without believing you can hold it against the world. But holding isn't the same as building. And I was a builder, not a holder.

If I open-sourced the architecture, Ours might fail. Some other platform might take our code and do it better. Some corporation might absorb our methods and claim the credit. The thing I'd built might disappear, absorbed into something I couldn't control.

But the principle would survive.

Sixty-five percent would become a standard, not a feature. "Pay users for their attention" would become an expectation, not an innovation. The idea — the napkin idea, the idea that started everything — would spread beyond what I could build alone.

And that was worth more than ownership. Worth more than control. Worth more than the ego satisfaction of being the one who held the keys.

I'd built something that worked. Now I had to trust that other people could build it too. That the code was good enough to replicate. That the principle was strong enough to survive my letting go.

It was terrifying. It was right.

I went inside. Sat at the kitchen table. Opened my laptop.

"You're at the table," she said. Not a question. A diagnosis.

"I'm at the table."

She sat across from me. The way she'd sat across from me a hundred times — at Rinconcito, at the clinic, at her parents' table. Always across. Always facing. Never beside, where you can avoid each other's eyes. Sofia believed in facing things. It was the core of her — the woman who'd been lied to by someone who sat beside her on the couch and whispered false things into her ear, and who'd decided, after, that love should happen face to face. Eye to eye. No hiding.

"The government," she said.

"The government."

"It's not going to be like Zeta. Zeta wanted to kill you. The government wants to use you. That's harder to fight, because it comes dressed as a compliment."

She was right. She was always right about the things that mattered, because she thought in terms of living systems, and a government approaching a platform it wanted to absorb was, biologically speaking, the same thing as a larger organism attempting to subsume a smaller one. Not predation. Symbiosis, but the kind of symbiosis where one partner gradually disappears inside the other.

"I know what I'm going to do," I said.

"Tell me."

"I'm going to open-source the architecture. Not the data — never the data. But the system. The payment infrastructure. The attention-verification protocols. The revenue-sharing framework. All of it. Published. Documented. Available to anyone who wants to build what we've built."

Sofia's eyes changed. The clinical assessment shifted into something else — the look she got when an animal she'd been treating turned a corner, when the biology tipped from declining to recovering.

"You're giving it away."

"I'm making it impossible to kill. If Ours lives in one company, one server, one carpenter's kitchen table — it can be regulated out of existence. It can be subpoenaed. It can be acquired. But if the architecture is everywhere. If ten platforms, fifty platforms, a thousand platforms are all running some version of the model — then the idea is bigger than any company. Bigger than any government. You can't subpoena a principle. You can't regulate a math equation. Sixty-five percent is just a number. But if a thousand platforms are paying that number, it's not a feature anymore. It's a standard. It's the way things work."

"The way things should always have worked."

"Yes."

She reached across the table and put her hand on mine. The surgeon's hand on the carpenter's hand. The healer and the builder. Two people who'd spent their lives working with living things — wood and animals, systems and bodies, and who understood, in the way that people who work with their hands understand, that the purpose of building is not to own what you've built. It's to make sure it stands.

Build together. That's what the ring said. And that's what I was going to do, build something that didn't belong to me, or to Ours, or to any company. Something that belonged to the word itself. Ours. Not mine. Not theirs. Ours.

I opened a new document. Blank page. Cursor blinking.

And I started to build.
